Slave as Agent to Buy Himself: Specification Determines Outcome
Explains rulings when a slave is told to buy himself as agent for another

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a slave is appointed as an agent to buy himself and distinguish between the two outcomes based on whether a buyer is specified.

Core Idea
If a slave is told to buy himself and names a buyer, the purchase is for that person; if silent, he is freed.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding a slave who is appointed as an agent by another person to buy himself from his master. The ruling hinges on whether the slave specifies a buyer when addressing the master. Understanding this scenario clarifies how Hanafi fiqh balances agency, sale, and emancipation, and highlights a key distinction between the structure of sale and emancipation contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Recognize the situation: a master says to his slave, 'Buy yourself from your master for me.' The slave then goes to the master and either specifies a buyer (e.g., 'Sell me to so-and-so') or does not specify ('Sell me').
The phrase 'for me' indicates the slave is acting as an agent, not for himself.
2
Analyze the Two Branches
If the slave says 'Sell me to so-and-so' and the master sells, the purchase belongs to that named person (the commander). If he says only 'Sell me,' the slave becomes free. Understand why each outcome occurs.
Specification determines the intent: agency vs. emancipation.
3
Understand the Rationale for Agency
A slave can be an agent for another in buying himself because he is a stranger (ajnabī) regarding his own property value. The sale addresses him as property, but since his property (himself) is in his hand, the seller cannot detain him after sale.
This is a key Hanafi principle: a slave has capacity to act as agent despite being property.
4
Distinguish Sale from Emancipation
In the sale scenario (specified buyer), the contract requires offer and acceptance: the master's 'I sold' must be met by the slave's acceptance. In emancipation (no specification), the master's utterance 'I sold' alone completes the freeing, as one person can handle both sides in emancipation (like marriage).
This difference arises because emancipation is considered a ibāhat (relinquishment) while sale is a contract.
5
Note Practical Consequences
When the purchase is for the commander, the slave (as agent) is liable to pay the price and can seek reimbursement from the commander. When he becomes free, he is liable as principal. The slave's interdiction (hajr) is lifted by this contract.
The contract removes the legal disability of slavery regarding this transaction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قال لعبد اشتر لي نفسك من مولاك فقال للمولى بعني نفسي لفلان ففعل فهو للآمر وإن لم يقل لفلان عتق) بيان لما إذا كان العبد وكيلاً بشراء نفسه بعد بيان ما إذا كان العبد موكلاً، وإنما كان هكذا لأن العبد يصلح وكيلاً عن غيره في شراء نفسه لأنه أجنبي عن ماليته والبيع يرد عليه من حيث إنه مال إلا أن ماليته في يده حتى لا يملك البائع الحبس بعد البيع، فإذا أضافه إلى الأمر صلح فعله امتثالاً . فيقع العقد للآمر، وإن عقد لنفسه فهو حر لأنه إعتاق وقد رضي به المولى دون المعاوضة، والعبد وإن كان وكيلاً بشراء معين ولكنه أتى بجنس تصرف آخر وفي مثله ينفذ على الوكيل . وأشار بقوله وإن لم يقل لفلان عتق إلى أنه لو قال بعني نفسك لنفسي فإنه يعتق بالأولى. وإنما عتق في المطلق لأنه يحتمل الوجهين فلا يقع امتثالاً بالشك قيبقى التصرف واقعاً لنفسه. ولما قدم المؤلف أول البيوع أن البيع لا ينعقد إلا بلفظين ماضيين علم أن قوله هنا في صورة وقوعه للآمر بعني ليس إيجاباً فإذا قال المولى بعت فلا بد من قبول العبد ليحصل الإيجاب والقبول بخلافه في صورة وقوعه عتقاً فإنه إيجاب ويتم بقول المولى بعت من غير قبول العبد بناء على أن الواحد يتولى طرفي العقد في العتق كالنكاح ولا يتولى الطرفين في البيع وفي الكتاب إشارة إلى أنه يتم بقول المولى بعت لأنه قال ففعل؛ كذا في المعراج معزياً للفوائد الظهيرية وسكت المؤلف عن بيان المطالب بالثمن لما قدمه أن من الحقوق في البيع راجعة إلى الوكيل فيطالب العبد بالثمن في صورة وقوعه للآمر لكونه وكيلاً كما يطالب بالمال في صورة وقوعه عتقاً لكونه أصيلاً ويرجع في الأول على الأمر، ولا يقال العبد هنا محجور عليه والوكيل إذا كان محجوراً عليه لا ترجع الحقوق إليه لأنا نقول: زال الحجر هنا بالعقد الذي باشره مقترناً بأداء المولى والله أعلم.
And if he said to a slave, 'Buy yourself from your master for me,' and he said to the master, 'Sell me to so-and-so,' and he did, then it is for the commander. And if he did not say 'to so-and-so,' then [the slave] is free.) This is an explanation for when the slave is an agent to buy himself, after the explanation of when the slave is a principal. And it is so because the slave is suitable to be an agent for another in buying himself because he is a stranger regarding his own property, and the sale is directed at him insofar as he is property, except that his property is in his hand so that the seller does not have the right to detain after sale. So when he attributes it to the commander, his act is valid as compliance. Thus the contract occurs for the commander. And if he contracts for himself, he is free because it is an emancipation and the master has agreed to it without exchange. And even though the slave is an agent for a specific purchase, he performed a different type of action, and in such a case it is effective against the agent. And he indicated by his statement 'and if he did not say to so-and-so, [the slave] is free' that if he said 'sell me to yourself,' he is freed even more so. And he is only freed in the absolute case because it admits of two possibilities, so it does not occur as compliance due to doubt, so the action remains occurring for himself. And since the author presented at the beginning of sales that a contract is not formed except with two past-tense utterances, it is known that his statement here in the case of its occurrence for the commander, 'sell me,' is not an offer. So when the master says 'I sold,' then acceptance by the slave is necessary for offer and acceptance, unlike in the case of its occurrence as emancipation, for it is an offer and is completed by the master's statement 'I sold' without the slave's acceptance, based on the principle that one person can perform both sides of the contract in emancipation like marriage, but cannot perform both sides in sale. And in the book, there is an indication that it is completed by the master's statement 'I sold' because he said 'and he did.' As in al-Mi'raj attributed to al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriyyah. And the author remained silent about the explanation of who is demanded for the price because he already presented that the rights in sale revert to the agent, so the slave is demanded for the price in the case of its occurrence for the commander as an agent, just as he is demanded for the money in the case of its occurrence as emancipation as a principal, and he reimburses in the first case the commander. And it cannot be said that the slave here is interdicted and the agent if interdicted rights do not revert to him, for we say: the interdiction is removed here by the contract that he conducted coupled with the master's payment. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, addressing the situation where a slave is appointed as an agent to buy himself from his master. The matn states: if the slave tells the master to sell him to a specific person (the commander), the purchase is for the commander; if he does not specify, the slave becomes free. The commentary explains that a slave can act as an agent for another in buying himself because he is a stranger to his own property rights. If he specifies the commander, the contract belongs to the commander. If he omits specification, the slave is freed because the act is an emancipation, and the master has implicitly agreed to that. The commentary further distinguishes between two forms: if the slave says 'sell me to yourself,' he is freed even more directly. The core legal principle is that emancipation can be completed by one party (the master) alone, whereas sale requires offer and acceptance. Thus, in the emancipation scenario, the master's statement 'I sold' suffices without the slave's acceptance. The commentary also clarifies that the slave can be held liable for the price if he acted as agent, and the interdiction (hajr) is lifted by this contract.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakīl): Agent; here the slave acts as agent for another to buy him.
إعتاق (i‘tāq): Emancipation; the act of freeing a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The purchase is for the commander who appointed the slave.
B) The slave becomes free.
C) The contract is void for lack of specification.
D) The master retains ownership but must pay compensation.

Key Takeaway
If a slave is appointed as an agent to buy himself and names a buyer, the purchase is for that buyer; if he does not, he is freed because the master's intent is emancipation.

Restriction on Agent's Contracts: No Contracting with Inadmissible Witnesses
States a restriction on the agent's authority in sale and purchase contracts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi restriction on an agent for sale and purchase regarding contracting with a person whose testimony is rejected in his favor, and explain the rationale for this prohibition.

Core Idea
An agent may not contract with a person whose testimony would be inadmissible for him, to protect the principal's interest.

When you appoint someone as your agent for sale or purchase, you trust them to act in your best interest. But what if the agent deals with a person who is an enemy or untrustworthy from the agent's perspective? The Hanafi school, following Abu Hanifa, imposes a clear restriction: the agent must not contract with someone whose testimony would be rejected if given for the agent. This lesson unpacks that rule and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Subject
Who is the actor? The agent specifically authorized for sale and purchase (وكيل بالبيع والشراء). Note that this is a restricted agency.
The agent's authority is limited to acting in the principal's benefit.
2
Identify the Prohibited Party
The agent cannot contract with a person whose testimony would be rejected in the agent's favor (من ترد شهادته له). Under Hanafi rules of testimony, this includes enemies, relatives with bias, or persons with a known lack of integrity.
The prohibition is based on the person's relationship to the agent, not the principal.
3
Understand the Reason
The prohibition prevents a conflict of interest. If the agent contracts with such a person, there is a risk that the agent's judgment is compromised, harming the principal.
This is a protective measure for the principal's rights.
4
Note the School Attribution
The ruling is stated according to Abu Hanifa (عن أبي حنيفة). There may be disagreement from the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) or other schools.
The passage indicates potential intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
5
Recognize the Practical Outcome
The agent must avoid such contracts. If the agent violates this, the contract may be invalid or the agent liable for breach of trust.
The shari'a safeguards the principal's property.

Source Text

This passage is a ruling statement from a chapter heading, stating a restriction on the agent's authority: the agent for sale and purchase may not contract with someone whose testimony would be inadmissible in his own favor according to Abu Hanifa's view. Key Takeaway
An agent must not contract with a person who would be an inadmissible witness for the agent, as per Abu Hanifa's ruling to protect the principal's rights.

Agent's Acceptance of a Return with Acknowledgment
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agent's return with acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute regarding the binding effect of an agent's acceptance of a return with acknowledgment and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
An agent's acknowledgment of a defect binds only himself, not his principal, according to the dominant Hanafi narration.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq dives into a critical intra-Hanafi dispute: when an agent accepts the return of defective goods based on his own acknowledgment, does the return become binding on the principal (muwakkil) or only on the agent (wakil)? The author presents two conflicting narrations, analyzes their rationales, and clarifies the relied-upon ruling for fatwa. Understanding this nuance is essential for correctly allocating liability in sales agency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting narrations
Extract the two narrations: one from the general narrations of al-Mabsut (binds the agent) and one from the book of sales (binds the principal). Note which authority supports each.
The text explicitly states 'عامة روايات المبسوط' vs 'رواية البيوع'.
2
Analyze the rationale behind each narration
For the narration binding the agent: reason is that the agent's acknowledgment only concerns himself; the principal is a third party. For the narration binding the principal: because the judge would rule similarly based on evidence.
The text argues that return by mutual consent is a new sale regarding third parties.
3
Examine the qiyas (analogy) used
The text compares the agent for sale with the agent for hire (ijarah). In both, the agent acts without a judge's ruling, and the object is not yet received by the principal. The illa is the absence of judicial determination.
Qiyas leads to binding the principal according to the ijarah analogy.
4
Determine the mufta bihi (operative) position
The commentator prefers the view that the return does not bind the agent, nor does it bind the principal; rather the agent may litigate. But the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the return binds the agent, as per al-Mabsut.
The author says 'الأقرب أن يقال لا يلزمه ولكن له أن يخاصم.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

البائع بنكوله لا يرده على بائعه لاضطرار الوكيل إلى النكول بخلاف البائع؛ كذا في النهاية. وفيها: وقضاء القاضي مع إقرار الوكيل متصور فيما إذا أقر بالعيب وامتنع عن القبول فيقضى عليه جبراً على القبول. أطلق في جواز الرد على الوكيل فشمل ما إذا كان قبل قبض الثمن أو بعده كما في البزازية. وأشار إلى أن الخصومة إنما هي مع الوكيل فلا دعوى للمشتري على الموكل فلو أقر الموكل بعيب فيه وأنكره الوكيل لا يلزم الوكيل ولا الموكل شيء لأن الخصومة فيه من حقوق العقد والموكل أجنبي فيه، ولو أقر الوكيل وأنكر الموكل رده المشتري على الوكيل وإقراره صحيح في حق نفسه لا الموكل؛ كذا في البزازية. ولم يذكر المؤلف الرجوع بالثمن وحكمه أنه يرجع به على الوكيل إن كان نقده الثمن وعلى الموكل إن كان نقده كما في شرح الطحاوي ولم يذكر ما إذا نقد الثمن إلى الوكيل ثم أعطاه هو إلى الموكل ثم وجد المشتري عيباً يرده على الوكيل أم الموكل؟ أفتى القاضي أنه يرده على الوكيل؛ كذا في البزازية. وقيد بالوكيل بالبيع لأن الوكيل بالإجارة إذا أجر وسلم ثم طعن المستأجر فيه بعيب فقبل الوكيل بغير قضاء فإنه يلزم الموكل، ولم يعتبر إجارة جديدة في حق الموكل لأن المعقود عليه إن كان المنافع فهي غير مقبوضة فكان نظير الرد على الوكيل بالبيع قبل القبض وإن كان المعقود عليه العين باعتبار إقامتها مقام المنافع حادث أي كسن زائدة أو كان حادثاً إلا أنه لا يحدث مثله في تلك المدة فرده على الوكيل بإقراره بغير قضاء لزم الوكيل، وليس له أن يخاصم الموكل في عامة روايات المبسوط. وذكر في البيوع أنه يكون رداً على الموكل لأنهما فعلا عين ما يفعله القاضي لو رفع إليه إذ لا يكلفه القاضي على إقامة البينة ولا على الحلف في هذه الصورة بل يرده عليه بلا حجة فكان الحق متعيناً في الرد. قلنا الرد بالتراضي بيع جديد في حق ثالث والموكل ثالثهما ولا نسلم أن الحق متعين في الرد بل يثبت حقه أولاً في وصف السلامة، ثم إذا عجز ينتقل إلى الرد، ثم إذا امتنع الرد بحدوث العيب أو بزيادة حدث فيه ينتقل إلى الرجوع بالنقصان فلم يكن الرد متعيناً. وهكذا ذكر الروايتين في شرح الجامع الصغير وغيره وبين الروايتين تفاوت كثير، لأن فيه نزولاً من اللزوم إلى أن لا يخاصم بالكلية، وكان الأقرب أن يقال لا يلزمه ولكن له أن يخاصم. انتهت وبه علم أن قول المتن وكذا بإقرار فيما لا يحدث مثله أي فيلزم الموكل مبني على رواية البيوع المخالفة لعامة روايات المبسوط من لزومه للوكيل ولذا قال في المواهب: لورد عليه بما لا يحدث مثله بإقرار يلزم الوكيل ولزوم الموكل رواية اهـ. قوله : (ورد عليه بإقرار سواء كان بقضاء أو لا الأصوب الأخصر أن يقال إن كان بقضاء وإلا لم تصح خصومته . فهذا حكم ثبت بالضرورة فلا تعدو موضعها ، كذا في النهاية وقيد بالعيب لما في كافي الحاكم : وإذا قبل الوكيل العبد بغير قضاء القاضي بخيار شرط أو رؤية فهو جائز على الأمر، وكذا لو رده المشتري عليه بعيب قبل القبض بغير قضاء فهو جائز ا هـ .
He returns it with acknowledgment whether with a judge's ruling or not, but if with a ruling he needs litigation with the principal, otherwise his litigation is invalid because he is the buyer. And here nukul (refusal to swear) is treated like evidence, not acknowledgment, and it is not treated likewise regarding the seller, so if he returns to the seller by nukul, he does not return it to his seller due to the agent's necessity for nukul unlike the seller; as in al-Nihaya. And in it: The judge's ruling with the agent's acknowledgment is conceivable when he acknowledges the defect and refuses to accept, so he is compelled to accept. He made absolute the permissibility of return to the agent, so it includes whether before or after receipt of price as in al-Bazzaziyya. And he indicated that litigation is only with the agent, so the buyer has no claim against the principal: if the principal acknowledges a defect and the agent denies it, nothing is binding on either, because litigation is among the rights of the contract and the principal is a stranger to it; if the agent acknowledges and the principal denies, the buyer returns to the agent and his acknowledgment is valid regarding himself not the principal; as in al-Bazzaziyya. And the author did not mention recourse for the price; its ruling is that he has recourse against the agent if he had paid the price to him, and against the principal if he had paid to him as in Sharh al-Tahawi. And he did not mention if he paid the price to the agent, then the agent gave it to the principal, then the buyer found a defect, does he return to the agent or the principal? The judge gave a fatwa that he returns to the agent; as in al-Bazzaziyya. And it is restricted to the agent for sale because the agent for hire, if he leases and delivers, then the lessee disputes a defect, and the agent accepts without a judge's ruling, it binds the principal; a new lease is not considered regarding the principal because if the object of contract is usufruct, it is not received, so it is analogous to return to the agent for sale before receipt; if the object is the property itself considering it as a substitute for usufruct, a new defect, i.e., like an increase or an event that does not occur like it in that period, then his return to the agent with acknowledgment without a judge's ruling binds the agent, and he cannot litigate the principal in the general narrations of al-Mabsut. And it is mentioned in the book of sales that it is a return to the principal because they both did exactly what the judge would do if the case were brought to him, since the judge would not require him to produce evidence nor to take an oath in this form, but would return it to him without proof, so the right is determined in the return. We said: The return by mutual consent is a new sale regarding a third party, and the principal is the third of them, and we do not concede that the right is determined in the return; rather his right is first established in the description of soundness, then if he is unable, it transfers to return, then if return is prevented by occurrence of a defect or an increase, it transfers to recourse for the decrease, so the return is not determined. Thus he mentioned the two narrations in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir and elsewhere, and between the two narrations there is great difference, because it is a descent from binding to not litigating at all, and the closer view is that it is not binding but he may litigate. End. And with this it is known that the statement of the text 'and likewise with acknowledgment regarding what does not occur like it' meaning it binds the principal is based on the narration of the book of sales contrary to the general narrations of al-Mabsut of its binding the agent, and therefore he said in al-Mawahib: 'It is returned to him with what does not occur like it by acknowledgment, it binds the agent, and its binding the principal is a narration.' He said: (He returns with acknowledgment whether with a judge's ruling or not) the most correct and concise is to say if with a ruling [then valid], otherwise his litigation is not valid. This is a ruling established by necessity, so it does not go beyond its place, as in al-Nihaya. And it is restricted by defect because in Kafi al-Hakim: If the agent accepts the slave without a judge's ruling due to option of stipulation or sighting, it is valid against the principal, and likewise if the buyer returns to him due to a defect before receipt without a judge's ruling, it is valid.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This is a detailed discussion of the Hanafi ruling on an agent's acceptance of a return of a defective item with acknowledgment. The passage type is an intra-Hanafi dispute (two narrations) with rational reasoning. The text first states the general rule: the agent can accept a return with or without a judge's ruling, but if there is a judge's ruling, litigation with the principal is needed. Then it explains that nukul (refusal to swear) is treated like evidence (bayyina) for the agent but not for the seller. Next, it distinguishes between the agent's acknowledgment of a defect and the principal's, noting that the buyer's claim is only against the agent. The author then discusses the ruling on recourse for the price and cites a fatwa from al-Bazzaziyya. The passage compares the agent for sale with the agent for hire, using qiyas (analogy). A key point is the existence of two narrations: one from al-Mabsut (binding the agent) and one from the book of sales (binding the principal). The commentator analyzes the difference and suggests the closer view is that the return does not bind the agent but he may litigate. Finally, he clarifies the matn's reference to 'acknowledgment regarding what does not occur like it,' citing al-Mawahib. The discussion concludes with a quote from Kafi al-Hakim that confirms the agent's acceptance without a judge's ruling is valid.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment by the agent of a defect in the sold item or of a rightful return
نكول (nukul): Refusal of a party to take an oath in court, treated here as evidence (bayyinah) for the agent
الرد بالتراضي (al-radd bi al-taradi): Return of goods by mutual consent between the agent and the buyer, considered a new sale regarding third parties

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the general narrations of al-Mabsut, whom does the agent's acceptance of a return with acknowledgment bind?
A) The principal (al-muwakkil)
B) The agent (al-wakil)
C) The buyer (al-mushtari)
D) The judge (al-qadi)

Key Takeaway
The agent's acknowledgment of a defect binds himself, not his principal, in the dominant Hanafi position.

The Agent's Credibility in Debt Payment
Fatwa on agent's credibility in debt payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when an agent appointed to pay a debt claims he paid and the creditor confirms, and explain the basis of his credibility as an amin.

Core Idea
An agent appointed to pay a debt is believed if the creditor confirms because he is an amin.

In Munyat al-Mufti, a concise fatwa addresses a common scenario: a person appoints someone to pay his debt. Later, the appointee claims he paid, and the creditor confirms. This lesson unpacks the ruling and the underlying principle of trusteeship (amanah) that governs the agent's credibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their roles
The principal (al-muwakkil) appoints an agent (al-ma'mur) to pay a debt to a creditor (al-ba'i'). The agent claims he performed the task.
2
Recognize the agent's legal status
The agent is an amin (trustee) in Hanafi law regarding the payment. This means he is entrusted with the task and his word is trusted unless evidence suggests otherwise.
Amin status is a key feature in agency and deposit contracts.
3
Apply the ruling with the creditor's confirmation
If the creditor confirms the agent's claim of payment, the agent is believed. This is because his trusteeship combined with external confirmation removes any doubt.
The ruling does not address the case where the creditor denies or is silent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي منية المفتي : أمر رجلاً أن يقضي عنه دينه فقال المأمور بعد ذلك قضيت وصدقه قوله : (في يد البائع) أي الموكل قوله : ( والمأمور على المديون بما قضى) قال الرملي : صوابه على
And in Munyat al-Mufti: A man appointed another to pay his debt, then the appointee said after that 'I paid' and [the creditor] confirmed him. He said: (in the hand of the seller) i.e., the principal. He said: (and the appointee is [trusted] regarding the debtor for what he paid). Al-Ramli said: The correct is on
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a brief ruling statement from Munyat al-Mufti, cited as a fatwa. It addresses the case where a person is appointed to pay a debt (i.e., as an agent for payment). The appointee claims he paid the debt, and the creditor confirms. The text then has a problematic section: 'قوله : (في يد البائع) أي الموكل' appears to be a comment explaining a phrase from the matn, but the OCR is corrupt. The end mentions Al-Ramli's correction, but it is cut off. The key point is that the appointee is believed regarding payment if the creditor confirms, as he is an amin (trustee).

Key Terms
أمين (amin): Trustee; the appointee is considered an amin regarding the payment

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The agent must produce a receipt
B) The creditor must confirm
C) The principal must witness the payment
D) The debt must be small

Key Takeaway
An agent appointed to pay a debt is believed if the creditor confirms, due to his status as an amin.

Agent's Right to Reimbursement: Condition of Delivery of Benefit
Ruling on agent's recourse after paying principal's debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for an agent's right to reimbursement when he pays the principal's debt from his own money, and apply the qiyas (analogy) to a buyer used by the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
An agent's right to reimbursement from the principal is conditional on delivering the benefit—like a buyer pays only after receiving goods.

In this passage, we examine a common fiqh scenario: an agent pays the principal's debt from his own money, but the principal denies the debt or the agency. When can the agent demand repayment? The Hanafi school, drawing on an analogy to a buyer, rules that the agent only has recourse if he has actually delivered the benefit to the principal—i.e., freed him from the debt. We will unpack the legal reasoning and its procedural implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Disputed Ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? An agent pays the principal's debt from his own money. The dispute: does the agent have immediate recourse against the principal?
The principal denies the debt or the order; the agent claims repayment.
2
Extract the Hanafi Position
The text states clearly: 'the agent does not have recourse against the principal because… he only has recourse if he delivered to the principal what was in his liability.' So the condition is delivery of benefit (tasleem al-manfa'ah).
This is the mufta bihi position; al-Quduri affirms it.
3
Analyze the Qiyas (Analogy) to a Buyer
The agent is compared to a buyer who must pay the price only after the seller delivers the purchased item. Similarly, the agent's payment is only reimbursable after he has delivered the benefit of freeing the principal from debt.
Asl: buyer-seller contract; far': agent-principal relationship; illa: delivery of benefit precedes payment.
4
Apply the Ruling to the Given Scenario
The scenario: agent pays, creditor absent, principal denies. The rule is that the agent must prove three things: the debt, the order, and the payment. If proven, the judge rules that the principal owes the agent reimbursement, even though the creditor is absent.
The agent can serve as a representative of the absent creditor for litigation purposes.
5
Note the Ikhtilaf and Cross-Madhab View
Some non-Hanafi schools allow the agent immediate recourse without requiring delivery of benefit. The Hanafi school's stricter condition is based on the principle that reimbursement follows actual benefit.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; the Hanafi position is zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأمر وكذبه رب الدين وحلف رجع رب الدين على الأمر لكن لا يرجع المأمور على الأمر لأن المأمور وكيل بشراء ما في ذمة الآمر بمثله وبنقد الثمن من مال نفسه فإنما يرجع على الآمر لو سلم للآمر ما في ذمته كالمشتري إنما يؤمر بدفع الثمن إذا سلم له ما اشترى. وذكر القدوري أنه يرجع رب الدين على المديون بالدين والمأمور على المديون بما قضى أمر غيره بقضاء دينه فقضاه وجاء ليرجع عليه فقال الآمر ما كان لفلان علي شيء أصلاً ولا أمرتك أن تقضيه ولا أنت قضيته شيئاً ورب الدين غائب فأقام المأمور البينة على الدين والأمر بالقضاء والقضاء فإن القاضي يقضي بالمال على الآمر للغائب وبالرجوع للمأمور على الأمر وإن كان رب الدين غائباً لأن عنه خصماً حاضراً حكماً لأن ما يدعيه الغائب سبب لثبوت ما يدعيه وفي مثله ينتصب الحاضر خصماً ا هـ.
The creditor denied [the debt] and swore, so the creditor has recourse against the principal, but the agent does not have recourse against the principal because the agent is an agent for buying what is in the principal's liability with its equivalent and paying the price from his own money. So he only has recourse against the principal if he delivered to the principal what was in his liability, like a buyer who is only ordered to pay the price when what he bought is delivered to him. Al-Quduri mentioned that the creditor has recourse against the debtor for the debt, and the agent has recourse against the debtor for what he paid, [as] he ordered someone else to pay his debt and he paid it. Then he came to seek recourse against him, and the principal said, 'There was nothing owed by me to so-and-so at all, nor did I order you to pay it, nor did you pay anything.' The creditor was absent. The agent presented evidence of the debt, the order to pay, and the payment. So the judge decides that the property is owed by the principal to the absent creditor, and the agent has recourse against the principal, even though the creditor is absent, because there is a present adversary [acting] as a representative, since what the absent [creditor] claims is a cause for establishing what [the agent] claims. In such a case, the present one is appointed as adversary. End quote.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on a dispute between an agent who paid the principal's debt and the principal who denies the debt or the agency. The principle is that the agent, though he paid from his own money, only has recourse against the principal if he actually delivered the benefit to the principal — i.e., freed the principal from the debt. If the principal does not acknowledge the debt, the agent must prove the debt, the order, and the payment. If the creditor is absent, the agent can be considered a representative of the absent creditor for the purpose of litigation, allowing the judge to rule in favor of the absent party. This is based on the logic that the agent's claim is causally linked to the creditor's claim.

Key Terms
الآمر (al-āmir): The principal who orders the agent to perform an act (here, pay a debt).
المأمور (al-ma'mūr): The agent who is commanded to perform the act.
رب الدين (rabb al-dayn): The creditor (the one to whom the debt is owed).
تسليم المنفعة (tasleem al-manfa'ah): Delivery of the benefit; here, freeing the principal from the debt.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning; here, the analogy between the agent's payment and a buyer's payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, when does the agent who pays the principal's debt from his own money have recourse against the principal?
A) Immediately upon payment, regardless of the principal's acknowledgment.
B) Only if the principal acknowledges the debt and order.
C) Only if he delivered the benefit of the payment to the principal (i.e., freed him from the debt).
D) Only if the creditor is present to testify.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school conditions the agent's right to reimbursement on delivering the benefit of freeing the principal from debt, supported by qiyas to a buyer, and requires the agent to prove the debt, order, and payment for judicial enforcement.

Whose Word Is Accepted in Principal-Agent Disputes: Hanafi Summary
Summary of whose word is accepted in principal-agent disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for whose statement is believed in a dispute between principal and agent and apply it to different types of agency given the timing of the claim.

Core Idea
In agency disputes, the agent's word is not automatically accepted; it depends on timing, delivery, and the type of agency.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq summarizes the relied-upon positions in the Hanafi school regarding conflicts between a principal and his agent over whether the agent performed the assigned act. The ruling varies based on several factors: the timing of the agent's report relative to dismissal, whether the principal is alive or dead, and the specific type of agency. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for anyone dealing with agency contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general default rule
Start by recognizing that the default presumption depends on when the agent reports. If the agent claims he performed the act after being dismissed by the principal, the principal's word is taken (the agent is not believed). If before dismissal, the agent is believed provided the subject matter (e.g., the sold item) was delivered to him.
This is the core principle for agents for sale.
2
Consider the scenario after the principal's death
If the principal is deceased and the agent's report is after death, the agent's word is accepted only if the item in question was already destroyed (e.g., the sold item perished). Otherwise, the heir's denial prevails.
The heir steps into the principal's position.
3
Distinguish between types of agency
The passage lists specific rulings for different agents: for purchase (ruling given earlier), for emancipation (agent not believed), for kitabah (believed re contract but not receipt/destruction), for marriage (not believed), and for debt collection (believed if claims receipt and destruction).
Each type has its own rule, which may differ from the default for sale.
4
Synthesize the pattern
Notice that the agent is generally believed when he reports an act that is known to be within his authority and when there is no conflicting interest (e.g., after dismissal the principal wants to avoid the contract). The exceptions arise when the agent's claim cannot be easily verified or involves a transfer of cash.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أنهما إذا اختلفا في فعل الوكيل بأن ادعى الوكيل الفعل وأنكره موكله فإن كان أخبار الوكيل بعد عزله فالقول للموكل، وإن كان قبله في حياة الموكل فالقول للوكيل إن كان المبيع مسلماً إليه وإلا لا، وإن كان بعد موته حال هلاك العين فكذلك وإلا لم يقبل قوله إذا كذبه الوارث هذا في الوكيل بالبيع، وأما الوكيل بالشراء فسبق حكمه عند الاختلاف، وأما وكيل العتق فلا يقبل قوله، وأما وكيل الكتابة فيقبل قوله في العقد لا في القبض والهلاك، ولا يقبل قول وكيل النكاح والوكيل بقبض الدين إذا ادعى القبض والهلاك مصدق.
The summary: When they disagree about the action of the agent — the agent claiming the action and the principal denying it — then if the agent's report is after his dismissal, the word is the principal's. If it was before his dismissal during the principal's lifetime, the word is the agent's if the sold item was delivered to him, otherwise not. If it was after his death at the time of the destruction of the item, likewise, otherwise his word is not accepted if the heir denies him. This is for an agent for sale. As for an agent for purchase, its ruling preceded under the topic of disagreement. As for an agent for emancipation, his word is not accepted. As for an agent for kitabah, his word is accepted regarding the contract, but not regarding receipt or destruction. The word of an agent for marriage is not accepted. The agent for receipt of debt, if he claims receipt and destruction, is believed.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage provides a concise summary of the ruling on whose statement is believed when the principal and agent dispute whether the agent performed the assigned act. The key factors are: timing of the agent's report (before or after dismissal), whether the subject matter was delivered, and whether the principal is alive or dead. For an agent for sale, if the agent claims he sold after dismissal, the principal is believed; if before dismissal, the agent is believed provided the sold item was delivered to him. After the principal's death, the agent's word is accepted only if the item was already destroyed. For an agent for purchase, the ruling was given earlier in the chapter. An agent for emancipation is not believed without proof. An agent for kitabah is believed about the contract but not about receipt of money or loss. An agent for marriage is not believed. An agent for debt collection is believed if he claims he received the debt and the property was destroyed.

Key Terms
وكيل بالبيع (wakīl bil-bay'): Agent authorized to sell the principal's property.
وكيل بالشراء (wakīl bil-shirā'): Agent authorized to purchase for the principal.
وكيل بالعتق (wakīl bil-'itq): Agent authorized to emancipate a slave.
وكيل الكتابة (wakīl bil-kitābah): Agent authorized to enter into a kitabah contract (manumission by payment).
وكيل النكاح (wakīl bil-nikāh): Agent authorized to contract marriage on behalf of the principal.
وكيل بقبض الدين (wakīl bi-qabd al-dayn): Agent authorized to collect a debt owed to the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
An agent for marriage claims he conducted a marriage contract for the principal. The principal denies. Whose word is accepted?
A) The agent's, because he is the one who performed the contract.
B) The principal's, because marriage is a sensitive matter.
C) The agent's, only if the marriage was consummated.
D) Neither, the judge decides based on evidence.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the acceptance of an agent's word in a dispute with the principal hinges on the timing of the report, the delivery of the subject matter, and the specific type of agency.

Agent Buying the Principal's Brother: Denial and Consequences
Ruling on agent buying brother and principal's denial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a principal denies that the agent bought his brother, and explain the consequences for the slave and the agent.

Core Idea
The principal's denial is accepted with oath, but the agent's declaration frees the slave, and the agent bears the price.

This lesson examines a specific case from Khizanat al-Muftin where an agent is appointed to purchase the principal's brother. After the purchase, the principal denies that the purchased slave is his brother. The passage provides a nuanced ruling that balances the rights of the principal, agent, and slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claims
The principal claims the purchased slave is not his brother, denying the obligation to pay the price. The agent claims the opposite and also declares the slave is the principal's brother.
The principal's oath is required.
2
Determine who is believed
The principal's word is taken with his oath because he denies the obligation of the price. This aligns with the principle that the defendant's word is accepted with an oath.
3
Assess the effect on the slave
Despite the principal's denial, the slave is freed due to the agent's statement 'this is your brother.' The agent's declaration of brotherhood effects the emancipation.
This is a unique ruling.
4
Determine liability for the price
The purchase is binding on the agent, meaning the agent must pay the price. The principal is not liable because he denies the obligation.
The agent becomes the owner but then the slave is freed by his declaration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي خزانة المفتين : وكل رجلاً بأن يشتري أخاه فاشترى فقال الأمر ليس هذا أخي فالقول له مع يمينه لأنه ينكر وجوب الثمن عليه ويلزم الشراء للوكيل لكن يعتق بقوله هذا أخوك ا.هـ. وفي كافي الحاكم في باب الوكالة بالعتق : وإن وكله أن يكاتب عبده يوم الجمعة فقال الوكيل يوم السبت قد كاتبته أمس بعد الوكالة على كذا وكذا وكذبه المولى فالقول للمولى في القياس ولكني أدع القياس وأجيزه، وكذلك البيع والإجارة والعتق على مال والخلع فإن الوكيل مصدق، ولو وكله أن يكاتبه فقال الوكيل وكلتني أمس وكاتبته آخر النهار بعد الوكالة وقال رب العبد إنما وكلتك اليوم فالقول قول رب العبد وتبطل المكاتبة، وكذلك البيع والنكاح والخلع والعتق ا هـ.
In Khizanat al-Muftin: A man appointed someone to buy his brother, so he bought him, and the principal said, 'This is not my brother.' The word is his, with his oath, because he denies the obligation of the price upon him. The purchase is binding on the agent, but [the slave] becomes free by the agent's statement 'this is your brother.' End quote. In Kafi al-Hakim, in the chapter of agency for emancipation: If he appointed him to make a kitabah contract with his slave on Friday, and the agent said on Saturday, 'I made kitabah with him yesterday after the agency for such and such,' and the master denied him, then the word is the master's according to qiyas, but I (the judge) abandon qiyas and uphold it. Similarly for sale, lease, emancipation for money, and khul': the agent is believed. If he appointed him to make kitabah, and the agent said, 'You appointed me yesterday, and I made kitabah at the end of the day after the agency,' and the master of the slave said, 'I only appointed you today,' then the word is the master's, and the kitabah is void. Similarly for sale, marriage, khul', and emancipation. End quote.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage presents two specific scenarios from later Hanafi works. In Khizanat al-Muftin: If a man appoints an agent to buy his brother, and the agent does so, but the principal denies that the slave is his brother, the principal's word is accepted with his oath, because he denies any obligation to pay the price. However, the purchase is binding on the agent personally, and the slave is freed by the agent's statement that he is the principal's brother. This is a nuanced ruling that protects the principal while recognizing the agent's action.

In Kafi al-Hakim: Two cases regarding kitabah timing. First, if the principal specifies a day (e.g., Friday) for making kitabah, and the agent claims he did it on Friday after the agency, but the master denies it, normally qiyas would favor the master, but the judge abandons qiyas and upholds the agent's claim, making the kitabah valid. The same ruling applies to sale, lease, emancipation for money, and khul'. Second, if the agent claims the appointment was yesterday and he acted immediately, while the master says the appointment was today, the master's word prevails and the contract is void. This distinction depends on the scope of the dispute.

Key Terms
خزانة المفتين (khizānat al-muftīn): A later Hanafi fatwa compilation, source of this ruling.
الوكالة بالعتق (al-wakālah bil-'itq): Agency for emancipation of a slave.
الكتابة (al-kitābah): A contract of manumission by which a slave agrees to pay a sum to the master in exchange for freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case from Khizanat al-Muftin where an agent buys the principal's brother and the principal denies brotherhood, what happens to the slave?
A) The slave remains enslaved and the agent must free him.
B) The slave is freed due to the agent's statement, and the agent is liable for the price.
C) The principal must pay the price because he is bound by the agent's action.
D) The slave is freed and the principal is not liable for the price.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when an agent buys the principal's brother and the principal denies, the agent's declaration frees the slave, but the agent bears the price.

Kitabah Timing Dispute: Agent's Claim vs. Master's Denial
Rulings on kitabah timing disputes and agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between two kitabah timing dispute scenarios and apply the Hanafi ruling on whose word is accepted.

Core Idea
When the agent claims he performed kitabah on the specified day, his word is accepted against the master's denial; when the dispute is about the date of appointment, the master's word prevails.

This lesson examines two scenarios from Kafi al-Hakim concerning disputes about the timing of a kitabah contract made by an agent. The rulings are applied uniformly to similar contracts like sale, lease, emancipation for money, and khul'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First scenario: master specifies a day for kitabah, agent claims he did it on that day, master denies. Second scenario: agent claims appointment was yesterday, master says today.
2
Apply the first scenario ruling
In the first scenario, the judge abandons qiyas and upholds the agent's claim, making the kitabah valid. The agent is believed because he is acting under agency.
This is an exception to the general rule that the master's word is taken.
3
Apply the second scenario ruling
In the second scenario, the master's word is taken, and the kitabah is void. The difference is that the dispute is about the existence of the agency itself, not just the act.
4
Note the application to other contracts
The same ruling applies to sale, lease, emancipation for money, and khul'. The agent is believed in the first type of dispute.

Source Text

Agent Taking Pledge or Surety for Price: Authority and Limits
Ruling with proof on agent's authority to take collateral

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the authority of a sale agent and a debt-collection agent to take collateral, and explain the Hanafi reconciliation of conflicting sources.

Core Idea
A sale agent is a principal in securing the price; a debt-collection agent is a deputy, limited to receipt.

This lesson examines a key distinction in Hanafi agency law: the scope of an agent's authority to take a pledge (rahn) or surety (kafala) for a debt. The classical texts distinguish sharply between an agent for sale (wakīl bil-bayʿ) and an agent for debt collection (wakīl bi-qabḍ al-dayn). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying agency rules in commercial transactions. We will also explore how later Hanafi jurists reconciled an apparent conflict between al-Hidayah and other authoritative works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Types of Agents
First, recognize the two categories: the agent for sale (who sells and collects the price) and the agent for debt collection (who is only appointed to collect an existing debt). Note that the source text treats them differently regarding collateral.
The sale agent is called wakīl bil-bayʿ; the debt collector is wakīl bi-qabḍ al-dayn.
2
Understand the Principle: Aṣīl vs. Nāʾib
The Hanafi rationale: the sale agent is considered an 'aṣīl' (principal) in rights related to the sale, so he has the authority to secure the price via collateral. The debt collector is a 'nāʾib' (deputy) and his authority is limited to receipt; he cannot take collateral unless explicitly authorized.
This distinction is based on qiyas (analogy) from the nature of agency.
3
Recognize the Textual Conflict
Al-Hidayah states that the debt-collection agent cannot take a surety. However, al-Khulāṣa and al-Bazzāziyya allow it. Note the disagreement within the Hanafi school.
The conflict is resolved by interpreting the surety in al-Hidayah as a hawala (transfer of debt), which the agent cannot accept.
4
Apply the Reconciliation
The resolution: the debt-collection agent may take a surety (kafāla) but not a hawala (which exonerates the original debtor). Thus, the texts are harmonized. The sale agent may take both pledge and surety without restriction.
This is the muftā bihī position according to al-Baḥr al-Rāʾiq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو أخذ الوكيل بالثمن رهناً فضاع أو كفيلاً فتوى عليه لا يضمن لأن الوكيل أصيل في الحقوق وقبض الثمن منها والكفالة توثق به والارتهان وثيقة لجانب الاستيفاء فيملكها بخلاف الوكيل بقبض الدين لأنه يفعل نيابة وقد أنابه في قبض الدين دون الكفالة وأخذ الرهن والوكيل بالبيع يقبض إصالة ولهذا لا يملك الموكل حجره عنه؛ كذا في الهداية. وهذا مخالف لما في الخلاصة والبزازية من أن الوكيل بقبض الدين له أخذ الكفيل فيحمل كلام الهداية على أخذ الكفيل بشرط براءة الأصيل فإنها حينئذ حوالة وهو لا يملكها لما في البزازية. ولو أخذ كفيلاً بشرط البراءة فهو حوالة لا يجوز للوكيل بقبض الدين قبولها اهـ. ومن هنا قال صاحب النهاية : المراد بالكفالة هنا الحوالة لأن التوي لا يتحقق في الكفالة، وقيل الكفالة على به قوله : (والظاهر أنها كالوكالة من حيث أن الأصل فيها التقييد قال الرملي : ومثل المضاربة الشركة الظاهر أن الأصل فيها الإطلاق لأنها مبنية عليها وما علل به الزيلعي كالصريح فيه فتأمل قوله : (والأوجه أن يقال الخ ما قاله الزيلعي نص عليه النسفي في الكافي بقوله : أو أخذ بثمنه
His saying: (And if the agent takes a pledge for the price and it is lost, or takes a surety and there is a fatwa against it, he is not liable), because the agent is a principal in rights, and receipt of the price is among them, and suretyship strengthens him, and pawning is a document for the side of collection, so he has the right to do it, unlike the agent for receipt of debt, because he acts as a deputy and has been appointed to receive the debt, not to take suretyship or pawning. The agent for sale receives as a principal, and for this reason the principal cannot bar him from it. So it is in al-Hidayah. This contradicts what is in al-Khulasa and al-Bazzaziyyah that the agent for receipt of debt has the right to take a surety. So the statement of al-Hidayah is interpreted as taking a surety with the condition of exoneration of the original debtor, because that is a hawala (transfer of debt), which he does not have the right to accept. In al-Bazzaziyyah: 'If he takes a surety with the condition of exoneration, it is a hawala, and it is not permissible for the agent for receipt of debt to accept it.' End quote. Hence the author of al-Nihaya said: 'What is meant by kafala here is hawala, because tawi (?) is not realized in kafala.' And it is said: 'kafala upon it.' His saying: (And the apparent is that it is like agency in that the default is restriction.) al-Ramli said: 'And similar to mudarabah is partnership (shirkah); the apparent is that the default is generality because it is based on it, and what al-Zayla'i reasoned seems clear, so consider.' His saying: (And the most appropriate is to say... what al-Zayla'i said, al-Nasafi stated in al-Kafi by saying: Or he takes with its price... [text cut off].
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage clarifies the agent's authority to take collateral (pledge or surety) for the sale price. The agent for sale (wakil bil-bay') is considered an 'asil' (principal) in rights related to the sale, so he can take a pledge or surety to secure the price. If the pledge is lost without his negligence, he is not liable. In contrast, an agent appointed merely to collect a debt (wakil bi-qabd al-dayn) is a deputy and cannot take a pledge or surety because his authority is limited to receipt. However, there is a conflict in the sources: al-Khulasa and al-Bazzaziyyah allow the debt-collection agent to take a surety. Al-Hidayah's position is reconciled by restricting it to cases where the surety is taken with the condition that the original debtor is exonerated; such an arrangement is actually a hawala (transfer of debt), which the agent cannot accept because it changes the nature of the obligation. Thus, the agent for debt collection can take a regular surety (without exonerating the original debtor) according to some texts, but cannot accept a hawala. The passage ends with a note on mudarabah and partnership, but the OCR is fragmented.

Key Terms
الرهن (al-rahn): A pledge: an item given as security for a debt.
الكفالة (al-kafālah): Suretyship: a guarantee by a third party that the debt will be paid.
الحوالة (al-ḥawālah): Transfer of debt: a transaction where the original debtor is exonerated and the surety becomes the primary debtor.
وكيل بقبض الدين (wakīl bi-qabd al-dayn): Agent appointed solely to collect a debt owed to the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the reconciliation in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, what type of surety is prohibited for a debt-collection agent?
A) A surety that does not exonerate the original debtor
B) A surety that exonerates the original debtor (hawala)
C) Any surety at all
D) A surety given by a person who is not the debtor

Key Takeaway
A sale agent has broad authority to take pledge or surety; a debt-collection agent may take only kafala, not hawala.

Joint Agency: One Agent Cannot Act Alone
Explains why one joint agent cannot act alone, with exceptions and comparisons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on joint agents acting alone, distinguish between simultaneous and consecutive appointment, and apply the ruling to cases of death or insanity.

Core Idea
Joint agents appointed together must act jointly; consecutive appointment permits independent action.

This lesson explores a key principle in Hanafi agency law: when two agents are appointed together, the principal's consent is to their combined judgment, not to either individually. Understanding when an agent may act alone is crucial for valid transactions. We will examine the ruling, its basis in consent, and important exceptions including consecutive appointment and the distinction from executors.

Guided Path
1
State the basic ruling and its condition
Identify that neither joint agent may act alone, and note the condition that they were appointed with one statement (simultaneously). This is the default ruling.
The passage says 'it is restricted to when he appointed them both with one statement.'
2
Analyze the underlying reason (talil)
Understand that the prohibition stems from the principal's consent: he agreed to both opinions together, not to one individually. Even if the price is fixed, there is room for discretion.
The principal may have chosen them for their collective judgment.
3
Recognize the exception for consecutive appointment
If the principal appointed them one after the other (consecutively), each may act alone because he consented to each individually at the time of appointment.
This is a key distinction from simultaneous appointment.
4
Distinguish agents from executors
Executors (wasiyyan) must always act jointly, even if appointed separately, because at death they become a single unit. This contrasts with agents where the ruling is based on the moment of appointment.
The passage says 'unlike the two executors'.
5
Apply to special cases: death, insanity, and the simultaneous purchase scenario
If one agent dies or becomes insane, the other cannot act alone. In the scenario from al-Nawazil, if two people are told 'one of you is appointed' and both buy simultaneously, both purchases are for the principal; if they buy one after the other, the second is for himself.
This illustrates the importance of timing and consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولا) يتصرف أحد الوكيلين وحده لأن الموكل رضي برأيهما لا برأي أحدهما والبدل وإن كان مقدراً ولكن التقدير لا يمنع استعمال الرأي في الزيادة واختيار المشتري . أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان أحدهما حراً بالغاً والآخر عبداً أو صبياً محجوراً عليه لكنه مقيد بما إذا كان وكلهما بكلام واحد، أما إذا كان توكيلهما على التعاقب فإنه يجوز لأحدهما الانفراد لأنه رضي برأي كل واحد منهما على الانفراد وقت توكيله فلا يتغير بعد ذلك بخلاف الوصيين فإنه إذا أوصى إلى كل منهما بكلام على حدة لم يجز لأحدهما الانفراد في الأصح لأنه عند الموت صارا وصيين جملة واحدة وفي الوكالة يثبت حكمها بنفس التوكيل وشمل ما إذا مات أحدهما أو ذهب عقله فلا يجوز للآخر التصرف وحده لعدم رضاه برأيه وحده . ولو كانا وصيين فمات أحدهما لا يتصرف الحي إلا بأمر القاضي كما في وصايا الخانية. وفي الخانية : رجل قال لرجلين وكلت أحدكما بشراء جارية لي بألف درهم فاشترى أحدهما ثم اشترى الآخر فإن الآخر يكون مشترياً لنفسه ولو اشترى كل واحد منهما جارية ووقع اشتراؤهما في وقت واحد كانت الجاريتان للموكل ؛ كذا ذكره في النوازل وعليه الفتوى ا هـ .
His saying: (And neither) one of the two agents acts alone because the principal consented to both their opinions, not to the opinion of one of them. And the consideration (consideration?) is that even if the price is fixed, estimation does not prevent the use of opinion regarding increase and selection of the buyer. He made it absolute, so it includes when one of them is a free adult and the other is a slave or a boy under interdiction. But it is restricted to when he appointed them both with one statement. As for when their appointment was consecutive, then one of them may act alone because he consented to the opinion of each one individually at the time of his appointment, so it does not change afterward. Unlike the two executors (wasiyyan), for if he bequeathed to each with separate statements, one of them may not act alone according to the most sound view, because at death they become executors as a single whole. And in agency, its ruling is established by the appointment itself. And it includes if one of them dies or loses his mind, then the other may not act alone because he did not consent to his opinion alone. And if they were two executors and one dies, the living one does not act except by order of the qadi, as in Wasaaya al-Khaniyyah. And in al-Khaniyyah: a man said to two men, 'I appointed one of you to buy a slave-girl for me for one thousand dirhams,' then one of them bought, then the other bought, then the other is a purchaser for himself. And if each of them bought a slave-girl and their purchases occurred at the same time, both slave-girls are for the principal; as mentioned in al-Nawazil, and upon it is the fatwa. End.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage expands on the matn ruling that one of two joint agents cannot act alone. The commentator explains that the reason is the principal's consent to the judgment of both together, not individually. This applies even if one is a slave and the other free, but the ruling is limited to when they were appointed with one statement. If appointed consecutively, each may act alone because the principal consented to each individually at the time of appointment. A contrast is made with executors (wasiyyan), where even if appointed separately, they must act jointly after the testator's death. The passage also covers the effect of death or insanity of one agent, and a special case from al-Khaniyyah where if a man says 'I appointed one of you' and both buy, the second purchase is for the agent himself unless simultaneous, in which case both are for the principal.

Key Terms
وكيلان (wakilaan): Two agents appointed jointly by the principal.
تعاقب (ta'aqub): Consecutive appointment, one after the other.
وصيان (wasiyyan): Two executors appointed in a will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a principal appoints two agents with a single statement, can one of them act alone?
A) Yes, because both are agents.
B) No, because the principal consented to both their opinions together.
C) Yes, if one is a slave.
D) No, unless one of them dies.

Key Takeaway
Joint agents appointed together must act jointly; only consecutive appointment or special circumstances allow independent action, and the ruling is based on the principal's consent at the time of appointment.

Joint Agents: The General Rule and Its Exceptions
Explains the default prohibition and the three exceptions for joint agents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general ruling that one of two joint agents cannot act alone and identify the three exceptions (lawsuit, divorce without compensation, manumission without compensation) and explain the rationale and the intra-Hanafi disagreement on ratification.

Core Idea
One of two joint agents may not act alone except in lawsuits, divorce without compensation, and manumission — the principal's consent is to both combined.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author expounds the ruling on jointly appointed agents (wakilayn). The general principle is that neither agent may act independently because the principal's satisfaction is with their combined judgment, not with either individually. However, the law carves out three exceptions where practical necessity or explicit textual relaxation permits individual action. This lesson walks through the rule, its rationale, the scope conditions, and the scholarly differences within the Hanafi school on the effect of subsequent ratification.

Guided Path
1
State the default ruling
Identify what the passage rules: one of two joint agents cannot act alone. The ruling is that any such act is not effective (la yanfudhu) unless an exception applies.
The key term is 'لا يتصرف' meaning 'may not act' — this is a prohibition of validity, not just permissibility.
2
Identify the 'illa (reason)
The passage gives the reason: 'because the principal was satisfied with both opinions, not with the opinion of one.' This is the effective cause: the principal's consent is limited to joint action.
Even if the price is predetermined, discretion remains in choosing the buyer, so individual action still violates the condition.
3
Determine the scope of the ruling
The ruling applies when both agents are appointed in a single statement. If appointed successively, each may act alone because the principal accepted each opinion separately at the time of appointment.
This is contrasted with executors (wasiyyain), who are considered joint even if appointed separately, because the obligation arises at death.
4
List the three exceptions
The passage states three exceptions: (1) lawsuits (khusumah) — one agent may litigate alone due to the difficulty of joint litigation; (2) divorce without compensation (talaq bi-la badal); (3) manumission without compensation (itaq bi-la badal).
The divorce and manumission exceptions are specifically without compensation; if there is compensation (e.g., khul'), then joint action is required.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on ratification
If one agent sells in the presence of the other and the other immediately approves, the sale is valid. But if the other agent is absent and later ratifies, Abu Hanifa holds that the ratification does not validate the sale, while Abu Yusuf holds it does. Also, a sale between the two agents themselves is invalid according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but valid according to Abu Yusuf.
The mufta bihi is the position of Abu Hanifa, as followed in the majority of Hanafi works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولا) يتصرف أحد الوكيلين وحده لأن الموكل رضي برأيهما لا برأي أحدهما والبدل وإن كان مقدراً ولكن التقدير لا يمنع استعمال الرأي في الزيادة واختيار المشتري . أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان أحدهما حراً بالغاً والآخر عبداً أو صبياً محجوراً عليه لكنه مقيد بما إذا كان وكلهما بكلام واحد، أما إذا كان توكيلهما على التعاقب فإنه يجوز لأحدهما الانفراد لأنه رضي برأي كل واحد منهما على الانفراد وقت توكيله فلا يتغير بعد ذلك بخلاف الوصيين فإنه إذا أوصى إلى كل منهما بكلام على حدة لم يجز لأحدهما الانفراد في الأصح لأنه عند الموت صارا وصيين جملة واحدة وفي الوكالة يثبت حكمها بنفس التوكيل وشمل ما إذا مات أحدهما أو ذهب عقله فلا يجوز للآخر التصرف وحده لعدم رضاه برأيه وحده . ولو كانا وصيين فمات أحدهما لا يتصرف الحي إلا بأمر القاضي كما في وصايا الخانية. وفي الخانية : رجل قال لرجلين وكلت أحدكما بشراء جارية لي بألف درهم فاشترى أحدهما ثم اشترى الآخر فإن الآخر يكون مشترياً لنفسه ولو اشترى كل واحد منهما جارية ووقع اشتراؤهما في وقت واحد كانت الجاريتان للموكل ؛ كذا ذكره في النوازل وعليه الفتوى ا هـ . وفي الذخيرة وفي المنتقى عن محمد: رجل وكل رجلاً بقبض كل حق له ثم فارقه ثم وكل آخر بقبض كل دين له فقبض الوكيل الأوّل شيئاً من الدين فليس للوكيل الثاني أن يقبضه من الأول لأنه الساعة عين وليس بدين ولو وكل الأوّل بقبض كل حق له ثم وكل الثاني بقبض كل شيء له وقبض الأوّل شيئاً من الدين فللثاني أن يقبضه من الأول، ولو وكل رجلاً بقبض داره التي في موضع كذا التي في يد فلان فمضى الوكيل ثم وكل آخر بعده بمثل ما وكل به الأول في قبض هذه بعينها، فإن كان الأول قد قبض الدار قبل توكيل الثاني فللثاني أن يقبضها لأنها صارت مقبوضة لصاحبها ا.هـ. والمراد من قوله لا يتصرف عدم نفاذ تصرفه ، فلو باع أحدهما بحضرة صاحبه فإن أجاز صاحبه صحته كما في الإصلاح، جاز وإلا فلا، ولو كان غائباً فأجازه لم يجز في قول أبي حنيفة ؛ كذا في الشرح. قال الحاكم أبو الفضل: هذا خلاف ما ذكر في الأصل. وقال أبو يوسف جاز ذلك؛ كذا في الخزانة . أحدهما ولو باع من صاحبه شيئاً لم يجز لما في وصايا الخانية : ولو باع أحد الوصيين شيئاً من التركة لصاحبه لم يجز عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد ويجوز عند أبي يوسف ا هـ.
قوله : (إلا) في خصومة فإن لأحدهما أن يخاصم وحده لأنها وإن كانت تحتاج إلى الرأي إلا أن اجتماعهما على الخصومة والتكلم متعذر لأنه يلتبس على القاضي ويصير شغباً، فأما اجتماعهما على البيع فغير متعذر ، وظاهر ما في الكتاب أنه إذا خاصم أحدهما لم يشترط حضرة الآخر وهو قول العامة لعدم الفائدة بسماعها وهو ساكت ؛ كذا في الشرح. وبه ظهر أن ما ذكره ابن الملك من اشتراط الحضرة ضعيف ولكن لا يملك القبض إلا مع صاحبه؛ في الهداية. وفي الذخيرة وفي نوادر ابن سماعة عن أبي يوسف: رجل وكل رجلين بخصومة رجل في دار ادعاها وقبضها منه فخاصماه فيها ثم مات أحد الوكيلين قال أقبل من الحي البينة على الدار وأقضي بها للموكل ولا أقضي بدفع الدار إليه ولكن أجعل للوكيل الميت وكيلاً . مع هذا الحي ودفعت الدار إليهما، وكذا لو كان الوكيل واحداً فأقام البينة على الدار
قوله : (الأولى) لو وكلهما الخ قال الرملي : إنما لم يقيد المصنف الطلاق والعتاق بالمعين لأنهما عند الإطلاق ينصرفان إلى المعين لا إلى المبهم فتأمل
His statement: (And not) may one of the two agents act alone because the principal was satisfied with both opinions, not with the opinion of one; and the price, even if estimated, does not prevent the use of opinion in increasing and choosing the buyer. He left it general, so it includes when one is free and adult and the other a slave or a minor under guardianship, but it is restricted to when he appointed them with one statement. As for when his appointment of them is successive, then one of them may act alone because he was satisfied with the opinion of each one individually at the time of his appointment, so it does not change after that, unlike two executors (wasiyy). And it includes when one of them dies or loses his mind, then the other cannot act alone due to lack of satisfaction with his opinion alone. And if they were two executors and one died, the living one may not act except by order of the judge as in Wasaaya al-Khaaniyyah. ... His statement: (Except) in a lawsuit, for one of them may litigate alone because, even though it requires opinion, their coming together for litigation and speech is difficult because it confuses the judge and becomes riot. ... His statement: (The first) if he appointed them etc. al-Ramli said: The author did not restrict divorce and manumission to the specific because when they are unqualified they refer to the specific, not the ambiguous, so reflect.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explaining the ruling that one of two jointly appointed agents (wakilayn) cannot act alone. The reason given is that the principal (muwakkil) consented to the combined opinion of both agents, not to either individually. This applies even if the price is fixed, because discretion remains in choosing the actual buyer. The ruling covers cases where one agent is free and adult and the other a slave or minor, but is restricted to when both were appointed in a single statement. If they were appointed successively, each may act alone because the principal accepted each opinion separately. The distinction from executors (wasiyy) is noted: in wasiyyah, even if appointed separately, they become joint at death. The text also covers scenarios where one agent collects a debt before the second is appointed; the second cannot take it from the first because it is no longer a debt but a specific property. Optional voidability is discussed: if one agent sells in the presence of the other, the other's immediate approval validates it; if absent, Abu Hanifa holds that later approval does not validate, while Abu Yusuf disagrees. Sale between two agents is invalid according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but valid according to Abu Yusuf. The exception for lawsuits (khusumah) is explained: one agent may litigate alone because joint litigation confuses the judge and causes disorder, but he cannot take possession (qabd) alone except with his partner. The exception for divorce (talaq) and manumission without compensation (itaq bi-la badal) is noted; al-Ramli clarifies that these are understood to be specific (mu'ayyan) rather than ambiguous when unqualified.

Key Terms
وكيلين (wakīlayn): Two agents jointly appointed by the same principal for the same task in a single statement.
خصومة (khuṣūmah): A lawsuit or legal dispute; one of the exceptions where one joint agent may act alone.
بدل (badal): Compensation or consideration; the exception for divorce and manumission is specifically without compensation (bila badal).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT an exception to the general rule that one joint agent cannot act alone?
A) Lawsuit (khusumah)
B) Divorce without compensation (talaq bi-la badal)
C) Sale of property (bay')
D) Manumission without compensation (itaq bi-la badal)

Key Takeaway
In joint agency, the general rule is that both must act together, with only three exceptions: lawsuits, divorce without compensation, and manumission without compensation.

Exception for Lawsuit by One of Two Agents
Commentary on the exception allowing one agent to conduct lawsuits alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the exception to the rule that one of two agents cannot act alone, explain the rationale based on necessity, and apply the ruling to distinguish between lawsuit and possession.

Core Idea
One agent may sue alone to avoid court confusion, but cannot take possession without the co-agent.

In agency law, the general rule is that two agents must act together. However, practical necessity creates an exception for lawsuits. This lesson examines the rationale and scope of that exception, clarifying that while litigation can proceed alone, taking possession of the awarded property still requires joint action.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the general prohibition
Note the default rule: one of two agents cannot act alone in liability or transactions.
The passage starts by stating this rule, then lists exceptions.
2
Identify the exception for lawsuit
See that one agent may sue alone because simultaneous speech confuses the judge and causes commotion.
The Arabic term شغب (commotion) is the key practical reason.
3
Distinguish lawsuit from possession
While one agent can sue alone, taking possession of the awarded property requires both agents.
This is stated clearly: لا يملك القبض إلا مع صاحبه.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Ibn al-Malik required the other agent's presence during the lawsuit; the relied view rejects this as weak.
The passage declares Ibn al-Malik's view ضعيف (weak) based on necessity.
5
Examine the death scenario
If one agent dies after winning, a guardian is appointed for the deceased to join the survivor in taking possession.
This is from Abu Yusuf's ruling, showing the school's care for joint possession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يضمن ولا يتصرف أحد الوكيلين وحده إلا في خصومة وطلاق وعتاق بلا بدل. قوله: (إلا) في خصومة فإن لأحدهما أن يخاصم وحده لأنها وإن كانت تحتاج إلى الرأي إلا أن اجتماعهما على الخصومة والتكلم متعذر لأنه يلتبس على القاضي ويصير شغباً، فأما اجتماعهما على البيع فغير متعذر. وظاهر ما في الكتاب أنه إذا خاصم أحدهما لم يشترط حضرة الآخر وهو قول العامة لعدم الفائدة بسماعها وهو ساكت؛ كذا في الشرح. وبه ظهر أن ما ذكره ابن الملك من اشتراط الحضرة ضعيف ولكن لا يملك القبض إلا مع صاحبه؛ في الهداية. وفي الذخيرة وفي نوادر ابن سماعة عن أبي يوسف: رجل وكل رجلين بخصومة رجل في دار ادعاها وقبضها منه فخاصماه فيها ثم مات أحد الوكيلين قال أقبل من الحي البينة على الدار وأقضي بها للموكل ولا أقضي بدفع الدار إليه ولكن أجعل للوكيل الميت وكيلاً مع هذا الحي وأدفع الدار إليهما، وكذا لو كان الوكيل واحداً فأقام البينة على الدار وقضيت بها للموكل فمات هذا الوكيل قبل أن أدفعها إليه فإني أجعل له وكيلاً وأمر المقضى عليه بدفع الدار إليه ولا أتركها في يد الغاصب الذي قضيت عليه ا هـ.
One of two agents is not liable and cannot act alone except in lawsuit, divorce, and manumission without compensation. His statement: (except) in lawsuit, for one of them may sue alone, because even though lawsuit requires opinion, their simultaneous talking is impossible as it would confuse the judge and cause commotion. As for simultaneous sale, it is not impossible. The apparent meaning in the book is that when one sues, the presence of the other is not stipulated; this is the general view because hearing the litigation is pointless while the other is silent; thus in al-Sharh. It becomes clear that what Ibn al-Malik mentioned about stipulating presence is weak, but he cannot take possession except with his partner; in al-Hidaya. And in al-Dhakhira and in Nawadir Ibn Samama from Abu Yusuf: A man appointed two agents to sue a man over a house which he claimed and took possession of; the two agents sued and then one agent died. He said: I accept evidence from the living agent regarding the house and rule for the principal, but I do not order delivery of the house to him; rather, I appoint a guardian for the deceased agent along with the living one and deliver the house to both. Similarly, if there was one agent and he presented evidence for the house and I ruled for the principal, and then this agent died before I deliver it to him, I appoint a guardian for him and order the defendant to deliver the house to him, and I do not leave it in the hand of the usurper against whom I ruled.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq discusses the exception to the general rule that one of two agents cannot act alone. The exception for lawsuits is based on practical necessity: if both agents speak simultaneously, it confuses the judge and causes disorder. Therefore, one agent may sue alone without the other's presence. The text refutes Ibn al-Malik's view that presence is required, stating it is weak. However, the agent cannot take possession of the property awarded without his partner. The passage then quotes a specific scenario from Abu Yusuf in al-Dhakhira and Nawadir: if one of two agents in a lawsuit dies after winning the case, the living agent can proceed with a guardian appointed for the deceased, and the property is delivered to both. This shows the diligence to protect the rights of all parties.

Key Terms
خصومة (khusuumah): A lawsuit or legal proceeding in court.
شغب (shaghab): Commotion or disorder; here, the confusion caused by two agents speaking simultaneously in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is one of two agents allowed to sue alone?
A) Because lawsuit requires less skill than sale
B) Because simultaneous speech would confuse the judge and cause commotion
C) Because the other agent is always absent
D) Because lawsuits are not considered agency

Key Takeaway
One of two agents may sue alone to prevent court disorder, but cannot take possession of the awarded property without the co-agent.
