Exception for Return of a Deposit by One of Two Agents
Commentary on the exception for returning a deposit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that one of two agents may alone return a deposit (and similar items) but cannot take possession alone, and explain the underlying criterion of 'requiring deliberation'.

Core Idea
One agent may return a deposit alone because it needs no thought; only joint possession safeguards the principal's interest.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies an important exception in agency law: when two agents are appointed to handle a deposit, returning it does not require both. The commentary expands on the base text by explaining the reasoning and refuting a potential objection about partial liability. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying rules of joint agency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exception and its scope
The matn states that one of two agents may return a deposit. Note that the passage clarifies this applies also to returning borrowed items, usurped property, and property from a void sale.
The exception is not limited to deposits.
2
Analyze the underlying reason (illa)
The reason given: 'because it does not require deliberation' (la yahtaj ila al-ra'y). Understand that returning is a simple mechanical act, unlike assessing value or making decisions.
Deliberation would require joint judgment.
3
Distinguish from taking possession (qabd)
The passage restricts the exception to returning. Taking possession alone is invalid because it involves deliberation and the principal values double protection. One agent who takes possession alone becomes liable for the entire deposit.
Double protection is a valid interest.
4
Address the hypothetical objection
An objector might argue that each agent is authorized to take half, so a single taker should guarantee only half. The response: when alone, he is not authorized to take any part, so the entire act is unauthorized.
Authority is joint, not several.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ورد وديعة) لأنه مما لا يحتاج إلى الرأي فرد أحدهما كردهما. ولو قال ورد عين لكان أولى فإنه لا فرق بين رد الوديعة والعارية والمغصوب والمبيع فاسداً كما صرح به في الخلاصة. وقيد بالرد احترازاً عن الاسترداد فليس لأحدهما القبض بدون صاحبه لإمكان اجتماعهما وللموكل فيه غرض صحيح لأن حفظ اثنين ليس كحفظ واحد فإذا قبضه أحدهما ضمن كله لأنه قبض بغير إذن المالك. فإن قيل: ينبغي أن يضمن النصف لأن كل واحد منهما مأمور بقبض النصف قلنا: ذلك حال الانفراد فغير مأمور بقبض شيء منه؛ كذا في السراج الوهاج.
His statement: (and returning a deposit) because it does not require deliberation, so the return by one is like the return by both. And if he said 'returning an asset' it would be more appropriate, as there is no difference between returning a deposit, a borrowed item, a usurped item, and a void sale, as stated in al-Khulasa. He restricted with 'return' to exclude reclaiming; for one of them cannot take possession without the other because it is possible for them to come together, and the principal has a valid interest in that; the protection by two is not like protection by one. So if one of them takes possession, he guarantees the whole because he took possession without the owner's permission. If it is said: he should guarantee half because each is ordered to take half, we say: that is in the case of being alone, so he is not ordered to take any of it; thus in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage discusses the exception for returning a deposit (wadi'ah) by one of two agents. Since returning a deposit is a simple action requiring no deliberation, one agent may return it alone, and it is as effective as if both returned it. The same ruling applies to returning borrowed items, usurped property, or property from a void sale. However, the exception is limited to returning; one agent cannot take possession (i.e., receive) a deposit without the other agent, because taking possession requires deliberation and the principal has an interest in having two persons safeguard the property. If one agent takes possession alone, he becomes liable (guarantor) for the entire deposit because he acted without the owner's permission. The passage refutes an objection that he should only guarantee half by explaining that each agent is only authorized to take possession jointly, not individually.

Key Terms
وديعة (wadi'ah): A deposit; property entrusted to someone for safekeeping.
رد (radd): Return; here specifically the act of giving back a deposit to its owner.
قبض (qabd): Taking possession; receiving the deposit from the owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two agents are appointed to handle a deposit, which action can ONE of them perform alone?
A) Taking the deposit from the owner
B) Returning the deposit to the owner
C) Both taking and returning the deposit
D) Neither taking nor returning the deposit

Key Takeaway
One of two agents may alone return a deposit because it requires no deliberation, but cannot take possession alone because that requires joint judgment and safeguards the principal's interest.

When One of Two Agents Can Act Alone: Returning Deposits and Similar Acts
Explains ruling for two agents in returning deposits and extends by qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between acts that one of two joint agents can perform alone versus those requiring both, applying the Hanafi criterion of need for opinion and the principal's interest in dual guardianship.

Core Idea
Returning a deposit needs no opinion, so one agent suffices; reclaiming needs both for preservation.

When a principal appoints two agents for a task, can one act alone? The answer depends on whether the act requires independent judgment (ra'y) or not. This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq explains the rule for returning deposits and extends it by qiyas to similar acts like returning loans or usurped items, while also highlighting exceptions like reclaiming and the intra-Hanafi difference on gifts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
Find the statement that one of two agents can return a deposit alone. Note the reason: it requires no opinion (lā yaḥtāju ilā al-ra'y).
This is the asl (original case) for the qiyas.
2
Trace the qiyas extension
The commentator extends the same ruling to returning a loan (ʿāriya), usurped item (maghsūb), and void sale item (mabīʿ fāsid). Identify the illa (effective cause): absence of need for opinion.
This is a clear qiyas jaliy (manifest analogy).
3
Distinguish reclaiming from returning
Reclaiming (istirdād) requires both agents because it involves preservation and the principal's interest in dual guardianship. One agent acting alone would be liable.
The distinction hinges on the principal's valid purpose (gharaḍ ṣaḥīḥ).
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on gifts
For delivering a gift (hiba) to a specified donee, all agree one agent may act alone. For an unspecified donee, Abū Ḥanīfa and Muḥammad require both; Abū Yūsuf permits one. The preferred view requires both.
This shows the boundary of the general rule: gifts are like returning only when the donee is specified.
5
Compile the list of exceptions
The passage provides a comprehensive list of acts where one agent may act alone: litigation, specified emancipation, specified divorce without compensation, acts suspended on both wills, tadbīr, returning deposits/loans/usurped items/void items, delivery, gift, and debt payment. Other appointments (general agency, wasiyya, etc.) require both.
This list is the thamarah (practical output) of the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ورد وديعة) لأنه مما لا يحتاج إلى الرأي فرد أحدهما كردهما، ولو قال ورد عين لكان أولى فإنه لا فرق بين رد الوديعة والعارية والمغصوب والمبيع فاسداً كما صرح به في الخلاصة . وقيد بالرد احترازاً . عن الاسترداد فليس لأحدهما القبض بدون صاحبه لإمكان اجتماعهما وللموكل فيه غرض صحيح لأن حفظ اثنين ليس كحفظ واحد فإذا قبضه أحدهما ضمن كله لأنه قبض بغير إذن المالك. فإن قيل : ينبغي أن يضمن النصف لأن كل واحد منهما مأمور بقبض النصف قلنا : ذلك حال الانفراد فغير مأمور بقبض شيء منه ؛ كذا في السراج الوهاج . مع إذن صاحبه، وأما في (وفي الخانية رجل له الخ لا مدخل له في هذا المحل تأمل ففيهما يكون تفويضاً الخ) أي في المسألتين الثانية والثالثة ثم حيث كانا تمليكاً أو تعليقاً لم يكونا داخلين في كلام المصنف لأن كلامه في الوكيلين بالطلاق والعتاق فلا يصح الاستثناء، واستثناء الزيلعي لهما منقطع بمعنى لكن بدليل ما ذکر نبه عليه الرملي قوله : (الرابعة لو قال الخ قال الرملي : إنما لم يستثن المصنف الرابعة لعدم دخولها لأن فيها زيادة وهي شرط اجتماعهما صريحاً فتأمل. وكذلك لم يسثن الخامس لعارض النهي عن الإنفراد . قوله : (فليس لأحدهما القبض بدون صاحبه أي بدون إذن صاحبه كما صرح به في الذخيرة ورد وديعة وقضاء دين ولا يوكل إلا بإذن أو اعمل برأيك فإن وكل بلا إذن الموكل قوله : (وقضاء الدين) فهو كرد الوديعة واقتضاؤه فهو كاستردادها، ولم يذكر المؤلف الهبة في المستثنيات وفي الولوالجية وكلهما الواهب في تسليم الهبة للموهوب له فلأحدهما أن ينفرد وإذا وكلهما الموهوب له في قبضها من الواهب فليس لأحدهما الانفراد، فالأول كرد الوديعة والثاني كاستردادها وفي الخانية من باب الوصي ولو وكل رجل رجلين بأن يهبا هذه العين ولم يعين الموهوب له عندهما لا ينفرد أحدهما بذلك، وعند أبي يوسف ينفرد، وإن عين الموهوب له ينفرد أحدهما عند الكل ا هـ . فلو زاد المصنف الهبة للمعين لكان أولى وعبارة المجمع هكذا : وإذا وكل اثنين لم ينفرد أحدهما في كل تمليك أو عقد فيه بدل ا هـ . ويرد عليه الهبة لمعين فإنها تمليك وله الانفراد ويرد عليه استرداد العين والاقتضاء فإنه لا ينفرد فيهما ولا تمليك ولا عقد كما ورد على الكنز قضاء الدين ورد ما عدا الوديعة والهبة للمعين، والأولى أن يقال لا ينفرد أحدهما إلا في خصومة وعتق معين وطلاق معينة بلا بدل وتعلق بمشيئتهما وتدبير ورد وديعة وعارية ومغصوب ومبيع فاسد وتسليم وهبة وقضاء الدين، ثم اعلم أن الوكالة والوصايا والمضاربة والقضاء والتولية على الوقف، سواء فليس لأحدهما الانفراد، والأولان في الكتاب والمضاربة في السراج الوهاج وقدمنا حكم القاضيين في القضاء والناظر، إما وكيل أو وصي فلا ينفرد أحدهما
His statement: (and returning a deposit) because it does not require opinion, so the return by one is like the return by both. If he had said 'return of a specific item' it would be better, for there is no difference between returning a deposit, a loan, a usurped item, and a void sale item, as stated in al-Khulāṣa. He restricted to 'return' to exclude reclaiming, so one of them may not take possession without his partner because it is possible for them to gather, and the principal has a valid interest in having two guardians. If one takes possession, he guarantees the whole because he takes without the owner's permission. If it is said: he should guarantee half because each is ordered to take half, we say: that is in the state of isolation, so he is not ordered to take anything. ... His statement: (and paying a debt) so it is like returning a deposit, and its collection is like reclaiming it. The author did not mention gifts among the exceptions. ... The best position is that one of them cannot act alone except in litigation, specified emancipation, specified divorce without compensation, suspension on their wish, tadbir, returning a deposit, loan, usurped item, void sale item, delivery, gift, and paying a debt. Then know that agency, wills, mudaraba, judgeship, and supervision of waqf are equal – one of them cannot act alone.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage comments on the matn statement 'wa radd wadīʿa' (and returning a deposit). The commentator explains that one of two agents can return a deposit alone because no deliberation is needed. This also applies to returning a loan (ʿāriya), a usurped item (maghsūb), and an unsound sale item (mabīʿ fāsid). However, reclaiming the deposit (istirdād) requires both agents, as the principal's interest in dual guardianship is valid (since two people provide better security). If one agent takes possession alone, he is liable for the whole item. The same rule applies to paying a debt (qaḍāʾ dayn) – paying is like returning, but collecting is like reclaiming. The passage then discusses gifts (hiba): if the donor appoints two agents to deliver a gift, one can act alone (like returning a deposit), but if the donee appoints them to claim it, they must act together (like reclaiming). There is an ikhtilāf regarding unspecified gifts: according to Abū Yūsuf, one agent can act alone, but the preferred view is that it requires both. The commentator concludes with a summary list of actions where one agent may act alone, including litigation, specified ʿitāq and ṭalāq without consideration, acts suspended on both wills, tadbīr, returning deposits/loans/usurped items/void sale items, delivery, gift, and debt payment. Other appointments like general agency, wasiyya, mudāraba, judgeship, and waqf supervision require both agents.

Key Terms
رد الوديعة (radd al-wadīʿa): Returning a deposit to its owner or his representative.
استرداد (istirdād): Reclaiming a deposit or taking possession back from the depositary.
هبة (hiba): Gift – a proprietary transfer without consideration.
قضاء الدين (qaḍāʾ al-dayn): Payment of a debt.
اقتضاء (iqtiḍāʾ): Collection of a debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following acts may one of two joint agents perform alone?
A) Reclaiming a deposit (istirdād)
B) Collecting a debt (iqtiḍāʾ)
C) Returning a loan (radd al-ʿāriya)
D) Selling a house (tamlīk bi-ʿiwaḍ)

Key Takeaway
One of two joint agents may act alone only in acts requiring no opinion, such as returning deposits, loans, usurped items, and specified gifts; reclaiming and general tamlīk require both.

Exceptions to Joint Agency: When One Agent May Act Alone
Listing exceptions to joint agency rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and state the exceptions to the rule that two joint agents must act together, and distinguish acts where one agent may act alone from those where both are required.

Core Idea
One of two joint agents may act alone in specific acts primarily not tamlik or contracts with consideration.

In Hanafi law, when a principal appoints two agents jointly for a single task, the default rule is that both must act together. However, there are well-recognized exceptions where one agent can act independently. This lesson parses those exceptions as enumerated in Al-Bahr al-Raiq, clarifying the underlying principle and addressing nuances like gifts and debt repayment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Start with the default: two joint agents must act together. This is the baseline.
The exceptions are deviations from this baseline.
2
List the exceptions
Read through the passage and compile the acts where one agent may act alone: litigation, manumission, divorce without consideration, matters contingent on both wills, tadbir, returning deposits/loans/usurped items/void sales, delivery, gift, repayment of debt.
Memorize the list; note it is exhaustive per the preferred formulation.
3
Understand the underlying criterion
Notice that most exceptions are not tamlik (transfer of ownership) or contracts with a consideration (like sale). Acts that are mere delivery or cancellation of rights permit solo action.
Compare: sale (requires both) vs. repayment of debt (one may act).
4
Analyze the gift sub-case
Distinguish between donor appointing agents to give (one may act alone, like returning a deposit) and donee appointing agents to receive (both required, like retrieving a deposit).
This illustrates that the agent's role (giving vs. receiving) matters.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf holds that one agent may act alone even when giving a gift to an unspecified donee, contrary to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. If the donee is specified, all agree one may act alone.
The author subtly endorses the majority view by suggesting a better formulation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وقضاء الدين) فهو كرد الوديعة واقتضاؤه فهو كاستردادها، ولم يذكر المؤلف الهبة في المستثنيات وفي الولوالجية وكلهما الواهب في تسليم الهبة للموهوب له فلأحدهما أن ينفرد وإذا وكلهما الموهوب له في قبضها من الواهب فليس لأحدهما الانفراد، فالأول كرد الوديعة والثاني كاستردادها وفي الخانية من باب الوصي ولو وكل رجل رجلين بأن يهبا هذه العين ولم يعين الموهوب له عندهما لا ينفرد أحدهما بذلك، وعند أبي يوسف ينفرد، وإن عين الموهوب له ينفرد أحدهما عند الكل ا هـ . فلو زاد المصنف الهبة للمعين لكان أولى وعبارة المجمع هكذا : وإذا وكل اثنين لم ينفرد أحدهما في كل تمليك أو عقد فيه بدل ا هـ . ويرد عليه الهبة لمعين فإنها تمليك وله الانفراد ويرد عليه استرداد العين والاقتضاء فإنه لا ينفرد فيهما ولا تمليك ولا عقد كما ورد على الكنز قضاء الدين ورد ما عدا الوديعة والهبة للمعين، والأولى أن يقال لا ينفرد أحدهما إلا في خصومة وعتق معين وطلاق معينة بلا بدل وتعلق بمشيئتهما وتدبير ورد وديعة وعارية ومغصوب ومبيع فاسد وتسليم وهبة وقضاء الدين.
His statement: (and repayment of debt) it is like returning a deposit, and demanding it is like retrieving it. The author did not mention gift among the exceptions. In al-Wilwaljiyyah: if the donor appoints two agents to deliver a gift to the donee, one of them may act alone. If the donee appoints them to receive it from the donor, one may not act alone. The first is like returning a deposit, the second like retrieving it. In al-Khaniyyah from the chapter on the executor: if a man appoints two men to give a specific item as a gift without specifying the donee, in their view one may not act alone; according to Abu Yusuf, he may. If the donee is specified, one may act alone according to all. So if the author had added 'gift to a specified person' it would have been better. The wording of al-Majma' is: when two are appointed, one may not act alone in any act of transfer of ownership or contract involving a consideration. This is objected to by the case of a gift to a specified person, for it is a transfer and one may act alone; and by the case of retrieving an item and demanding a debt, for one may not act alone in them, and they are neither a transfer nor a contract, just as was objected to al-Kanz regarding 'repayment of debt' and 'returning other than a deposit' and 'gift to a specified person'. The better formulation is: one of two may not act alone except in litigation, manumission of a specific slave, divorce of a specific wife without consideration, matters contingent on both their wills, tadbir, returning a deposit, a loan, a usurped item, a void sale, delivery, gift, and repayment of debt.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on joint agency. The general rule is that when two agents are appointed together for a task, neither may act alone without the other's concurrence. However, the author lists exceptions where one may act independently. These exceptions include litigation, manumission of a specific slave, divorce of a specific wife without financial consideration, matters that are contingent on both their wills (e.g., a will that requires both to execute), tadbir (declaring a slave free after the master's death), returning items held as deposit, loan, usurped property, items sold in a void sale, delivery of goods, gift, and repayment of debt. The passage discusses nuances: e.g., for gifts, if the donor appoints two agents to hand over the gift, one can act alone (like returning a deposit), but if the donee appoints them to receive it, they cannot act alone (like retrieving a deposit). There is also an ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school: according to Abu Yusuf, if the donee is not specified, one may act alone; otherwise, both views agree one may act alone. The author suggests a more precise formulation to avoid objections.

Key Terms
الوكيلان (al-wakīlān): Two agents appointed jointly for a single task; neither may act alone except in specified exceptions.
الانفراد (al-infirād): One agent acting alone without the other joint agent.
التمليك (al-tamlīk): Transfer of ownership, such as gift or sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT an exception where one of two joint agents may act alone?
A) Returning a deposit
B) Selling a house for a price
C) Manumitting a specific slave
D) Repaying a debt

Key Takeaway
One of two joint agents may act alone in specific acts such as returning deposits, gifts to specified persons, and repaying debts, but not in acts of tamlik or contracts with consideration.

Sub-delegation by an Agent: Prohibition and Exceptions
Stating the rule and sub-rulings on sub-delegation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general Hanafi ruling on sub-delegation by an agent and identify the two conditions that make it valid.

Core Idea
A principal trusts the agent's judgment, not another's — delegation without permission is not allowed.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the Hanafi position on sub-delegation (tawkīl al-wakīl). The general rule is that an agent cannot appoint another person to act in his place unless the principal has explicitly permitted it or given such broad authority as 'اعمل برأيك' (act on your own judgment). The reasoning is that the principal is satisfied with the agent's personal judgment, and people differ in opinions. After stating the rule, the passage explores several practical consequences, including the effect on debt payment and liability, showing how the principle applies consistently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Find the statement that prohibits sub-delegation without permission. Note the exact phrasing: 'ولا يوكل إلا بإذن أو اعمل برأيك'.
The rule is negative: the agent may not delegate except with permission or a broad directive.
2
Understand the reason (talīl)
The text gives the reason: 'لأنه فوض إليه التصرف دون التوكيل به' — the principal authorized the agent to act, not to appoint a delegate. Trust is personal.
The underlying principle is that agency is based on personal trust; delegation breaks that trust unless the principal agrees.
3
Identify the exceptions
Two exceptions: 1) explicit permission (إذن) from the principal, 2) the principal says 'اعمل برأيك' (act on your own judgment), which implies full authority to do what the agent deems appropriate, including delegation.
Both exceptions establish the principal's consent to the agent using his own discretion.
4
Apply the rule to a specific scenario
Examine the scenario of debt collection: if an agent without permission appoints a sub-agent to receive payment, the payment reaches the first agent if the sub-agent collects; otherwise the debtor is not discharged unless the sub-agent is from the agent's household.
This shows that unauthorized sub-delegation does not bind the principal; the debtor remains liable until the principal or first agent actually receives the money.
5
Contrast with valid sub-delegation
When sub-delegation is valid (with permission or broad authority), the sub-agent becomes directly the principal's agent: the first agent cannot dismiss him, and the sub-agent's agency does not end with the first agent's death, only with the principal's death.
This highlights that valid sub-delegation creates a direct agency relationship between the principal and the sub-agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ولا يوكل إلا بإذن أو اعمل برأيك) لأنه فوض إليه التصرف دون التوكيل به وهذا لأنه رضي برأيه والناس مختلفون في الآراء إلا أن يأذن له الموكل لوجود الرضا أو يقول له برأيك لإطلاق التفويض إلى رأيه. وإذا وكل الوكيل بالقبض بلا إذن فدفع له المديون فإنه وصل إلى الوكيل الأول بريء وإلا فإن وكل من في عياله برئ وإلا لا، فإن هلك المال في يد الثاني كان للغريم تضمينه وللثاني الرجوع على الوكيل الأوّل وتمامه في الذخيرة من الفصل الثاني. وإذا وكل بإذن أو تفويض كان الثاني وكيلاً عن الموكل حتى لا يملك الأول عزله ولا ينعزل بموته وينعزلان بموت الأول وقد مر نظيره في أدب القاضي وفي الخلاصة: رجل وكل رجلاً ببيع شيء وشرائه وقال له اصنع ما شئت فوكل الوكيل رجلاً بذلك ثم مات الوكيل الأعلى فالوكيل الأسفل على وكالته، ولو أخرجه الوكيل الذي وكله جاز، ولو أخرجه الموكل كان إخراجه جائزاً أيضاً، عن نص محمد في الأصل
His statement: (and he may not delegate except with permission or [if the principal says] 'act on your own judgment') because he was authorized to act directly, not to appoint a delegate. This is because the principal was satisfied with his judgment, and people differ in opinions. Unless the principal grants permission, because then satisfaction exists, or says 'act on your own judgment', because that gives full authority to his opinion. If the agent appoints someone to receive a debt without permission, and the debtor pays the sub-agent, it reaches the first agent and the debtor is discharged. If not [i.e., the debtor pays someone else?], and if he appointed someone from his household, he is discharged; otherwise not. If the property perishes in the sub-agent's hands, the creditor has the right to hold the sub-agent liable, and the sub-agent can recourse against the first agent. The complete details are in al-Dhakhira from the second chapter. If he appoints with permission or [the principal] delegated [authority], the second becomes an agent of the principal, so that the first may not dismiss him, and he is not dismissed by the death of the first; both are dismissed by the death of the first. Its parallel was mentioned in Adab al-Qadi. In al-Khulasah: A man appointed an agent to sell something and buy it, and said to him 'do whatever you wish'. The agent appointed another man for that, then the higher agent died. The lower agent remains on his agency. If the agent who appointed him dismisses him, it is permissible. If the principal dismisses him, his dismissal is also permissible, according to the text of Muhammad in al-Asl.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on sub-delegation by an agent. The general rule is that an authorized agent (wakil) cannot appoint another agent (sub-agent) without the principal's permission. The reason is that the principal trusts the agent's own judgment; if the agent delegates, that trust is not extended to another. However, there are two exceptions: if the principal explicitly permits sub-delegation, or if the principal says 'اعمل برأيك' (act on your own judgment), which implies broad authority to do whatever the agent deems appropriate, including delegation. The passage then discusses several sub-rulings: (1) If an agent without permission appoints someone to collect a debt, and the debtor pays the sub-agent, the debtor is discharged; but if the sub-agent is not from the agent's household, the debtor is not discharged unless the payment reaches the agent. (2) If the sub-agent is appointed with permission or broad authority, the sub-agent becomes the principal's agent, so the original agent cannot dismiss him, and the sub-agent's agency does not end with the original agent's death but ends with the principal's death. (3) If the principal says 'اصنع ما شئت' (do whatever you wish), the agent can sub-delegate and can also dismiss the sub-agent, as per Imam Muhammad's text. The passage clarifies the scope of authority and the effects of sub-delegation.

Key Terms
توكيل الوكيل (tawkīl al-wakīl): The act of an agent appointing another person to act in his place in the agency.
اعمل برأيك (iʿmal bira'yik): Principal's statement granting the agent authority to exercise his own judgment, which permits sub-delegation.
ولي (walī): Not directly used here; instead 'وكيل' (agent).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if an agent delegates without permission but with broad authority (e.g., 'act on your own judgment'), what is the status of the sub-agent's relationship with the principal?
A) The sub-agent is the agent of the original agent only.
B) The sub-agent becomes the agent of the principal directly.
C) The sub-agent is considered a servant of the principal.
D) The sub-agent has no relationship with the principal.

Key Takeaway
An agent cannot sub-delegate without the principal's permission or a grant of broad authority; otherwise, the sub-agent's acts are suspended.

Ratification of Unauthorized Acts: Presence and Ijazah
Ruling on validity of contracts by unauthorized agents or third parties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a contract by an unauthorized agent or third party becomes valid in the Hanafi school and apply the rule of ratification (ijazah) to scenarios of presence and absence.

Core Idea
A contract without authorization is valid if the principal's judgment is present, either in person or by later ratification.

In Hanafi fiqh, an act performed by an agent without prior permission (fuduli) or by a complete stranger is not automatically void. Its validity depends on the principal's judgment (ra'y). This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains when such acts are considered sound, focusing on the principal's presence at the time of contract and the effect of subsequent ratification. Understanding this ruling is essential for navigating agency relationships and the concept of ijazah (ratification) in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is an unauthorized contract by an agent (without permission) or a third party (ajnabi). The ruling: it is valid (sahih) if the principal is present at the time of contract or later ratifies it. If the principal is absent and no ratification occurs, it is not valid.
The term 'فعل' (act) includes sale and other contracts.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is based on the principle that the intended element (المقصود) is the principal's judgment (رأيه). The text states: 'لأن المقصود رأيه وقد حضر' – 'because the intended element is his judgment, and he was present.'
No direct Quran or hadith is cited; the reasoning is qiyas on the concept of judgment being the foundation of authorization.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The contract is linked to the principal's will. When the principal's judgment is manifest – either through presence (and silence) or through explicit ratification – the contract becomes sound. Conversely, absence of judgment (ghaybah) without later ratification leaves the contract invalid.
Presence alone suffices as tacit consent, as per the Hanafi view.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (dispute)
The text mentions a discussion about the rights (huquq) of the contract – specifically, to whom they revert. The correct view (الصحيح) is that they revert to the second (the sub‑agent) because he is the actual contracting party. This indicates an intra‑school discussion.
The dispute may relate to whether the original agent or the sub‑agent is entitled to commissions or obligations.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preponderance)
The author endorses the view that rights belong to the sub‑agent, implying that the contract's effects attach to the actual contracting party. This is the relied‑upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school.
The tarjih authority is likely al‑Marghinani in al‑Hidayah, as noted in the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإن وكل بلا إذن الموكل فعقد بحضرته أو باع أجنبي فأجاز صح) لأن المقصود رأيه وقد حضر وتكلموا في حقوقه والصحيح رجوعها إلى الثاني لأنه هو العاقد وإن عقد بغيبته لم يجز لأنه فات رأيه إلا أن يبلغه فأجازه لأنه حضر رأيه، وكذا إذا باع غير الوكيل فبلغه فأجازه ولو قدر الأوّل الثمن للثاني فعقد بغيبته يجوز لأن الرأي يحتاج إليه لتقدير الثمن ... صح
His statement: (If he appoints without the principal's permission, and he contracts in his presence, or a stranger sells and he [the principal] ratifies, it is valid) because the intended element is his judgment, and he was present. They discussed the rights, and the correct view is that they revert to the second (the sub-agent) because he is the one who contracted. If he contracted in his absence, it is not valid because his judgment was absent, unless he is informed and ratifies it, because then his judgment is present. Similarly, if someone other than the agent sells and he is informed and ratifies it. And if the first set the price for the second, and he contracts in his absence, it is valid because judgment is needed for estimating the price.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage explains the rule of ratification (ijazah) in the context of unauthorized contracts by an agent or a stranger. The text clarifies that if an agent contracts without permission but in the presence of the principal, and the principal does not object, or if a third party (ajnabi) sells and the principal subsequently ratifies, the contract becomes valid. The reason is that the principal's judgment (ra'y) was present or later formed. The passage also contrasts the case where the contract occurs in the principal's absence: then it is not valid unless the principal later ratifies it, because his judgment was not available at the time of contracting. An exception is made if the original agent had set a price for the sub-agent; then even if the sub-agent contracts in the principal's absence, it is valid because the principal's judgment was exercised in setting the price. This shows that the key factor is the principal's judgment, and once it is manifested, the contract becomes valid.

Key Terms
الإجازة (al-ijāzah): Ratification by the principal of a contract made without his prior authorization.
الفضولي (al-fuḍūlī): A person who acts on behalf of another without authorization; the agent who sub-delegates without permission is considered a fuduli in that act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
An agent without permission sells the principal's car in the principal's presence. The principal remains silent. What is the ruling?
A) The sale is invalid because the agent had no permission
B) The sale is valid because the principal's presence and silence imply consent
C) The sale is suspended until the principal expressly ratifies
D) The buyer must return the car

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the validity of an unauthorized contract hinges on the principal's judgment (ra'y), which can be expressed through presence, silence, or later ratification.

Agent's Authority to Delegate and Revoke Sub-agents
Explains when an agent can delegate and whether the first agent can revoke the sub-agent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Hanafi rulings on delegation and revocation based on the principal's wording (e.g., 'act according to your opinion' vs 'do whatever you wish') and apply them to scenarios involving contractual rights and personal acts.

Core Idea
The agent's power to delegate and revoke depends on the principal's wording: broad discretion allows revocation, while trust-based appointment prevents it.

This lesson examines the commentary on al-Hidayah regarding an agent's authority to appoint a sub-agent and then revoke that sub-agent. The text presents key distinctions in wording that determine the outcome, as well as exceptions for matters where the agent acts as principal, such as sale and purchase. Understanding these nuances is crucial for correctly applying Hanafi agency law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principal's Wording
Analyze the exact words used by the principal when delegating authority. The passage distinguishes between 'اصنع ما شئت' (do whatever you wish) and 'اعمل برأيك' (act according to your opinion). Each triggers different rulings on revocation.
The wording 'اعمل برأيك' implies full trust in the agent's judgment, including choice of sub-agent.
2
Understand the Effect on Revocation
With 'اصنع ما شئت', the agent can revoke the sub-agent. With 'اعمل برأيك', he cannot. The reasoning is that when the principal relies on the agent's opinion, revoking the sub-agent contradicts that trust.
Al-Khaniyyah reasons that delegation implies satisfaction with the sub-agent's action.
3
Examine the 'وكل فلاناً' Rule
If the principal says 'appoint so-and-so', the agent cannot revoke him unless the principal used permissive language like 'if you wish' or 'whoever you wish'.
The default is that the agent does not have authority to revoke a sub-agent explicitly appointed by the principal.
4
Distinguish Contractual vs Personal Acts
In matters where rights revert to the agent (e.g., sale, purchase), the agent can delegate without permission because he is principal in these actions. For personal acts like divorce or manumission, which often involve oaths, the sub-agent cannot replace the agent.
The passage gives examples: a sub-agent can execute sale/purchase but not free a slave or divorce a wife on behalf of the agent.
5
Apply Special Cases
Consider exceptions: an agent delegated to collect a debt from dependents can delegate because the sub-agent's hand is like his hand; zakat payment can be delegated without suspension; but buying a sacrifice by a sub-agent requires ratification of the first agent.
These cases illustrate the principle that some delegations are valid without permission while others are suspended.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الهداية من في شئت سواء كان الوكيل الأول حياً أو ميتاً ا هـ. فقد صحح عزل الوكيل لوكيله وهو مخالف لما الثاني صار وكيل الموكل فلا يملك الوكيل عزله إلا أن يفرق بين قوله اصنع ما شئت فيملك عزله وبين قوله اعمل برأيك فلا يملك عزله والفرق ظاهر. وعلل في الخانية بأنه لما فوضه إلى صنعه فقد رضي بصنعه وعزله من صنعه وفيها : إذا وكل ثم قال للوكيل وكل فلاناً فإن الوكيل لا يملك عزله إلا إذا قال له وكل فلاناً إن شئت أو وكل من فيملك عزله ا هـ . والمراد لا يوكل فيما وكل فيه فيخرج التوكيل بحقوق العقد فيما ترجع الحقوق فيه إلى الوكيل فله التوكيل بلا إذن لكونه أصيلاً فيها، ولذا لا يملك الموكل نهيه عنها وصح توكيل الموكل كما قدمناه. وقيد بقوله اعمل برأيك احترازاً عن قوله ما صنعت من شيء فهو جائز . قال في القنية : قال للوكيل ما صنعت من شيء فهو جائز من بيع أو شراء أو عتق عبده أو طلاق امرأته فوكل هذا الوكيل غيره بعتق عبد موكله أو طلاق امرأته ففعل لا ينفذ لأن هذا مما يحلف به فلا يقوم غيره مقامه بخلاف البيع والشراء فإنه لا يحلف بهما فقام غيره مقامه ا هـ. وخرج عن قوله لا يوكل إلا بإذن أو اعمل برأيك ما لو وكل الوكيل بقبض الدين من في عياله فدفع المديون إليه فإنه يبرأ لأن يده كيده؛ ذكره الشارح في السرقة. وفي وكالة الخزانة : وما لو وكل الوكيل بدفع الزكاة ثم وثم فدفع الآخر جاز ولا يتوقف كما في أضحية الخانية. وذكر قبله : رجل وكل غيره بشراء أضحية فوكل الوكيل غيره ثم وثم فاشترى الآخر يكون موقوفاً على إجازة الأوّل إن أجاز جاز وإلا فلا ا هـ. وما إذا قدر الوكيل لوكيله الثمن كما سيأتي .
From al-Hidayah: '... whoever you wish, whether the first agent is alive or dead.' So he confirmed the agent's revocation of his sub-agent, which contradicts what follows: the second becomes the agent of the principal, so the agent cannot revoke him unless there is a distinction between saying 'do whatever you wish'—in which case he can revoke—and saying 'act according to your opinion'—in which case he cannot revoke. The difference is clear. It is reasoned in al-Khaniyyah that when he delegated the action to him, he was satisfied with his action, and revoking him is part of his action. In it: If he appoints an agent and then says to the agent 'appoint so-and-so', the agent cannot revoke him unless he said 'appoint so-and-so if you wish' or 'appoint whoever you wish'—then he can revoke. The meaning is: he does not delegate in what he was delegated; exceptions are delegation of contractual rights where rights revert to the agent, so he may delegate without permission because he is principal in them, and therefore the principal cannot forbid him from them, and the principal's delegation is valid as we presented. He restricted 'act according to your opinion' to exclude the case where he says 'whatever you do is permissible.' He said in al-Quniya: If he said to the agent 'whatever you do is permissible' regarding sale, purchase, freeing his slave, or divorcing his wife, and this agent delegates another to free the principal's slave or divorce his wife, it is not effective because these involve oaths, so another cannot take his place, unlike sale and purchase which do not involve oaths, so another can take his place. Excluded from his saying 'do not delegate except with permission' or 'act according to your opinion' is the case where the agent is delegated to collect a debt from his dependents; if the debtor pays him, he is discharged because his hand is like his hand; the commentator mentioned this in al-Sariqa. In Wakalat al-Khazanah: The case where the agent delegates payment of zakat, and then another pays, it is valid and not suspended, as in the sacrifice of al-Khaniyyah. And before it: A man delegated another to purchase a sacrifice, and the agent delegated another, and then the other bought; it is suspended on the first's ratification: if he ratifies, it is valid; otherwise no. And the case where the agent sets the price for his sub-agent will come later.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage from the commentary discusses the nuanced rules of delegation in agency contracts. The author begins by quoting al-Hidayah that if the principal said to the agent 'do whatever you wish', the agent can revoke the sub-agent, but if he said 'act according to your opinion', he cannot revoke. The difference is that 'act according to your opinion' implies full trust in the agent's judgment, including the choice of sub-agent, while 'do whatever you wish' leaves room for the agent to change his mind. The commentary then cites al-Khaniyyah for the reasoning: the agent's delegation means he is satisfied with the sub-agent's action, so revoking him would contradict that satisfaction. Further, if the principal says 'appoint so-and-so as agent', the agent cannot revoke him unless the principal used permissive language like 'if you wish'. The passage also distinguishes between cases where the sub-agent acts in matters involving rights that revert to the agent (like sale and purchase) versus matters involving personal acts like divorce or freeing a slave, where the sub-agent cannot replace the agent because these acts often involve oaths or personal choice. Special cases: an agent delegated to collect a debt from family members can delegate because the sub-agent's hand is like his hand; paying zakat can be delegated without suspension; but buying a sacrifice by a sub-agent requires ratification of the first agent. This set of rulings provides a comprehensive view of when substitution in agency is valid and under what conditions the agent retains control.

Key Terms
الوكيل (al-wakīl): The person appointed by the principal (الموكل) to act on his behalf in a specific transaction.
التوكيل (al-tawkīl): The act of appointing another person to act as one's agent, or the act of an agent appointing a sub-agent.
اصنع ما شئت (iṣnaʿ mā shiʾta): An expression by the principal giving the agent broad discretion, including the ability to delegate and revoke sub-agents.
اعمل برأيك (iʿmal bi-raʾyik): An expression implying trust in the agent's judgment; prevents the agent from revoking a sub-agent once appointed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the principal says to his agent 'act according to your opinion' (اعمل برأيك), can the agent revoke the sub-agent he appointed?
A) Yes, because the agent retains full authority.
B) No, because the expression implies trust in the agent's choice, so revocation would contradict that trust.
C) Yes, but only if the sub-agent agrees.
D) No, except with the principal's permission.

Key Takeaway
The agent's authority to delegate and revoke a sub-agent hinges on the principal's wording: broad permission allows revocation, while trust-based appointment prevents it.

Delegation Without Permission and Price Setting Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sub-agent's contract when first agent sets a price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the validity of a sub-agent's contract when the first agent sets a price, and explain the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of the dispute.

Core Idea
Setting a price prevents only loss, not gain—thus the sub-agent cannot act alone without the first agent's oversight.

This lesson dives into a nuanced intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: when an agent delegates without permission and sets a price, can the sub-agent sell in the agent's absence? al-Hidayah appears to permit it, but the majority of narrations and later authorities (al-Ramli, Munyat al-Mufti) restrict it. We will trace the positions, proofs, and the relied-upon ruling for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Start with the general principle: if a sub-agent contracts in the principal's presence or with ratification, it is valid. The dispute focuses on the scenario where the first agent sets a price for the sub-agent and then is absent.
The base matn (Kanz al-Daqaiq) states the general rule; the commentary expands on the exception.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Identify the three positions: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say the sub-agent's contract is invalid whether the first agent is present or absent; Abu Yusuf says it is valid; Ibn Abi Layla (non-Hanafi) also permits it.
The dispute hinges on whether price-setting fully satisfies the need for the agent's opinion (ra'y).
3
Analyze the proofs (talil)
For the restrictive view: setting the price only prevents a decrease, not an increase, and the agent might have negotiated a better price or buyer. For the permissive view: the need for opinion is achieved by setting the price, so no further oversight is needed.
Al-Ramli critiques the attempt to reconcile al-Hidayah and Munyat al-Mufti by distinguishing who set the price (agent vs. principal).
4
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The majority of narrations (Zahir al-Riwayah, except Kitab al-Rahn) and later authorities (al-Ramli, al-Khaniyyah) uphold the restrictive view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: the sub-agent cannot sell alone in the first agent's absence without ratification.
The thamarah al-khilaf: if the sub-agent sells without the first agent's presence or ratification, the contract is invalid (fasid) and does not transfer ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإن وكل بلا إذن الموكل فعقد بحضرته أو باع أجنبي فأجاز صح) لأن المقصود رأيه وقد حضر وتكلموا في حقوقه والصحيح رجوعها إلى الثاني لأنه هو العاقد وإن عقد بغيبته لم يجز لأنه فات رأيه إلا أن يبلغه فأجازه لأنه حضر رأيه، وكذا إذا باع غير الوكيل فبلغه فأجازه ولو قدر الأوّل الثمن للثاني فعقد بغيبته يجوز لأن الرأي يحتاج إليه لتقدير الثمن ظاهراً وقد حصل بخلاف ما إذا وكل وكيلين وقد الثمن لأنه لما فوض إليهما مع تقدير الثمن ظهر أن غرضه اجتماع رأيهما في الزيادة واختيار المشتري، أما إذا لم يقدر الثمن وفوض إلى الأول كان غرضه رأيه في معظم الأمر وهو التقدير في الثمن؛ كذا في الهداية. وفي منية المفتي: وقيل إذا باع الثاني بثمن عينه الموكل جاز بغيبة الأوّل، وفي الأصح لا إلا بحضرة الأول ا هـ . ولا مخالفة بين ما في الهداية وما صححه في المنية لأن الأوّل فيما إذا قدر الوكيل الثمن لوكيله والثاني فيما إذا قدر الموكل الأوّل لوكيله كما لا يخفى. ومعنى قوله صح النفاذ المسألة ظاهر وقوله «كما سيأتي قريباً» أي أوّل المقولة الآتية، وقيد بتقدير الوكيل الأول للثمن احترازاً عن تقدير الموكل الثمن فإنه لا يجوز للوكيل الثاني الانفراد كما سيأتي تصحيحه عن المنية قوله : (ولا مخالفة بين ما في الهداية وما صححه في المنية الخ قال الرملي : هذا غير صحيح بل بينهما مخالفة إذ في اختلاف الرواية قال في الكفاية عند قول صاحب الهداية ولو قدر الأوّل الثمن للثاني فعقد بغيبته يجوز أطلق الجواز وهو رواية كتاب الرهن وقد اختارها لأن الرأي يحتاج فيه لتقدير الثمن ظاهراً وقد حصل، وفي كتاب الوكالة لا يجوز لأن تقدير الثمن لمنع النقصان لا لمنع الزيادة وربما يزيد الأوّل على هذا الثمن لو كان هو المباشر للعقد اهـ. وفي التتارخانية نقلاً عن الخانية : وإن كان بغير محضر العدل وبين الثمن للوكيل بالبيع فوكل الوكيل غيره فباع الثاني بذلك الثمن ذكر في رواية أنه يجوز من كما ذكر في كتاب الرهن وفي عامة الروايات لا يجوز وإن بين الثمن ما لم يجز المالك أو الوكيل الأوّل اهـ. فكيف مع هذا يحمل على اختلاف الموضوع وقد ظهر بقول صاحب المنية وفي الأصح لا إلا بحضرة الأوّل وبقول الخانية وفي عامة الروايات لا يجوز ضعف ما في الهداية ووجهه لأن التقدير يمنع النقصان لا الزيادة واختيار المشتري خصوصاً إذا كان الثمن مؤجلاً لتفاوته في الذمم والاحتياج إلى الرأي في ذلك كما هو واضح فتأمل. وفي الخانية أيضاً: رجل وكل رجلاً أن له هذا الثوب بعشرة دراهم فوكل الوكيل بذلك غيره فباعه الثاني بحضرة الأول. روي عن أبي يوسف أنه يجوز هذا البيع كان الوكيل الأوّل حاضراً أو غائباً ولا يتوقف على الإجازة. وقال أبو حنيفة ومحمد : لا يجوز كان الوكيل الأوّل حاضراً أو غائباً. وقال ابن أبي ليلى : يجوز كان الوكيل الأوّل حاضراً أو غائباً لأن الموكل رضي بزوال ملكه بالثمن المقرر .اهـ. فهو مؤيد لما قلناه فتدبر . اهـ كلام الرملي . قلت : وفيه نظر إذ لا شك فيما قاله المؤلف من أن ما في الهداية تقدير الثمن من جهة الوكيل، وما في المنية من جهة موكله وغاية ما نقله المحشي وجود خلاف في الأولى ولا يلزم منه وجوده في الثانية إلا بنقل صريح، نعم على تقدير عدمه يحتاج إلى الفرق بين المسألتين وهو ظاهر من كلام الهداية، وذلك أن عند تقدير الثمن من الموكل لوكيله يظهر أن غرضه حصول رأيه في الزيادة، واختيار المشتري وإن لم يقدره له كان غرضه رأيه في معظم الأمر وهو التقدير في الثمن فنقول : إذا لم يقدر الموكل له الثمن وقدره الوكيل للوكيل الثاني فقد حصل غرض الموكل الأول فيصح عقده بغيبته، وإن قدره له فباع الثاني بذلك الثمن في غيبة الوكيل الأوّل لم يحصل غرض الموكل الأول وهو حصول رأي وكيله في الزيادة واختيار المشتري.
His saying: (If he delegates without the principal's permission, and then [the sub-agent] contracts in his presence, or a stranger sells and he ratifies, it is valid) because the objective is his opinion and he was present. They discussed the rights: the correct view is that they revert to the second because he is the contracting party. If he contracts in his absence, it is not valid because his opinion was missed, unless he is informed and ratifies it, because then his opinion is present. Similarly, if someone other than the agent sells and he is informed and ratifies. And if the first [agent] sets the price for the second and he contracts in his absence, it is valid because the opinion needed is for setting the price, which has apparently been obtained, unlike the case where he appoints two agents and sets the price, because when he delegates to them with the price set, it appears that his objective is their combined opinion in increasing [the price] and choosing the buyer. As for when he does not set the price and delegates to the first, his objective is his opinion in most of the matter, which is setting the price. This is stated in al-Hidayah. In Munyat al-Mufti: It is said that if the second sells at the price specified by the principal, it is valid in the absence of the first; but the correct view is that it is not valid except in the presence of the first. There is no contradiction between what is in al-Hidayah and what Munyat al-Mufti confirms, because the first is where the agent sets the price for his sub-agent, and the second is where the principal sets the price for the first [agent]. The meaning of his saying 'correct' is apparent. His saying 'as will come shortly' refers to the beginning of the next statement. He restricted by the first agent setting the price to exclude the principal setting the price, because then the second agent cannot act alone, as will be confirmed from Munyat al-Mufti. His saying: (There is no contradiction between what is in al-Hidayah and what he confirmed in Munyat al-Mufti, etc.) al-Ramli said: This is incorrect; rather there is a contradiction. There is a difference in narration: in al-Kifayah on the author of al-Hidayah's statement, 'if the first sets the price for the second and he contracts in his absence, it is valid' – he gave absolute permission, and this is the narration of Kitab al-Rahn, which he chose because the opinion needed is for setting the price, which has been obtained. In Kitab al-Wakalah, it is not valid because setting the price is to prevent a decrease, not to prevent an increase, and perhaps the first would have increased this price had he been the direct contractor. In al-Tatarkhaniyyah quoting al-Khaniyyah: If [the agent] is in the absence of the judge (?) and he specifies the price for the agent to sell, and the agent delegates another, and the second sells at that price, it is mentioned in one narration that it is valid, as in Kitab al-Rahn, but in the majority of narrations it is not valid unless the owner or the first agent ratifies. How then can this be considered a difference in subject matter? It has become apparent from the statement of the author of Munyat al-Mufti ('the correct view is that it is not valid except in the presence of the first') and from al-Khaniyyah ('in the majority of narrations it is not valid') that the view of al-Hidayah is weak. Its reasoning: setting the price prevents decrease, not increase, and the choice of buyer, especially if the price is deferred because of differences in creditworthiness and the need for opinion in that, is clear. In al-Khaniyyah also: A man appointed an agent to sell this cloth for ten dirhams. The agent then delegated another, and the second sold it in the presence of the first. It is narrated from Abu Yusuf that this sale is valid whether the first agent is present or absent, and it is not suspended on ratification. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad said: It is not valid whether the first agent is present or absent. Ibn Abi Layla said: It is valid whether the first agent is present or absent, because the principal consented to the removal of his ownership at the fixed price. So this supports what we said, so reflect. End of al-Ramli's words. I (the commentator) say: There is consideration in this; there is no doubt in what the author said that what is in al-Hidayah is about the agent setting the price, and what is in Munyat al-Mufti is about his principal setting it. The extent of what the marginal commentator (al-muhashi) reported is the existence of a difference in the first case, and this does not necessarily imply its existence in the second case except by explicit transmission. Assuming its absence, a distinction between the two cases is needed, and it is clear from the words of al-Hidayah: When the principal sets the price for his agent, his objective appears to be obtaining his opinion in increasing [the price] and choosing the buyer. If he did not set the price for him, his objective was his opinion in most of the matter, which is setting the price. So we say: If the principal did not set the price for him, and the agent set the price for the second agent, then the principal's objective is achieved, so the contract is valid in his absence. If he set it for him, and the second sold at that price in the absence of the first agent, the principal's objective of obtaining his agent's opinion in increasing and choosing the buyer is not achieved.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This lesson presents a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute about the validity of a sub-agent's contract when the first agent has set a price. The passage opens with the general rule: if an agent delegates without permission, the sub-agent's contract is valid if the principal is present or ratifies; otherwise invalid unless ratification. The key issue is the case where the first agent sets the price for the sub-agent. According to al-Hidayah, the sub-agent can contract in the absence of the first agent because the opinion needed for pricing has been obtained. However, there is a conflict in the school: Kitab al-Rahn permits it, while Kitab al-Wakalah and the majority of narrations hold it invalid, reasoning that price-setting only prevents underpayment, not overpayment, and the agent might have negotiated a better price. Al-Ramli criticizes the attempt to reconcile al-Hidayah and Munyat al-Mufti by distinguishing who set the price (agent vs. principal). He brings evidence from al-Khaniyyah citing Abu Yusuf's permissive view against Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's restrictive view. Ibn Abi Layla (a Kufi scholar outside the Hanafi school) also permits it. The final commentator defends al-Hidayah's position by maintaining the distinction: if the principal sets the price, the agent's opinion on increase is needed; if the agent sets it, the principal's objective (the agent's overall judgment) is satisfied. In practice, the mufta bihi (relied upon) position is the restrictive one (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), meaning the sub-agent cannot contract alone unless the first agent is present or ratifies.

Key Terms
تقدير الثمن (taqdīr al-thaman): The act of the agent or principal specifying the exact price for the sale, limiting the sub-agent's discretion.
الوكيل الأول (al-wakīl al-awwal): The first agent who is appointed by the principal and who then appoints a sub-agent (الوكيل الثاني).
رأي (ra'y): personal judgment or opinion; in agency, the agent's discretion to negotiate price, choose buyer, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), if the first agent sets a price and the sub-agent sells in the first agent's absence, what is the ruling?
A) The sale is valid and binding without ratification.
B) The sale is invalid and cannot be ratified.
C) The sale is suspended (mawqūf) on the first agent's ratification or rejection.
D) The sale is valid only if the principal also sets the price.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is that a sub-agent cannot sell in the first agent's absence even if a price is set; ratification by the first agent or principal is required.

Scope of Agency for Litigation and Receipt: The Zufar Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute with Zufar on scope of agency for litigation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the scope of agency for litigation and receipt, identify the intra-Hanafi dispute with Zufar, and explain why the fatwa follows Zufar's view.

Core Idea
An agent for litigation does not receive; an agent for receipt may litigate. Fatwa follows Zufar due to custom and trust.

This lesson examines a key distinction in Hanafi agency law: the scope of powers granted to an agent for litigation versus an agent for receipt. While the majority (Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn) held that litigation includes receipt, Zufar disagreed. The later fatwa tradition, influenced by custom and prevalent dishonesty, adopted Zufar's restrictive view, making it the operative ruling today.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of agents
The passage distinguishes between an agent for litigation (wakīl bi al-khuṣūma) and an agent for receipt (wakīl bi qabḍ al-dayn). Understand the primary task of each: litigation vs. receipt of debt.
Note that the agent for litigation is also called wakīl bi al-taqāḍī.
2
State the majority Hanafi position
Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad hold that an agent for litigation also has power to receive. Their reasoning: 'Whoever owns something owns its completion' (من ملك شيئاً ملك إتمامه). Litigation's completion is receipt, so he may receive.
This is a qiyas-based argument.
3
State Zufar's opposing position
Zufar restricts the agent to litigation only; receipt is a separate, independent act not implicitly authorized by the principal.
Zufar's view is based on a stricter understanding of the principal's consent.
4
Explain the fatwa preference
The fatwa today follows Zufar due to the prevalence of betrayal (khā'ina) among agents and the custom that one entrusted with litigation may not be trustworthy with money. Custom also influences the ruling for the agent for suing (mutqadi).
The shift from majority to Zufar is an example of istihsan overriding qiyas.
5
Apply the ruling to related agents
The passage lists several types of agents (for division, pre-emption, etc.) and whether they have litigation and receipt powers. Understand that custom can override the default principle.
The messenger (rasūl) for suing has receipt but not litigation by consensus.

Chapter of Agency for Litigation and Receipt. The agent for litigation and suing does not have the power to receive, and by the receipt of debt he has the power to litigate. And if he appointed him to demand every right he has over people, or for every settlement he has in Khwarazm, it includes existing rights not future ones. And Shaykh al-Islam mentioned that if he appointed him for receipt of every right he has over so-and-so, it includes existing and future rights as well, so one should reflect when giving fatwa. And in al-Muntaqa: If he appointed him for receipt of every debt he has, future ones are also included, just as if he appointed him for receipt of his produce, he receives the future produce as well. And he missed the agent for settlement; he does not litigate as in Kafi al-Hakim from the chapter of agency for blood. Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the ruling that an agent appointed for litigation and suing does not have the authority to receive payment, while an agent appointed for receipt of a debt does have authority to litigate. The commentary begins by referencing earlier definitions. It then presents the view of Zufar, who restricts the agent for litigation solely to litigation and denies him receipt. The Hanafi position (with us) is that the agent for litigation is also an agent for receipt because whoever has authority over something has authority over its completion, and litigation is completed by receipt. However, the fatwa today follows Zufar’s view due to prevalent dishonesty among agents; often one entrusted with litigation is not trustworthy with money. The text then discusses the agent for suing (mutqadi), who according to the original transmission has power to receive, but custom often contradicts this, so the fatwa is that he does not receive. The passage also clarifies that a messenger (rasul) for suing has receipt power but not litigation, by consensus. The author then signals that the agent for litigation cannot settle, and the agent for following (mulazama) cannot litigate or receive. A list of ten types of agents follows from al-Bazzaziyya, specifying which have litigation and receipt powers. Various additional issues are discussed, such as whether a general appointment for demand includes existing or also future rights, with a note from Shaykh al-Islam that for a specific person, both are included. The passage ends with a note on the father's role and a reaffirmation that the custom of merchants is now the basis of fatwa.

Key Terms
وكيل بالخصومة (wakīl bi al-khuṣūma): An agent appointed specifically to conduct litigation on behalf of the principal.
وكيل بقبض الدين (wakīl bi qabḍ al-dayn): An agent appointed to receive a debt.
التقاضي (al-taqāḍī): To demand one's right through legal process; suing.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi position restricts an agent for litigation from receiving, shifting from the majority qiyas to Zufar's view due to custom and risk of betrayal.

Proving Agency in Court: The Condition of a Present Defendant and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Combined Evidence
Procedural rules for proving agency in court, with an intra-Hanafi dispute on combined evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for proving agency in court when the principal is absent, and explain the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding combined evidence for agency and the underlying right.

Core Idea
Without a present defendant, an agent's witnesses are not heard — agency is procedurally distinct from the underlying claim.

When an agent appears before a judge to prove his authority, the judge cannot simply accept witnesses to the agency. The Hanafi school requires that a present defendant (khasm) be brought before the court. This procedural safeguard protects the absent principal's rights. But an even more subtle question arises: if the agent brings evidence that simultaneously proves both the agency and the debt, should the judge accept it for both? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, differ on this point. This lesson unpacks these rules and their practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the procedural condition
When the principal is absent, the agent must bring a present contesting defendant (khasm jahid or muqir) for the judge to hear evidence on the agency. Without such a defendant, the judge rejects the witnesses.
This condition is explicitly stated: the judge does not listen to witnesses until the agent brings a defendant who denies or admits the claim.
2
Understand the dispute scope
The dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn arises when the agent brings witnesses who testify to both the agency and the underlying right (e.g., the debt) simultaneously. The question is whether one set of evidence suffices for both or each must be proven separately.
The dispute is an intra-Hanafi legitimate ikhtilaf based on differing approaches to the unity of the claim.
3
Examine each position
Imam Abu Hanifa: The evidence is accepted only for the agency; the agent must repeat the evidence for the debt. The Sahibayn: The evidence is accepted for both, and judgment is given accordingly.
The text uses 'at the Imam' (عند الإمام) to refer to Abu Hanifa's position, indicating it is his specific view.
4
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, two separate witness sessions are needed: one for agency, one for the debt. Under the Sahibayn, one session suffices for both.
This affects the efficiency of litigation and the burden on the agent.
5
Note the judge's knowledge requirement when principal is present
If the principal is present and appoints the agent in court, no opponent is needed, but the judge must know the principal's identity (name and lineage). If the judge does not know him, the agency cannot be established without an opponent.
This is from the commentary of Munyat al-Mufti, stressing that the judge cannot rule for an unknown person.

Source Text

Book of Agency, Chapter of Agency for Litigation and Receipt. 303 And with litigation therein, and he came with evidence of agency and the principal is absent, and the agent did not bring anyone before him who has a claim against the principal, then the judge does not listen to his witnesses until he brings a defendant who denies or admits the claim; then he listens and enforces the agency for him. If he then brings another defendant, he does not need to repeat the evidence. And if he claimed agency to demand every right he has against a specific person, his presence is required. And if it is established by his presence, and then he comes with another defendant and establishes evidence of agency again, claiming that he appointed him to receive every right he has against his principal, and this person owes such and such, and he brought evidence that they witnessed the agency and the right against the defendant all at once, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the evidence is accepted only for the agency, and he is ordered to repeat the evidence for the right. And also in Munyat al-Mufti: If the principal comes to the judge and appoints the agent, but there is no opponent with him, it is permissible and he becomes an agent if the judge knows the principal. But if the judge does not know him, it is not permissible because the principal at the time of judgment on the agency is absent, and an absent person is only known by name and lineage. So if the judge knows the principal's name and lineage, judgment on the agency is possible; otherwise, if he judges, he judges for a known person against an unknown. And if the principal says 'I will establish evidence that I am so-and-so son of so-and-so,' because the condition for hearing evidence on lineage requires litigation, which is not present. And in al-Qunyah: Evidence from an agent for litigation on his agency is not accepted without a present opponent; and if judgment is given, it is valid according to the one who differs. Because it is a judgment in a case not heard. And in Khizanat al-Muftin: A man appointed a man to sell a specific asset from his wealth, and the agent wanted to establish the agency for sale before the judge so that if the principal comes and denies, his denial is not considered. There are three methods: first, the agent hands the asset to a man, then claims he is the agent of its owner for receipt and sale, and the possessor says he has no knowledge of the agency, so he establishes evidence that he is his agent for receipt and sale, and the judge hears that and orders delivery to him, and he sells it. Second, he says 'This is so-and-so's property, I sell it to you'; when he sells it, he orders him to receive the sold item; the buyer says 'I will not receive from you because I fear the owner will come and deny the agency, and perhaps the received item will be in my hand and suffer damage, making me liable'; so the agent establishes evidence that he is agent of so-and-so for sale and delivery, and he compels him to receive, and by establishing evidence, the authority to compel receipt is proven. Third, a man claimed that the house in your hand is so-and-so's property, and you are his agent for sale, and you have sold; he says 'I sold to you, but I am not an agent from so-and-so, and he did not appoint me for sale'; then the claimant of purchase establishes evidence that he is the agent of so-and-so for sale; he is a defendant so evidence can be heard against him, and it is established that he is an agent in the sale. Commentary
This passage focuses on the legal procedure for an agent to prove his agency before a judge, especially when the principal is absent. The key requirement is that the agent must bring a present, contesting defendant (khasm jahid or muqir) before the judge will accept witnesses to the agency. If no opponent is present, the judge cannot hear the evidence. The text details various scenarios: if the agent brings a specific defendant and obtains a judgment, then brings another, he does not need to re-prove the agency. However, if he claims a general agency to collect all rights from a specific person, the presence of that person is required. There is a dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the acceptance of combined evidence for both agency and the underlying right: Abu Hanifa requires separate evidence for each, while the Sahibayn accept combined evidence. The commentary from Munyat al-Mufti stresses that the judge must know the principal's identity; otherwise, the agency cannot be established. The text then presents three practical methods from Khizanat al-Muftin for an agent to establish an agency for sale before the judge, protecting the agent from future denial by the principal. These methods involve various parties and evidence. Finally, the passage ends with a note from al-Ramli that the rule 'by receipt of debt he has power to litigate' applies to a jabi (collector) of waqf, who can sue tenants who claim they paid the nazir.

Key Terms
البينة (al-bayyinah): Legal evidence, usually witness testimony, required to establish agency in court.
الخصم (al-khaṣm): The opponent or defendant in a lawsuit; his presence is necessary for the judge to hear evidence on agency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what must the agent present to the judge to have his witnesses heard regarding his agency when the principal is absent?
A) A written document from the principal.
B) A present defendant (khasm) who either admits or denies.
C) Two witnesses who know the principal personally.
D) A surety (kafil).

Key Takeaway
To prove agency in Hanafi court, a present defendant is required; and evidence for agency is distinct from evidence for the underlying right according to Imam Abu Hanifa.

Agent for Debt Collection: Authority to Litigate
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agent's litigation authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether an agent for debt collection becomes a litigant, and identify the mufta bihi position with its limitation.

Core Idea
An agent to collect a debt may become a litigant, but only for claims of receipt or discharge—not for set-off.

The relationship between agency and litigation is subtle. Does appointing someone to collect a debt automatically give them the right to sue? The Hanafi school is divided, and the practical answer is not as broad as one might think. This lesson unpacks the arguments and the operative ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling in the matn
The base text states that the agent for debt collection 'owns the litigation' (يملك الخصومة). Recognize that this is the starting point for the dispute.
The matn is from Kanz al-Daqa'iq; the sharh is from al-Bahr al-Raiq.
2
State the two positions
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the agent becomes a litigant. The two companions (Sahibayn) hold that he does not. Also a narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa agrees with the Sahibayn.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Examine the dalil for each
The Sahibayn argue that taking possession (qabd) is not litigation, and not every trusted person is adept at litigation. Abu Hanifa argues by analogy to the agent for pre-emption (shuf'a) who litigates before taking possession, because debt collection involves rights that require litigation.
The qiyas is central: debts are repaid by equivalents, so collection resembles exchange.
4
Note the limitation by al-Ramli
Although Abu Hanifa's view is the mufta bihi, al-Ramli restricts it: the agent is a litigant only when the debtor claims receipt (istīfā’) or discharge (ibrā’) by the principal, not for set-off or defect claims.
This is the operative fatwa (waqi'at al-fatwa).
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if the agent is a litigant, his evidence of the principal's receipt or discharge is accepted, protecting the debtor from double payment. If not, the matter is postponed until the principal appears.
The dispute affects the debtor's risk and the efficiency of collection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قول المصنف : ( وبقبض الدين يملك الخصومة ) أي الوكيل بقبض الدين يلي الخصومة مع المديون عند أبي حنيفة حتى لو أقيمت عليه البينة على استيفاء الموكل أو إبرائه تقبل عنده . وقالا : لا يكون خصماً وهو رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة لأن القبض غير الخصومة، وليس كل من يؤتمن على المال يهتدي في الخصومات فلم يكن الرضا بالقبض رضا بها. ولأبي حنيفة أنه وكله بالتملك لأن الديون تقضى بأمثالها إذ قبض الدين نفسه لا يتصور يتمكن من قبضه بل يوقف الأمر إلى حضور الغائب ا هـ . وفي النهاية : فتقبل بينة الشريك على الوكيل بالقسمة أن موكله أخذ نصيبه، وكذا الموهوب له فتقبل بينته على الوكيل في الرجوع أن موكله أخذ عوضها وكذا البائع تقبل بينته على الوكيل بالرد بالعيب أن موكله رضي به ا هـ لا يقال لو كان وكيلاً أن تلحقه العهدة في المقبوض لأنه استيفاء عين الحق من وجه لأن من الديون بالمبادلة وجب ما لا يجوز الاستبدال به فلشبهه بالمبادلة جعلناه خصماً ولشبهه بأخذ العين لا تلحقه العهدة عملاً بهما ؛ كذا في النهاية والذخيرة. وأورد أيضاً لو كان وكيلاً بالمبادلة لم يجز توكيل المسلم في قبض الخمر كما لا يوكل في تمليكها، وأجيب بأنه تمليك حكماً والمسلم يصح أن يملكها حكماً، وإن لم يجز عقده ؛ كذا في غاية البيان. وفي كافي الحاكم : يصح توكيل الذمي المسلم في قبض الخمر ويكره للمسلم قبضها. وفي الذخيرة إن الاختلاف مبني على أن الموكل فيه ملك الموكل أو ملك الغير فقالا بالأول بناء على أن المقبوض عين صاحب الدين حكماً كان له الأخذ من غير قضاء ولا رضا كما لو كان عنده وديعة أو غصب. وقال الإمام: إنه وكيل بقبض ملك الغير بناء على أن المقبوض ليس ملك رب الدين حقيقة بل هو بدله بدليل أن للمديون التصرف فيما في يده وإن لم يرض الدائن ا هـ . ثم اعلم أنا قدمنا عن الهداية أن الوكيل بقبض الدين ينتصب خصماً للمديون إذا ادعى استيفاء الموكل أو إبراءه. وفرق في حتى القبض لما عليهم وهي واقعة الفتوى وانظر لما كتبناه في أحكام الوكلاء على جامع الفصولين قوله : (حتى لو أقيمت عليه البينة على استيفاء الموكل أو إبرائه قال الرملي : قيد بهما لأنه لو ادعى ديناً على الموكل وأراد مقاصصته به لا يكون الوكيل خصماً عنه وهي واقعة الفتوى وكذلك لو ادعى المشتري على وكيل البائع في قبض ثمن المبيع عيباً وأراد رده عليه لا يكون خصماً فيه كما يدل عليه الكلام الآتي وهي واقعة
