The author's statement: 'And by taking possession of the debt, he owns the litigation' meaning the agent for taking possession of the debt undertakes litigation with the debtor according to Abu Hanifa, such that if evidence is brought against him regarding the principal's receipt or his discharge, it is accepted with him. But the two companions say: he is not a litigant, and it is a narration from Hasan from Abu Hanifa, because taking possession is not litigation, and not everyone entrusted with property is skilled in litigations, so consent to taking possession is not consent to it. Abu Hanifa's proof: he appointed him to own, because debts are repaid by their equivalents since taking possession of the debt itself is inconceivable; rather, it is made a collection for someone else's right in some respect, so it resembles the agent for taking pre-emption and revoking a gift, and the agent for purchase, division, and return due to defect. These resemble the agent for taking pre-emption such that he is a litigant before taking possession just as he is a litigant before taking there. The agent for purchase is not a litigant before undertaking the purchase. This is because exchange entails rights, and he is principal in them, so he is a litigant therein. As in al-Hidayah. In al-Dhakhirah: According to their view, his evidence (of payment) is not accepted for his discharge but is accepted to restrict the agent's hand so that he cannot take possession; rather, the matter is postponed until the absent person appears. End. In al-Nihayah: So the evidence of a partner against the agent for division that his principal took his share is accepted; similarly, the donee's evidence against the agent for revocation that his principal took its compensation; and similarly, the seller's evidence against the agent for return due to defect that his principal was satisfied with it. End. It cannot be said that if he were an agent, he would be liable for what he took, because it is a recovery of the very right in some respect. Since debts are by exchange, it necessitates what is not permitted to be substituted, so due to its resemblance to exchange we made him a litigant, and due to its resemblance to taking an in-kind item, he is not held liable, working by both aspects. As in al-Nihayah and al-Dhakhirah. Also, it was raised: if he were an agent by exchange, it would not be permissible to appoint a Muslim to take possession of wine, just as he is not appointed to own it. The answer: it is ownership in a legal sense, and a Muslim may own it in a legal sense, even if his contract is not valid. As in Ghayat al-Bayan. In Kafi al-Hakim: It is valid to appoint a Jew as an agent for a Muslim to take possession of wine, and it is disliked for the Muslim to take possession. In al-Dhakhirah: The difference is based on whether the subject of agency is the principal's property or another's property. The two companions said the former, based on the view that the taken item is legally the very asset of the creditor; he may take it without court order or consent, just as if he had a deposit or usurped item. Imam Abu Hanifa said: he is an agent for taking possession of another's property, based on the view that the taken item is not actually the creditor's property but its substitute, evidenced by the debtor's ability to dispose of what is in his hand even if the creditor is not satisfied. End. Then know we previously mentioned from al-Hidayah that the agent for taking possession of a debt becomes a litigant against the debtor if the debtor claims the principal has received or discharged. He distinguished regarding 'even' (حتى) ...? And this is the operative fatwa. Consider what we wrote in Ahkam al-Wukala' on Jami' al-Fusulain. His statement: 'such that if evidence is brought against him regarding the principal's receipt or his discharge' – al-Ramli said: he specified these two because if he claimed a debt against the principal and wanted to set it off, the agent is not a litigant regarding it, and this is the operative fatwa. Similarly, if the buyer claims a defect against the seller's agent for receiving the sale price and wants to return it, the agent is not a litigant therein, as the later discussion indicates, and this is also the operative fatwa. Consider this to understand. What is mentioned in al-Mujtaba commentary on al-Quduri is clear regarding what we said: he said, 'And the agent for taking possession of a debt is an agent for litigation therein according to Abu Hanifa.' By 'therein' he means concerning the debt.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains and debates the ruling that an agent appointed to collect a debt (الوكيل بقبض الدين) also gains the authority to litigate (الخصومة) against the debtor. The author presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that such an agent becomes a litigant, so if evidence is brought against him regarding the principal having already received the debt or having discharged the debtor, that evidence is accepted from him. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, saying he is not a litigant, and this is also a narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa. Their reasoning: taking possession (qabd) is separate from litigation, and not every trusted person is adept at litigation. Abu Hanifa's reasoning, derived from al-Hidayah, is that the agent is appointed to 'own' the debt, because debts are repaid by their equivalents (since the physical debt itself cannot be taken). Therefore, it resembles the agent for taking pre-emption (shuf'a) or revoking a gift, where the agent is a litigant before actual taking. The text then notes a consequence: according to the two companions, the agent's evidence of the principal's receipt is not accepted to discharge the debtor, but is accepted to restrict the agent's authority (so he cannot collect again). The dispute is ultimately based on a deeper usul difference: do agents collect the principal's own property or another's? The two companions consider the collected amount as legally the principal's property (so the agent collects the principal's own), while Abu Hanifa sees it as the debtor's property until actually transferred. The passage also discusses exceptions: the agent is not a litigant in cases like set-off or claims of defect. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) according to al-Ramli is that the agent is a litigant only regarding claims of receipt or discharge by the principal, not for other claims like set-off. Thus, the operative fatwa agrees with Abu Hanifa but with limitation.

Key Terms
وكيل بقبض الدين (wakīl bi-qabḍ al-dayn): An agent appointed to collect a debt owed to the principal.
خصومة (khuṣūmah): Litigation, the right to represent the principal in court as a litigant.
إستيفاء (istīfā’): The act of receiving full payment of the debt by the principal.
إبراء (ibrā’): Discharge of the debtor by the principal, releasing him from the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in the text, for which of the following claims is the agent for debt collection considered a litigant?
A) The debtor claims he has a set-off against the principal
B) The debtor claims he has already paid the principal (استيفاء)
C) The buyer claims a defect in the sold item
D) The principal claims the agent embezzled the money

Key Takeaway
The agent for debt collection is a litigant only regarding the debtor's claim of the principal's receipt or discharge, not for other counterclaims.

The Wakil for Debt as Litigant: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on litigation authority of a debt collection agent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on whether a wakil bi-qabd al-dayn becomes a khasm, and apply the qiyas argument used by Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
Debt collection is an exchange; thus the agent becomes a litigant by analogy to shuf'a.

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute: does an agent appointed solely to collect a debt also become a litigant (khasm) when the debtor claims payment or discharge? Abu Hanifa holds that he does, while the Sahibayn and a narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad disagree. The difference turns on whether debt collection is fundamentally an exchange (mubadala) or a mere receipt (qabd). Understanding this dispute is crucial for Hanafi legal practice, as it determines where evidence is accepted and how debtors interact with agents.

Guided Path
1
State the issue and positions
Identify the precise legal question: Is an agent appointed to collect a debt also a representative in litigation (khasm) when the debtor claims to have paid or been discharged? State Abu Hanifa's position (agent is khasm) and the Sahibayn's position (agent is not khasm).
The dispute is intra-Hanafi; both views are transmitted within the school.
2
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
Examine the qiyas argument: the agent for debt collection is analogous to agents for shuf'a, revocation of gift, purchase, division, and return due to defect. The common element (illa) is that these agencies involve an exchange (mubadala) where the agent acts as a principal. Since debt is paid by equivalents, collecting it is a form of exchange.
The closest analogy is the agent for shuf'a, who becomes a litigant even before taking possession.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof
Their argument: collection (qabd) and litigation (khusumat) are distinct functions. Consent to one does not imply consent to the other. Not every trusted person is skilled in litigation. Therefore, the agent's authority is limited to receipt.
They also point out that a mere messenger (rasul) or one ordered (ma'mur) to collect has no litigation capacity by consensus.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the debtor can bring evidence of payment or discharge against the agent; the agent's testimony is accepted. Under the Sahibayn, such evidence is not accepted against the agent; the matter is postponed until the principal appears.
This affects the practical rights of the debtor and the speed of dispute resolution.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preponderance)
Later authorities (e.g., Jami' al-Fusulayn) consider the Sahibayn's view stronger, noting it is also a narration from Abu Hanifa. However, the issue remains disputed, and mufti must be aware of both positions.
The dispute also distinguishes between claims of payment (ifa') and discharge (ibra'). Some sources make the agent a khasm only for payment.

الخصومة، وليس كل من يؤتمن على المال يهتدي في الخصومات فلم يكن الرضا بالقبض رضا بها. ولأبي حنيفة أنه وكله بالتملك لأن الديون تقضى بأمثالها إذ قبض الدين نفسه لا يتصور إلا أنه جعل استيفاء لغير حقه من وجه وإن لم يجز عقده ؛ كذا في غاية البيان. وفي كافي الحاكم : يصح توكيل الذمي المسلم في قبض الخمر ويكره للمسلم قبضها. وفي الذخيرة إن الاختلاف مبني على أن الموكل فيه ملك الموكل أو ملك الغير فقالا بالأول بناء للمديون التصرف فيما في يده وإن لم يرض الدائن ا هـ . ثم اعلم أنا قدمنا عن الهداية أن الوكيل بقبض الدين ينتصب خصماً للمديون إذا ادعى استيفاء الموكل أو إبراءه. وفرق في الذخيرة بينهما فجعله خصماً له في دعوى الإيفاء لرب الدين دون الإبراء لأنه خصم في الإثبات لكونه سبباً لقبضه والإيفاء إلى الطالب وقبض الوكيل سواء بخلاف الإبراء لأنه ليس في جعله خصماً فيه إحياء لحقه بل فيه إبطال حقه وهو قياس مسألة الوكيل بأخذ الشفعة فإنه في الإثبات، وإذا ادعى عليه تسليم الآخر فإنه لا يصير خصماً لما ذكرنا من إبطال حق الموكل. وذكر شيخ الإسلام في شرحه مسألة دعوى الإبراء على الوفاق، وذكرها الشيخ الإمام الزاهد أحمد الطواويسي على الخلاف الذي ذكرنا في دعوى الإيفاء وإليه إشار محمد في أول وكالة الأصل ا هـ . والحوالة كالإبراء ولم يذكر محمد في الجامع الصغير إلا أنه خصم في دعوى الإيفاء وسكت عن الإبراء، وكذا سكت عنه في كافي الحاكم الذي هو جمع كلام محمد . وفي البدائع : لو أقام الغريم البينة على الإيفاء سمعت عنده خلافاً لهما، وعلى هذا الاختلاف لو أقامها الغريم على أنه أعطى الطالب بالدراهم دنانير أو باعه بها عرضاً فبينته مسموعة عنده خلافاً لهما لأن إيفاء الدين بطريقين بالمقاصة والمبادلة ويستوي فيهما الجنس وخلافه ا هـ . ولم يذكر الإبراء. ونقل في المعراج التسوية بين دعوى الإيفاء والإبراء عن الأئمة ولم يذكر غيره. وصرح في الفتاوى الصغرى بأن الوكيل بقبض الدين يصير خصماً في إثبات الدين وفي إثبات الإبراء والإيفاء عليه بالبينة عند أبي حنيفة خلافاً لهما ثم قال : والرسول أو المأمور بقبض الدين لا يملك الخصومة. وذكر خواهرزاده في المعقود أن الوكيل بقبض الدين لا يملك الخصومة إجماعاً إن كان وكيل القاضي كما لو وكل وكيلاً بقبض ديون الغائب .ا.هـ. وظاهره أن الأمر ليس بتوكيل وقدمنا ما فيه. وفي جامع الفصولين وكيل طلب الشفعة والرد بعيب والقسمة تسمع بيئته عليه أن موكله سلم الشفعة أو أبرأ عن العيب. ثم رقم : لا تسمع البينة عليه أن موكله سلم الشفعة، وكتب على حاشية يمنع كونه وكيلاً بالخصومة في غيره كادعاء المديون الدين وكادعائه العيب في واقعتي الحال فتأمل قوله : (وقالا لا يكون خصماً قال في الفصل الخامس من جامع الفصولين: ولو وكله بقبض دينه فبرهن على الإيفاء إلى موكله يقبل عند أبي حنيفة بخلاف العين ويوقف عندهما في الكل العين والدين والحق أن قولهما أقوى وهو رواية عنه كذا في (عده) وغيره اهـ ملخصاً. ومثله في نور العين لكن في تصحيح العلامة قاسم وعلى قول الإمام المحبوبي في أصح الأقاويل والاختيارات والنسفي والموصلي وصدر الشريعة قوله : (إلا) أنه جعل استيفاء العين حقه من وجه) قال الرملي : إنما كان كذلك لئلا يمتنع قضاء ديون لا يجوز الاستبدال بها كبدل السلم والصرف قوله: (وظاهره أن الأمر ليس بتوكيل أي ظاهر قوله أو المأمور كذا قاله الرملي. وقوله وقدمنا ما فيه أي أول كتاب الوكالة في الرد على الزيلعي حيث جعله رسالة
Book of Agency: Chapter on Agency for Litigation and Collection. The debtor according to Abu Hanifa is such that if evidence is brought against him of the principal's receipt or discharge, it is accepted in his view. They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He is not a litigant, and this is also a narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa, because collection is not litigation, and not everyone entrusted with wealth is skilled in litigation, so consent to collection is not consent to litigation. Abu Hanifa's proof: He appointed him for ownership because debts are paid by their equivalents, since collection of the debt itself is inconceivable, except that it is made a collection for another's right in a sense, so it resembles the agent for taking shuf'a, revoking a gift, the agent for purchase, division, and return due to defect. And these resemble the agent for taking shuf'a such that he becomes a litigant before collection just as he becomes a litigant before taking there. The agent for purchase does not become a litigant before undertaking the purchase, because exchange entails rights and he is a principal therein, so he becomes a litigant therein. As in al-Hidayah. In al-Dhakhirah: According to them, his evidence for his discharge is not accepted, but it is accepted for restricting the agent's hand so that he cannot collect; rather the matter is postponed until the absent person appears, end. In al-Nihayah: So the partner's evidence against the agent for division that his principal took his share is accepted, and likewise the donee's evidence against the agent for revocation that his principal took its compensation, and the seller's evidence against the agent for return due to defect that his principal was satisfied with it, end. It cannot be said that if he were an agent, liability would attach to him for what is collected, because it is collection of the very right in a sense, because from debts through exchange, what is not permissible to substitute becomes obligatory. So due to its resemblance to exchange we made him a litigant, and due to its resemblance to taking a specific item, liability does not attach to him, acting on both aspects. As in al-Nihayah and al-Dhakhirah. It was also raised that if he were an agent for exchange, it would not be permissible to appoint a Muslim to collect wine just as he cannot be appointed to own it. The answer is that it is ownership in a legal sense, and a Muslim can own it legally even if his contract is not valid. As in Ghayat al-Bayan. In Kafi al-Hakim: It is valid for a dhimmī to appoint a Muslim to collect wine, and it is disliked for the Muslim to collect it. In al-Dhakhirah: The difference is based on whether the object of agency is the principal's property or someone else's property. They (the Sahibayn) said the former, based on the fact that what is collected is the creditor's property in a legal sense, he could take it without judgment or consent as if it were a deposit or usurped property with him. Imam (Abu Hanifa) said: He is an agent for collecting another's property, based on the fact that what is collected is not truly the creditor's property but its substitute, evidence being that the debtor may dispose of what is in his hand even if the creditor does not consent. Then know that we mentioned from al-Hidayah that the agent for collecting a debt becomes a litigant against the debtor if he claims the principal's receipt or discharge. And al-Dhakhirah distinguished between them, making him a litigant in the claim of payment to the creditor but not in discharge, because he is a litigant in affirmance as he is a cause for collection, and payment to the claimant and collection by the agent are the same. Unlike discharge, because making him a litigant therein does not revive his right but rather nullifies it. This is analogous to the case of the agent for taking shuf'a, who is for affirmance. If the other party claims delivery against him, he does not become a litigant for the reason mentioned of nullifying the principal's right. Shaykh al-Islam mentioned in his commentary the case of the claim of discharge as agreed upon, and Shaykh Imam Zahid Ahmad al-Tawawisi mentioned it as disputed as we mentioned in the claim of payment, and Muhammad referred to this at the beginning of Wakalat al-Asl. Hawala is like discharge. In al-Jami' al-Saghir, Muhammad only mentioned that he is a litigant in the claim of payment and remained silent on discharge, and likewise remained silent in Kafi al-Hakim which is a compilation of Muhammad's words. In al-Bada'i': If the debtor brings evidence of payment, it is heard in his (Abu Hanifa's) view, contrary to them. On this difference, if the debtor brings evidence that he gave the claimant dinars instead of dirhams or sold him goods in exchange, his evidence is heard in his view contrary to them, because payment of debt is by two methods: set-off and exchange, and they are equal whether the type is same or different, end. He did not mention discharge. In al-Mi'raj, equality between the claim of payment and discharge was reported from the imams, and no other view was mentioned. In al-Fatawa al-Sughra, it was explicit that the agent for collecting a debt becomes a litigant in proving the debt and in proving discharge and payment against him by evidence according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to them. Then he said: The messenger or one ordered to collect a debt does not possess the capacity for litigation. Kharazada mentioned in al-Ma'qud that the agent for collecting a debt does not possess the capacity for litigation by consensus if he is the agent of the judge, as if he appointed an agent to collect the debts of an absent person. Its apparent meaning is that the matter is not an agency, and we have already discussed this. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: The agent for requesting shuf'a, return due to defect, and division, his evidence is heard against him that his principal relinquished shuf'a or discharged from defect. Then he numbered: His evidence is not heard that his principal relinquished shuf'a. And in the margin it was written: It prevents him from being an agent for litigation in other matters, such as the debtor's claim of debt and his claim of defect in the two real situations. So reflect. His statement: (And they said he is not a litigant). In the fifth chapter of Jami' al-Fusulayn: If he appointed him to collect his debt and he proved payment to his principal, it is accepted according to Abu Hanifa, unlike a specific item. And it is suspended according to them in all cases, specific item and debt. The truth is that their view is stronger, and it is a narration from him. As in 'Uda and others. End summarized. Similarly in Nur al-'Ayn, but in the correction of 'Allamah Qasim, according to the saying of Imam al-Mahbubi in the most authentic sayings and choices, and al-Nasafi, al-Mawsili, and Sadr al-Shari'a. His statement: (Except that he made collection of the specific item as his right in a sense). Al-Ramli said: It is only like that so that the payment of debts that cannot be substituted is not prevented, such as the substitute for salam and sarf. His statement: (And its apparent meaning is that the matter is not an agency) i.e., the apparent meaning of his statement 'or the one ordered' as said by al-Ramli. And his statement: 'We have already discussed this' i.e., at the beginning of the Book of Agency in the refutation of al-Zayla'i where he made it a message.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses a key intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the authority of an agent (wakil) appointed to collect a debt. The central question: does such an agent also become a representative in litigation (khusumat) against the debtor? The passage presents the positions of Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), their proofs, and various nuances.

The passage begins by stating the ruling of Abu Hanifa: if the debtor brings evidence that the principal (the creditor) has already received payment or discharged the debt, the agent is considered a litigant (khasm) and the evidence is accepted against him. The Sahibayn and a narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa hold that the agent is not a litigant, because collection and litigation are different functions; consent to the former does not imply consent to the latter.

Abu Hanifa's proof is based on qiyas (analogy). The agent for debt is effectively an agent for ownership (tamlik) because debts are paid by equivalents; the very act of collecting the debt is actually receiving a substitute, not the original loaned item. Thus, it resembles other agencies where litigation is accepted: agent for taking shuf'a (pre-emption), revocation of a gift, purchase, division, and return due to defect. Among these, the agent for shuf'a is the closest parallel: he becomes a litigant even before taking possession, because exchange entails rights where the agent is considered a principal. Similarly, the agent for purchase does not become a litigant before actually purchasing, because purchase is a pure exchange; but debt collection is a partial exchange, hence the analogy.

The passage then addresses potential objections. First, if the agent were treated as a litigant, would he bear liability (uhdah) for the collected funds? No, because the collection is, in a sense, taking the creditor's own right; it resembles taking a specific item (ayn) in that liability does not attach. The agent is made a litigant due to its resemblance to exchange, and liability is not imposed due to its resemblance to taking a specific item, applying both aspects.

Another objection: if the agent is considered for exchange, then a Muslim cannot be appointed to collect wine (khamr) just as he cannot be appointed to own it. The answer is that it is ownership in a legal sense (hukman), and a Muslim can legally own wine even if his contract is invalid.

The passage also discusses a deeper foundational difference. According to the Sahibayn, the object of agency is the principal's property, because what is collected is legally the creditor's property; he could take it without the debtor's consent, like a deposit or usurped item. According to Abu Hanifa, the agent collects another's property, because what is collected is not the creditor's actual property but its substitute; the debtor can still dispose of it until payment.

Further, the passage distinguishes between claims of payment (ifa') and discharge (ibra'). According to al-Dhakhirah, the agent is a litigant for payment but not for discharge, because affirming payment serves the principal's right (agent's collection is equivalent), while affirming discharge would nullify the right. However, other sources like al-Fatawa al-Sughra and al-Mi'raj equate the two, and al-Bada'i' only mentions payment. The passage notes that Muhammad al-Shaybani in al-Jami' al-Saghir only mentions the agent's litigation in payment and is silent on discharge.

Finally, the passage mentions that the agent of the judge (wakil al-qadi) does not have litigation capacity by consensus, and that a mere messenger (rasul) or one ordered (ma'mur) to collect does not have it. The discussion ends with a note that the stronger view is that of the Sahibayn, which is also a narration from Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
وكيل بقبض الدين (wakīl bi-qabḍ al-dayn): An agent appointed to collect a debt from the debtor on behalf of the creditor.
خصم (khaṣm): A litigant or party in a legal proceeding, capable of representing the principal in court.
مبادلة (mubādalah): Exchange; here it refers to the fact that debt repayment is a form of exchange (payment of one thing for another).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why does the wakil for debt collection become a litigant?
A) Because the debtor must always deal with the agent.
B) Because debt collection is a form of exchange (mubadala) similar to shuf'a.
C) Because the wakil is the principal's full representative in all matters.
D) Because the Sahibayn agreed with Abu Hanifa.

Key Takeaway
The wakil bi-qabd al-dayn's litigation authority is disputed within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa grants it by qiyas to exchange agencies, while the Sahibayn restrict it to collection only.

intermediate
8 min
Agent's Litigation Standing: Repayment vs. Discharge
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agent's litigation role

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding an agent's standing as a litigant for repayment and discharge, and identify the mufta bihi (relied-upon) view.

Core Idea
A debt collection agent is a litigant only for repayment claims according to Abu Hanifa, but the relied-upon view denies him any litigation role.

When a debtor claims he has repaid or been discharged from a debt, the creditor's agent may be called to testify. But does the agent have standing to be a litigant in court? The Hanafi imams differed on this point. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the views of Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and resolves the dispute in favor of the companions' more cautious opinion.

Guided Path
1
State the Issue
Identify the legal question: Is an agent appointed to collect a debt (wakil bi-qabd al-dayn) considered a litigant (khasm) when the debtor claims repayment (ifa') or discharge (ibra')?
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's Position and Dalil
Note that Abu Hanifa distinguishes between repayment and discharge: the agent is a litigant for repayment but not for discharge. His evidence is accepted for repayment. The proof is qiyas on the agent for preemption (shuf'a), who is a litigant for proving the right but not for surrendering it.
The illa is that the agent's authority is to benefit the principal; discharge nullifies the right, so it is outside the agency.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's Position and Dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the agent is not a litigant in either case. Their evidence is that the agent's role is purely custodial; litigation goes beyond his mandate. They prefer to suspend judgment (yuwaqaf) in all cases.
Their position is considered stronger by later scholars.
4
Analyze the Qiyas Argument
Examine the qiyas on the agent for preemption. The asl is that the preemption agent is a litigant for proving the right but not for discharging it. The far' is the debt collection agent. The illa is that both agents are appointed for a benefit; acts that nullify the principal's right are outside their authority.
The Sahibayn reject the analogy because they see a difference in the nature of the agency.
5
Determine the Tarjih and Mufta Bihi
The text states that the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) according to later Hanafi masters (al-Mahbubi, al-Nasafi, al-Mawsili, Sadr al-Shari'ah) is that the agent is not a litigant in any case. This is also the view in al-Hidayah.
Thus, the agent's evidence for repayment or discharge is not accepted; the debtor must prove his claim through other means.

Source Text

Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the legal capacity of an agent appointed to collect a debt to act as a litigant (خصم) in court. The central question is whether such an agent can present evidence or be a party to litigation when the debtor claims to have repaid the debt (إيفاء) or to have been discharged from it (إبراء). The text begins by citing al-Dhakhira's ruling that the agent becomes a litigant for the creditor in claims of repayment but not discharge. The reasoning is that repayment is a claim that proves the agent's own action (collection) and thus benefits the principal, whereas discharge would nullify the principal's right without benefit. This is compared by qiyas to the case of an agent for preemption (وكيل بأخذ الشفعة), who is a litigant for proving the preemption right but not for surrendering it. The text then notes that Shaykh al-Islam and others have mentioned this distinction, and that Imam Muhammad remained silent about discharge in his works. It then presents the view of Abu Hanifa that the agent's evidence for repayment is accepted, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the agent is not a litigant at all in either case, and that his evidence is suspended (يوقف). The passage evaluates the strength of these positions, noting that many later scholars prefer the view of the two companions as stronger (قولهما أقوى) and that the relied-upon view according to al-Hidayah is that there is no difference between repayment and discharge—meaning the agent is not a litigant for either. The text also discusses exceptions, such as when the principal is absent and the agent is appointed by a judge, where consensus holds that the agent has no litigation authority. Overall, the passage illustrates the Hanafi method of resolving internal differences through qiyas, istihsan, and reliance on the stronger opinion (tarjih).

Key Terms
وكيل بقبض الدين (wakīl bi-qabḍ al-dayn): An agent appointed specifically to collect a debt owed to the principal.
خصم (khaṣm): A litigant or party in a legal proceeding; one who can present evidence and argue in court.
إيفاء (īfā'): Repayment of a debt; the act of fulfilling the obligation by the debtor.
إبراء (ibrā'): Discharge or release from a debt; the creditor's act of forgiving the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) in the passage, what is the status of an agent for debt collection when the debtor claims discharge (إبراء)?
A) The agent is a litigant and his evidence for discharge is accepted.
B) The agent is not a litigant and his evidence for discharge is not accepted.
C) The agent is a litigant only if the principal is absent.
D) The agent's evidence is suspended until the principal confirms.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that an agent for debt collection has no litigation standing; his testimony is not accepted for either repayment or discharge claims.

Restrictions on the Debt Collection Agent's Authority
Listing restrictions on a debt collection agent's authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the actions a debt collection agent cannot perform without authorization and state when the agent's word is accepted or rejected concerning debt collection.

Core Idea
A debt collection agent cannot forgive, reduce, or postpone the debt without explicit permission; his word is accepted only where it does not bind the principal.

An agent appointed solely to collect a debt has a limited mandate: he must collect and remit to the principal, not alter the debt in any way. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lists specific prohibited actions and clarifies the evidentiary value of the agent's statements in various scenarios. Understanding these limits protects the principal from unauthorized modifications and ensures the agent stays within his authority.

Guided Path
1
List the prohibited actions
Identify all actions the agent cannot perform without authorization: granting discharge (ibrā'), reducing the debt (ḥaṭṭ), postponing (ta'jīl), taking a pledge (rahn), taking a guarantor (kafīl), accepting a transfer of debt (ḥawāla), and appointing a substitute (tawkīl).
These are modifications of the debt beyond mere collection.
2
Note the default rule on agent's statements
The agent's word is accepted regarding collection, loss, and payment to the principal, but only for the debtor's discharge; it does not allow the agent to seek recourse against the principal if a third party claims ownership.
The agent is a trustee (amīn) in these matters.
3
Analyze the exception when the principal denies the loan
From al-Wāqi'āt al-Ḥasāmiyya: if the principal denies making a loan, the agent's admission of collection is not accepted because it would impose liability on the principal. The fatwa follows this view.
Abū Yūsuf differed, but the fatwa is on the principal's side.
4
Distinguish between agents for collection and borrowing
The borrowing agent's admission of receipt is not accepted in disputes because it would impose liability for the loan on the principal, unlike the collection agent whose statement only benefits the debtor.
The difference lies in whether the statement imposes liability.
5
Apply additional rules from Kāfi al-Ḥākim
The agent's claim of collection during the principal's lifetime is not accepted without evidence. If the debt is transferred, the agent cannot collect from the transferee. Counterfeit money and other scenarios are discussed.
These rules further delineate the agent's authority and evidentiary burdens.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقدمنا شيئاً من أحكام الوكيل بالقبض من أنه لا يجوز إبراؤه ولا حطه ولا تأجيله ولا أخذه الرهن ولا الكفيل بشرط براءة الأصيل ولا قبول الحوالة ولا توكيله بغير إذن وتعميم وأنه يقبل قوله في دعوى القبض والهلاك في يده والدفع إلى موكله لكن في حق براءة المديون لا في حق الرجوع على الموكل على تقدير الاستحقاق حتى لو استحق إنسان ما أقر الوكيل بقبضه وضمن المستحق الوكيل فإنه لا يرجع الوكيل على موكله؛ كذا في الصغرى. ويستثنى من قبول إقراره بالقبض على موكله مسألة على المفتى به. قال في الواقعات الحسامية : إذا قال لآخر إن فلاناً قد أقرضك ألفاً فوكلتك بقبضها منه ثم قال الوكيل قبضت وصدقه المقرض وأنكر الموكل فالقول للموكل وعن أبي يوسف أن القول للوكيل. وجه الأول أن المقرض يدعي على الموكل ثبوت القرض وهو ينكر، وجه قول أبي يوسف أن الموكل سلط الوكيل على ذلك فينفذ عليه إقراره كما لو وكله بقبض الدين من مدیونه فقال قبضت والفتوى على الأول الوكيل بقبض الدين إذا قال قبضت ودفعت إلى الموكل فالقول له مع اليمين لأنه أمين أخبر عن تنفيذ الأمانة من حيث لا يلزم الموكل ضمان بخلاف الوكيل بالاستقراض إذا وقع التنازع بينه وبين موكله فالقول للموكل لأن الوكيل يريد إلزامه ضمان القرض فلا يلزمه بقوله ا هـ . وفي كافي الحاكم : ولو وكل رجلاً في دينه كان وكيلاً بقبضه، ولو قال الوكيل قد برئ إلى الغريم كان إقراراً منه بقبضه، وكذا إذا أقيمت عليه البينة بذلك، ولو قال الوكيل بالقبض قبضت في حياة الموكل ودفعت إليه لم يقبل إلا ببينة، ولو احتال الطالب بالمال على آخر لم يكن للوكيل بالقبض أن يقبضه من المحتال عليه ولا من الأول، فإن توى المال ورجع إلى الأول فالوكيل على وكالته، وكذا لو اشترى الموكل بالمال عبداً من المطلوب فاستحق من يده أو رده بعيب بقضاء بعد القبض أو بغير قضاء قبل القبض أو بخيار فالوكيل على وكالته وكذا لو كان قبض الدراهم فوجدها زيوفاً ولو أخذ الطالب منه كفيلاً لم يكن للوكيل أن يتقاضى الكفيل والمقبوض في يد الوكيل بمنزلة الوديعة، ولو وجده الكفيل زيوفاً أو ستوقة فرده فإنه ينبغي أن يضمن قياساً ولكن أستحسن
And we have previously presented some of the rulings of the agent for collection: that he may not grant discharge, nor reduce, nor postpone, nor take a pledge, nor a guarantor with the condition that the principal is released, nor accept a transfer of debt, nor appoint a substitute without permission or general authorization; and that his statement is accepted in claims of collection, loss in his hand, and payment to his principal, but in the right of the debtor's release, not in the right of recourse against the principal in case of entitlement, so that if someone claims the right to what the agent admitted collecting, and the entitled party holds the agent liable, the agent cannot recourse against his principal; as in al-Sughra. And an exception to the acceptance of his admission of collection against his principal is a case according to the fatwa. Al-Waqi'at al-Hasamiyya said: If he says to another, 'So-and-so has loaned you a thousand, so I appoint you to collect it from him,' then the agent says 'I collected' and the lender confirms but the principal denies, then the statement is the principal's; and from Abu Yusuf that the statement is the agent's. The reasoning for the first is that the lender claims the loan is established against the principal, and he denies. The reasoning for Abu Yusuf's position is that the principal authorized the agent for that, so his admission is binding on him, just as if he appointed him to collect a debt from his debtor and he said 'I collected'. And the fatwa is on the first. The agent for debt collection, if he says 'I collected and paid to the principal', his statement is accepted with an oath, because he is a trustee reporting the execution of the trust without imposing liability on the principal, unlike the agent for borrowing when a dispute arises between him and his principal, the statement is the principal's because the agent intends to impose liability for the loan on him, so it is not imposed by his statement, end. And in Kafi al-Hakim: If he appointed a man for his debt, he becomes an agent for its collection. If the agent says, 'He has been discharged to the debtor,' it is an admission of collection by him, and similarly if evidence is established against him to that effect. If the agent for collection says, 'I collected during the principal's lifetime and paid to him,' it is not accepted without evidence. If the claimant transferred the money to another, the agent for collection cannot collect from the transferee nor from the first. If the money is lost and recourse returns to the first, the agent remains on his agency. Similarly, if the principal bought a slave from the debtor with the money, and it is claimed from his hand or he returned it due to a defect by a judgment after collection or without judgment before collection or by option, the agent remains on his agency. Similarly, if he collected dirhams and found them counterfeit. If the claimant took a guarantor from him, the agent cannot claim from the guarantor. What is collected in the agent's hand is like a deposit. If the guarantor finds them counterfeit or deficient and returns them, then he should be liable by qiyas, but I apply istihsan.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings regarding the powers and limitations of an agent appointed to collect a debt. It begins by listing actions the agent cannot perform without explicit authorization: he cannot grant a discharge (إبراء), reduce the debt (حط), postpone payment (تأجيل), take a pledge (رهن) or guarantor (كفيل) with the condition that the principal is released, accept a transfer of debt (حوالة), or appoint a substitute (توكيل) without permission. These restrictions stem from the agent's limited mandate: his task is purely to collect the debt, not to modify it. The text then addresses the admissibility of the agent's statements. Generally, the agent's word is accepted regarding collection, loss, and payment to the principal, but this acceptance only benefits the debtor (it proves discharge) and does not bind the principal if a third party claims ownership of the collected property. For example, if someone proves that the collected item belongs to them, the agent cannot seek recourse from his principal. An exception is provided from al-Waqi'at al-Hasamiyya: if a principal appoints an agent to collect a loan he supposedly made to someone, and the agent admits collection while the lender confirms but the principal denies, the principal's word is taken (fatwa), contrary to a view of Abu Yusuf. The key distinction is that the agent's admission would impose liability on the principal, so it is not accepted. Finally, the passage quotes Kafi al-Hakim with additional rules: if the agent claims to have collected and paid during the principal's lifetime, it is not accepted without evidence; if the debt is transferred to another, the agent cannot collect from the transferee; and if the collected money is counterfeit, there are rules about liability. The passage exemplifies how the Hanafi school carefully delineates the agent's authority to protect the principal's interests while facilitating debt recovery.

Key Terms
إبراء (ibrā'): A legal discharge or forgiveness of a debt, releasing the debtor from the obligation.
حط (ḥaṭṭ): Reduction of the debt amount; forgiving part of the debt.
تأجيل (ta'jīl): Postponement of the debt's due date; granting an extension.
وكيل بالاستقراض (wakīl bi-al-istiqrāḍ): An agent appointed to borrow money on behalf of the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in the dispute from al-Waqi'at al-Hasamiyya, whose word is taken as the fatwa?
A) The agent's
B) The principal's
C) The lender's
D) Both the principal and lender's

Key Takeaway
A debt collection agent cannot alter the debt terms, and his word is accepted only where it does not impose liability on the principal.

Accepting the Agent's Claim of Receipt: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agent's claim of receipt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the agent's claim of receipt when the lender confirms but the principal denies, and identify the mufta bihi position with its legal reasoning.

Core Idea
When the principal denies the agency, his word is taken over the agent's claim, even with creditor confirmation.

In Hanafi law, an agent (wakil) is generally trusted in matters of receipt. However, a critical exception arises when the principal disputes the very existence of the loan or agency. This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on whose statement prevails. We will dissect the reasoning and understand why the fatwa follows Abu Hanifa, shifting the burden of proof to the lender.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their positions
There are three persons: the principal (muwakkil), the agent (wakil), and the lender (muqrid). The agent claims he collected the debt. The lender confirms the agent's claim. The principal denies the agency or the underlying loan.
This is a triangular dispute where the principal is the defendant.
2
State the two opinions
Abu Hanifa holds that the principal's statement (denial) is accepted. Abu Yusuf holds that the agent's statement (claim of receipt) is accepted, binding the principal.
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) sometimes disagree; here only Abu Yusuf's view is mentioned as opposing.
3
Analyze the reasoning (dalil) for each
Abu Hanifa's reasoning: The lender is claiming against the principal (that the loan exists and was collected by the agent). The principal denies this; so the claim is against the denier, and the denier's statement is taken with oath. Abu Yusuf's reasoning: The principal authorized the agent, so the agent's acknowledgement (iqrar) is binding on the principal, analogous to when the agent collects from a debtor who admits the debt.
The key distinction is whether the principal acknowledges the agency itself. In the normal case, the principal admits the agency, so the agent's statement binds him. Here, the principal denies the very agency or the debt, so the analogy fails.
4
Determine the mufta bihi and practical effect
The fatwa is on Abu Hanifa's view. The principal's denial is accepted, meaning the lender must prove the existence of the loan against the principal. The agent's statement alone, even with lender confirmation, does not bind the principal.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that the principal can avoid payment unless the lender provides independent proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستثنى من قبول إقراره بالقبض على موكله مسألة على المفتى به. قال في الواقعات الحسامية : إذا قال لآخر إن فلاناً قد أقرضك ألفاً فوكلتك بقبضها منه ثم قال الوكيل قبضت وصدقه المقرض وأنكر الموكل فالقول للموكل وعن أبي يوسف أن القول للوكيل. وجه الأول أن المقرض يدعي على الموكل ثبوت القرض وهو ينكر، وجه قول أبي يوسف أن الموكل سلط الوكيل على ذلك فينفذ عليه إقراره كما لو وكله بقبض الدين من مدیونه فقال قبضت والفتوى على الأول
And an exception is made from the acceptance of his acknowledgement of receipt against his principal in a case, according to the fatwa. He said in al-Waqi'at al-Husamiyya: When someone says to another, 'Verily so-and-so has lent you a thousand, so I appoint you as my agent to collect it from him,' then the agent says 'I have collected it,' and the lender confirms him, but the principal denies, then the statement is that of the principal. And from Abu Yusuf that the statement is that of the agent. The reasoning of the first is that the lender is claiming against the principal the establishment of the loan, and he denies it. The reasoning of Abu Yusuf's statement is that the principal authorized the agent over that, so his acknowledgement is effective against him, just as if he appointed him as agent to collect a debt from his debtor and he said 'I collected it.' And the fatwa is on the first.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the acceptance of the agent's (wakil) claim of having collected a debt when the creditor confirms but the principal denies. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa: the principal's denial is accepted because the creditor's claim (that the principal owes him) is disputed by the principal. Abu Yusuf analogizes to the normal case where the agent's claim of collection is accepted, but here the principal disputes the very existence of the debt or agency. The fatwa follows Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
الوكيل (al-wakil): Agent appointed by the principal to perform an act on his behalf.
المقرض (al-muqrid): The lender who gave the loan and now confirms the agent's claim of receipt.
الموكل (al-muwakkil): The principal who appointed the agent and denies the agency or receipt.
القبض (al-qabd): Taking possession or receipt of the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Abu Yusuf's position: the agent's statement is accepted
B) Abu Hanifa's position: the principal's statement is accepted
C) Both positions are practiced equally
D) The lender's statement is accepted

Key Takeaway
When the principal denies the agency, his denial is accepted against the agent's claim of receipt, shifting the burden of proof to the lender.

The Trustee Status of the Debt Collection Agent
Rulings on the agent's statement and liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule regarding the agent for debt collection's statement, identify the exception for borrowing agents, and apply the rule to scenarios involving acknowledgement of discharge and claims of collection during the principal's lifetime.

Core Idea
The debt collection agent is a trustee: his statement is accepted, unless it imposes liability on the principal.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lays down the foundational principle distinguishing the agent for debt collection from the agent for borrowing. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining liability and trust in agency relationships.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agent types
Note the two agents: wakil bi qabd al-dayn (collection) and wakil bil istiqraz (borrowing).
2
State the ruling for each
For collection: statement accepted with oath. For borrowing: statement of principal accepted.
Reason: collection agent does not impose liability; borrowing agent does.
3
Apply to agent's acknowledgement
If the agent says 'the debtor is discharged,' it is an acknowledgement of receipt.
Consistent with the trustee principle.
4
Recognize the exception
If the agent claims collection during the principal's lifetime, it requires evidence.
Why? Because the principal could have challenged during his life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوكيل بقبض الدين إذا قال قبضت ودفعت إلى الموكل فالقول له مع اليمين لأنه أمين أخبر عن تنفيذ الأمانة من حيث لا يلزم الموكل ضمان بخلاف الوكيل بالاستقراض إذا وقع التنازع بينه وبين موكله فالقول للموكل لأن الوكيل يريد إلزامه ضمان القرض فلا يلزمه بقوله ا هـ . وفي كافي الحاكم : ولو وكل رجلاً في دينه كان وكيلاً بقبضه، ولو قال الوكيل قد برئ إلى الغريم كان إقراراً منه بقبضه، وكذا إذا أقيمت عليه البينة بذلك، ولو قال الوكيل بالقبض قبضت في حياة الموكل ودفعت إليه لم يقبل إلا ببينة، ولو احتال الطالب بالمال على آخر لم يكن للوكيل بالقبض أن يقبضه من المحتال عليه ولا من الأول، فإن توى المال ورجع إلى الأول فالوكيل على وكالته، وكذا لو اشترى الموكل بالمال عبداً من المطلوب فاستحق من يده أو رده بعيب بقضاء بعد القبض أو بغير قضاء قبل القبض أو بخيار فالوكيل على وكالته وكذا لو كان قبض الدراهم فوجدها زيوفاً ولو أخذ الطالب منه كفيلاً لم يكن للوكيل أن يتقاضى الكفيل والمقبوض في يد الوكيل بمنزلة الوديعة، ولو وجده الكفيل زيوفاً أو ستوقة فرده فإنه ينبغي أن يضمن قياساً ولكن أستحسن أن لا أضمنه. أمره بقبض دينه وأن لا يقبضه إلا جميعاً فقبضه إلا درهماً لم يجز قبضه على الأمر وله الرجوع على الغريم بكله، وكذا لا تقبض درهماً دون درهم ا هـ . وفي الذخيرة : ولو لم يكن للغريم بينة على الإيفاء فقضى عليه وقبضه الوكيل فضاع منه ثم برهن المطلوب على الإيفاء فلا سبيل له على الوكيل وإنما يرجع على الموكل لأن يده يده ا هـ .
The agent for collecting a debt, when he says 'I collected and paid to the principal,' his statement is accepted with an oath, because he is a trustee informing about the execution of the trust in a way that does not oblige the principal to guarantee, unlike the agent for borrowing when a dispute occurs between him and his principal, then the statement is of the principal because the agent wants to oblige him the guarantee of the loan, so it is not imposed by his statement. End. In Kafi al-Hakim: If someone appoints a man over his debt, he is an agent for its collection. If the agent says 'the debtor has been discharged,' it is an acknowledgement of his receipt. Similarly if evidence is established against him. If the agent for collection says 'I collected in the life of the principal and paid to him,' it is not accepted except with evidence. If the claimant transfers the money to another, the agent for collection may not collect it from the transferee nor from the first. Then if the money is lost and returns to the first, the agent remains on his agency. Similarly if the principal buys a slave from the debtor with the money, and it is claimed from his hand, or he returns it due to defect by judgment after possession, or without judgment before possession, or by option, the agent remains on his agency. Similarly if he collected the dirhams and found them counterfeit. If the claimant took a guarantor from him, the agent may not demand payment from the guarantor. What is in the hand of the agent is like a deposit. If the guarantor finds it counterfeit or deficient and returns it, then by analogy he should be liable, but I istihsan that I do not make him liable. He ordered him to collect his debt and not to collect it except all together, but he collected it except one dirham, his collection is not valid by order, and he has recourse against the debtor for all. Similarly, do not collect one dirham without one dirham. End. In al-Dhakhira: If the debtor has no evidence of payment, and judgment is passed against him, and the agent collects it, then it is lost, then the debtor proves payment, there is no recourse against the agent; he only returns to the principal because his hand is his hand. End.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage collects various rulings on the agent (wakil) appointed specifically for debt collection (wakil bil qabd). The general principle is that the agent is a trustee (amin), so his statement about having collected and paid is accepted with an oath, as long as it does not impose liability on the principal. The exception is the agent for borrowing (istikrad) where his statement would oblige the principal. The text then lists specific scenarios: the agent's acknowledgement of discharge counts as acknowledgement of receipt; if he claims collection during the principal's lifetime, it requires evidence; he cannot collect after a transfer (hawala); he cannot demand from a guarantor; he must follow instructions regarding partial collection. The final ruling from al-Dhakhira emphasizes that the agent's hand is considered the principal's hand, so if the debtor later proves payment, the agent is not liable but the principal must refund.

Key Terms
الوكيل بقبض الدين (al-wakil bi qabd al-dayn): Agent appointed specifically to collect a debt.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Acknowledgement; here, the agent's statement that the debtor is discharged counts as acknowledgement of receipt.
الوديعة (al-wadi'ah): Deposit; what is in the agent's hand is treated as a deposit, so he is not liable for loss unless negligent.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference; used here to override analogy in the case of the guarantor returning counterfeit money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When the agent for debt collection says 'I collected and paid to the principal,' his statement is accepted:
A) Without oath, because he is a trustee.
B) With an oath, because he is a trustee and does not impose liability on the principal.
C) Only if he produces evidence, because he is a trustee.
D) Only if the principal confirms.

Key Takeaway
The agent for debt collection is a trustee; his statement is accepted with an oath, unlike the agent for borrowing.

Special Scenarios in Debt Collection Agency
Rulings on various scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules regarding the agent's authority in cases of hawala, guarantor, counterfeit money, partial collection instructions, and post-collection proof of prior payment, and explain the use of istihsan in the guarantor case.

Core Idea
The agent's authority is limited to his mandate; special situations like transfer, guarantor, and partial collection have detailed rules preserving trust.

This lesson examines various practical scenarios that arise when a debt collection agent encounters transfers, guarantors, counterfeit money, or instructions for full payment. Understanding these rulings is essential for properly resolving disputes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the hawala scenario
If the principal transfers the debt to another (hawala), the agent cannot collect from the transferee nor from the original debtor. If the money is lost and returns to the original debtor, the agent remains on his agency.
The agent's authority is tied to the original debt.
2
Examine the guarantor case
If the principal takes a guarantor, the agent cannot demand from the guarantor. What is in the agent's hand is like a deposit.
The agent's relationship is with the principal, not the guarantor.
3
Analyze the counterfeit money ruling
If the agent collected counterfeit dirhams and the guarantor finds them counterfeit and returns them, by analogy the agent should be liable, but istihsan (juristic preference) rules no liability.
Istihsan overrides strict qiyas to avoid hardship.
4
Apply the partial collection rule
If the principal orders the agent to collect only all at once, and he collects less, the collection is not valid per the order, and he can still claim the full amount from the debtor.
The agent must follow instructions exactly.
5
Interpret the post-collection proof
If the debtor later proves he had already paid before the collection, the agent is not liable; the principal must return the money to the debtor.
The agent's hand is the principal's hand; the principal must bear the loss.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوكيل بقبض الدين إذا قال قبضت ودفعت إلى الموكل فالقول له مع اليمين لأنه أمين أخبر عن تنفيذ الأمانة من حيث لا يلزم الموكل ضمان بخلاف الوكيل بالاستقراض إذا وقع التنازع بينه وبين موكله فالقول للموكل لأن الوكيل يريد إلزامه ضمان القرض فلا يلزمه بقوله ا هـ . وفي كافي الحاكم : ولو وكل رجلاً في دينه كان وكيلاً بقبضه، ولو قال الوكيل قد برئ إلى الغريم كان إقراراً منه بقبضه، وكذا إذا أقيمت عليه البينة بذلك، ولو قال الوكيل بالقبض قبضت في حياة الموكل ودفعت إليه لم يقبل إلا ببينة، ولو احتال الطالب بالمال على آخر لم يكن للوكيل بالقبض أن يقبضه من المحتال عليه ولا من الأول، فإن توى المال ورجع إلى الأول فالوكيل على وكالته، وكذا لو اشترى الموكل بالمال عبداً من المطلوب فاستحق من يده أو رده بعيب بقضاء بعد القبض أو بغير قضاء قبل القبض أو بخيار فالوكيل على وكالته وكذا لو كان قبض الدراهم فوجدها زيوفاً ولو أخذ الطالب منه كفيلاً لم يكن للوكيل أن يتقاضى الكفيل والمقبوض في يد الوكيل بمنزلة الوديعة، ولو وجده الكفيل زيوفاً أو ستوقة فرده فإنه ينبغي أن يضمن قياساً ولكن أستحسن أن لا أضمنه. أمره بقبض دينه وأن لا يقبضه إلا جميعاً فقبضه إلا درهماً لم يجز قبضه على الأمر وله الرجوع على الغريم بكله، وكذا لا تقبض درهماً دون درهم ا هـ . وفي الذخيرة : ولو لم يكن للغريم بينة على الإيفاء فقضى عليه وقبضه الوكيل فضاع منه ثم برهن المطلوب على الإيفاء فلا سبيل له على الوكيل وإنما يرجع على الموكل لأن يده يده ا هـ .
The agent for collecting a debt, when he says 'I collected and paid to the principal,' his statement is accepted with an oath, because he is a trustee informing about the execution of the trust in a way that does not oblige the principal to guarantee, unlike the agent for borrowing when a dispute occurs between him and his principal, then the statement is of the principal because the agent wants to oblige him the guarantee of the loan, so it is not imposed by his statement. End. In Kafi al-Hakim: If someone appoints a man over his debt, he is an agent for its collection. If the agent says 'the debtor has been discharged,' it is an acknowledgement of his receipt. Similarly if evidence is established against him. If the agent for collection says 'I collected in the life of the principal and paid to him,' it is not accepted except with evidence. If the claimant transfers the money to another, the agent for collection may not collect it from the transferee nor from the first. Then if the money is lost and returns to the first, the agent remains on his agency. Similarly if the principal buys a slave from the debtor with the money, and it is claimed from his hand, or he returns it due to defect by judgment after possession, or without judgment before possession, or by option, the agent remains on his agency. Similarly if he collected the dirhams and found them counterfeit. If the claimant took a guarantor from him, the agent may not demand payment from the guarantor. What is in the hand of the agent is like a deposit. If the guarantor finds it counterfeit or deficient and returns it, then by analogy he should be liable, but I istihsan that I do not make him liable. He ordered him to collect his debt and not to collect it except all together, but he collected it except one dirham, his collection is not valid by order, and he has recourse against the debtor for all. Similarly, do not collect one dirham without one dirham. End. In al-Dhakhira: If the debtor has no evidence of payment, and judgment is passed against him, and the agent collects it, then it is lost, then the debtor proves payment, there is no recourse against the agent; he only returns to the principal because his hand is his hand. End.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage collects various rulings on the agent (wakil) appointed specifically for debt collection (wakil bil qabd). The general principle is that the agent is a trustee (amin), so his statement about having collected and paid is accepted with an oath, as long as it does not impose liability on the principal. The exception is the agent for borrowing (istikrad) where his statement would oblige the principal. The text then lists specific scenarios: the agent's acknowledgement of discharge counts as acknowledgement of receipt; if he claims collection during the principal's lifetime, it requires evidence; he cannot collect after a transfer (hawala); he cannot demand from a guarantor; he must follow instructions regarding partial collection. The final ruling from al-Dhakhira emphasizes that the agent's hand is considered the principal's hand, so if the debtor later proves payment, the agent is not liable but the principal must refund.

Key Terms
الوكيل بقبض الدين (al-wakil bi qabd al-dayn): Agent appointed specifically to collect a debt.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Acknowledgement; here, the agent's statement that the debtor is discharged counts as acknowledgement of receipt.
الوديعة (al-wadi'ah): Deposit; what is in the agent's hand is treated as a deposit, so he is not liable for loss unless negligent.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference; used here to override analogy in the case of the guarantor returning counterfeit money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the guarantor returns counterfeit money to the agent, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The guarantor is liable for the loss.
B) The agent is liable for the loss.
C) The guarantor is not liable due to istihsan.
D) The principal must bear the loss.

Key Takeaway
The agent for debt collection must follow his mandate precisely; special cases like hawala, guarantor, and partial collection have detailed rules that uphold the trust character of the agency.

The Agent for Receiving a Specific Item: Pure Trustee and Istihsan
Detailed ruling distinguishing the agent for receiving a specific item as pure trustee, with istihsan override

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the agent for receiving a specific item is a pure trustee (amin mahd) and apply the istihsan ruling when the possessor produces evidence against the agent.

Core Idea
The wakil bi qabd al-ayn is a pure trustee, not a litigant; istihsan stays possession when the opponent has proof.

In Hanafi law, not all agents are equal. An agent appointed to receive a specific item (like a slave or deposit) has a limited scope: he is a pure trustee, not an advocate. This lesson explores that distinction through a key scenario: what happens when the possessor of the item claims the principal sold it to him? The Hanafi school uses istihsan to stay the matter, overriding strict qiyas. You will learn the reasoning behind this preference and how it applies to other claims like divorce and manumission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agent's legal capacity
Read the statement: the agent for receiving a specific item is not an agent for litigation because he is a pure trustee (amin mahd) and receipt is not an exchange. He resembles a messenger (rasul).
This distinguishes him from the wakil bi qabd al-dayn (agent for debt collection) who has authority to sue.
2
Analyze the conflict: qiyas vs. istihsan
In the scenario where the possessor claims the principal sold the item and brings evidence, qiyas would give the item to the agent because the evidence is not against the agent. The text says this is not considered. Instead, istihsan stays the matter until the absentee appears.
Istihsan overrides qiyas due to a hidden analogy: the agent is a 'contestant in shortening his possession'.
3
Uncover the illa of istihsan
The reason (illa) for istihsan is that the agent stands in place of the principal for receipt, so his possession is 'shortened' by the claim. He becomes a contestant regarding the duration of his possession.
This is a khafiyyah (hidden) illa, extracted by tahqiq al-manat.
4
Apply to other claims: divorce, manumission, pledge
The same istihsan applies when the opponent claims divorce or manumission: the evidence is accepted to shorten the agent's possession until the principal appears. It does not nullify the divorce or manumission itself.
The text emphasizes that the acceptance is in shortening possession, not in establishing the claim itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وبقبض العين ) أي الوكيل بقبض العين لا يكون وكيلاً بالخصومة لأنه أمين محض والقبض ليس بمبادلة فأشبه الرسول حتى إن من وكل وكيلاً بقبض عبد له فأقام ذو اليد البينة أن الموكل باعه إياه وقف الأمر حتى يحضر الغائب وهذا استحسان، والقياس أن يدفع إلى الوكيل لأن البينة قامت لا على الخصم فلم تعتبر وجه الاستحسان أنه خصم في قصر يده لقيامه مقام الموكل في القبض فتقصر يده حتى لو حضر البائع تعاد البينة على البيع وصار كما إذا أقام البينة على أن الموكل عزله عن ذلك فإنها تقبل في قصر يده كذا هذا، وكذا الإعتاق والطلاق وغير ذلك معناه إذا أقامت المرأة البينة على الطلاق والعبد أو الأمة على الإعتاق على الوكيل بنقلهم تقبل في قصر يدهم حتى يحضر الغائب استحساناً دون العتق والطلاق كما إذا ادعى ذو اليد الارتهان من الموكل وبرهن تقصر يد الوكيل عن القبض. وفي كافي الحاكم ولو وكل رجل عبد رجل بقبض وديعة له عنده مولاه أو عند غيره فباع المولى العبد أو أعتقه أو كانت أمة فولدت فالوكيل على وكالته وإذا وكله بقبض عبد له عند رجل فقتل العبد خطأ كان للمستودع أن يأخذ فيمته من العاقلة وليس للوكيل قبضها كالثمن، ولو قتل عند الوكيل كان له أخذها، ولو جنى على العبد قبل أن يقبضه الوكيل فأخذ المستودع أرشها فللوكيل أن يقبض العبد دون الأرش، وكذا لو كان المستودع أجره بإذن مولاه لم يأخذ الوكيل أجره، وكذلك مهر الأمة إذا وطئت بشبهة، ولو وكله بقبض أمة أو شاة فولدت كان للوكيل أن يقبض الولد مع الأم، ولو ولدت قبل أن يوكله بقبضها لم يكن له قبض الولد وثمرة البستان بمنزلة .الولد. ولو كان المستودع باع الثمرة في رؤوس النخل بأمر رب الأرض لم يكن للوكيل أن يقبضها وكذلك ولد الجارية إذا كانت الوديعة مما يكال أو يوزن فوكله بقبضها ثم استهلكها رجل فقبض المستودع من المستهلك مثلها لم يكن للوكيل أخذه قياساً، ولكن أستحسن أن يأخذه ولا أراه مثل قيمة العبد، أرأيت لو أكلها المستودع أما كان للوكيل أخذ مثلها منه؟ وإذا وكله بقبض وديعة ثم قبضها الموكل ثم أودعها ثانياً لم يكن للوكيل قبضها علم أو لم يعلم وكذا لو قبضها الوكيل ودفعها إلى الموكل ثم أودعها الموكل فإن قبضها فلرب المال تضمينه أو تضمين المودع ، فإن ضمن الوكيل لم يرجع على المودع، وإن
His statement: (And for receiving a specific item) i.e., the agent for receiving a specific item is not an agent for litigation because he is a pure trustee and receiving is not an exchange, so he resembles a messenger. So much so that if one appoints an agent to receive his slave, then the possessor establishes evidence that the principal sold it to him, the matter is stayed until the absentee appears. This is istihsan. Analogy would require that it be given to the agent because the evidence was not against the opponent, so it is not considered. The face of istihsan is that he is a contestant in shortening his possession because he stands in place of the principal in receiving, so his possession is shortened. So much so that if the seller appears, the evidence on the sale is retried. And it becomes like when he establishes evidence that the principal removed him from that; then it is accepted in shortening his possession. Similarly for manumission and divorce, etc. Meaning when the woman establishes evidence of divorce, and the slave or slave-girl establishes evidence of manumission against the agent, it is accepted in shortening their possession until the absentee appears, by istihsan, not regarding the manumission and divorce themselves. Similarly if the possessor claims pledge from the principal and proves it, the agent's possession over the item is shortened. In Kafi al-Hakim: If a man appoints another's slave to collect a deposit from his master or someone else, and the master sells the slave or manumits him, or if female and she gives birth, the agent remains on his agency. If he appoints him to collect a slave from a man, and the slave is killed accidentally, the depositary may take its value from the 'aqilah, and the agent has no right to collect it like the price. If it is killed with the agent, he may collect it. If injury occurs to the slave before the agent takes possession, and the depositary takes the compensation, the agent may take possession of the slave but not the compensation. Similarly if the depositary hired him with the master's permission, the agent does not take the hire. Likewise the dowry of a female slave if she is copulated with due to shubha. If he appoints him to collect a female slave or a sheep and she gives birth, the agent may take possession of the offspring with the mother. If she gave birth before he appointed him, he may not take the offspring. The fruit of the garden is analogous to the offspring. If the depositary sold the fruit on the palm trees by order of the landowner, the agent may not collect it. Similarly for the offspring of a slave-girl. If the deposit is of things weighed or measured, and he appointed him to collect it, then a man consumed it, and the depositary collected something similar from the consumer, the agent may not take it by analogy, but I istihsan that he takes it, and I do not see it like the value of the slave. Do you see that if the depositary consumed it, would the agent not take something similar from him? If he appointed him to collect a deposit, then the principal took possession of it, then deposited it again, the agent may not take possession of it, whether he knew or not. Similarly if the agent took possession and gave it to the principal, then the principal deposited it, then if he takes possession, the owner of the property has the option to hold the agent or the depositary liable. If the agent is held liable, he does not have recourse against the depositary...
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage explains the legal status of an agent appointed to receive a specific item (wakil bi qabd al-ayn). Unlike the agent for debt collection, he is a pure trustee (amin mahd) and not an agent for litigation. The key distinction is that receiving a specific item is not an exchange (mubadala), so he is like a messenger. The text then provides several scenarios: if the possessor of the item claims the principal sold it to him and produces evidence, the matter is stayed until the absent principal appears (istihsan); analogy would give the item to the agent, but istihsan delays. Similar rules apply for claims of manumission, divorce, or pledge. Then rulings from Kafi al-Hakim cover situations involving slaves, deposits, offspring, fruits, and substitutions. The agent's authority is limited to receiving the specific item; he does not have authority over other rights like blood money, hire, or compensation for injury. The passage also discusses cases where the deposit is consumed and the depositary takes a substitute—analogy denies the agent the right to take it, but istihsan allows it.

Key Terms
الوكيل بقبض العين (al-wakil bi qabd al-ayn): Agent appointed to receive a specific item (e.g., a slave, a deposit).
أمين محض (amin mahd): Pure trustee; one who is entrusted with property but has no authority to dispose of it or litigate.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference; here used to stay the transfer of the item until the absent principal appears, overriding qiyas.
الرسول (al-rasul): Messenger; someone who merely conveys or receives without independent authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, an agent appointed to receive a specific item (wakil bi qabd al-ayn) is considered a:
A) Agent with full power to litigate.
B) Pure trustee (amin mahd), similar to a messenger.
C) Guarantor for the item's safety.
D) Agent with authority to sell the item.

Key Takeaway
The agent for receiving a specific item is a pure trustee, not a litigant; when the possessor produces evidence of a prior claim, istihsan stays the matter rather than following strict analogy.
