His statement: (And whoever claims that he is the agent of the absent person to collect his debt, and the debtor confirms him, then he is ordered to pay it to him) because he (the debtor) has confessed against himself, since what he receives is pure property of the creditor. Later in the book will come the ruling on if he claims fulfillment. It has preceded that the agent for collecting debt is an agent for exchange, ownership, and taking ownership, so there is no problem in the validity of the agency thereby. What is in al-Dhakhira of question and answer is as is not hidden, and the statement of the commentator 'This is a good question and the answer is not satisfactory...' is negligence of what he presented. And the meaning of 'order him' is compulsion upon him as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. His statement: (If the absent person appears and confirms him, otherwise the debtor pays the debt a second time) because the collection is not proven when he denies it, and the saying in that is his saying with his oath, so the payment is invalidated if his collection was not permissible at its occurrence. His statement: (And he has recourse with it against the agent if it remains) meaning the payer has recourse for what the agent collected if it remains in his hand, because it becomes his property and the right of the claimant is cut off. He made it absolute in remaining, so it includes constructive remaining such as if the agent consumed it, for it remains by the remaining of its substitute. Hence it is said in al-Khulasa: If he consumed it, he guarantees its equivalent. If the agent claims its destruction or payment to the principal, he is made to swear to that. If the principal dies and the creditor inherits from him or gifts it to him while it is existing in the agent's hand, he takes it from him in all cases. If it is destroyed, he guarantees it, except if he confirms him regarding the agency. His statement: (And if it is lost, no) meaning if the collected property is lost in the agent's hand, there is no recourse against him, because by his confirmation he acknowledged that he was rightful in taking it, and he is oppressed in this taking, and the oppressed does not oppress others. An objection was raised: If one of two sons confirms the debtor's claim of fulfillment to the deceased and the other denies, and the one who denies has recourse on him for half, then the debtor has recourse on the confirmer for half if the deceased has an estate other than the debt, because in his view the denier is unjust. The answer is that the recourse on the confirmer is because he confessed a debt against his father. His statement: (that he is a minor) But if among them there is an adult, and the guardian claims agency on behalf of the creditor, he does not need to appoint a guardian, and that is the case of the fatwa. Consider the author's statement: (So the debtor confirms him). Al-Ramli said: This excludes the case where he does not confirm him, whether he denies or remains silent, as this commentator will clarify in his commentary on his statement 'or he does not confirm him regarding the agency'. His statement: (And by it falls what is in al-Dhakhira of question and answer). Al-Zayla'i said: In the issue there is a kind of problem: that the agency to collect a debt is agency to borrow in meaning, because debts are repaid with their equivalents, so what the creditor takes from the debtor becomes a guarantee upon him, and he has the like from the debtor, so they meet by set-off, and agency to borrow is not valid. The answer is that the agency to collect a debt is a message to borrow in terms of meaning, not agency to borrow, because the agent to collect a debt must attribute the collection to his principal by saying 'So-and-so appointed me to collect what he has against you of debt', and the messenger in borrowing must attribute to the sender by saying 'He sent me to you and said to you: lend me', so what we claimed is correct: this is a message in meaning, and the message to borrow is permissible. Thus it is mentioned in al-Nihaya. (PAGE_312) 312 Book of Agency, Chapter of Agency in Litigation and Receipt, on the agent if it remains and if it is lost, no, except if he guarantees at the time of payment or does not confirm him regarding the agency, because he took it from me. Except if he guarantees at the time of payment, because the second taking is guaranteed in both their views, and this is a guarantee added to the state of taking, so it is valid like the guarantee of what he has against so-and-so. They said: It is permissible in his guarantee to emphasize and lighten. The meaning of emphasis is that the debtor guarantees the agent, so the implicit pronoun refers to the debtor, and the explicit to the agent. The meaning of lightening is that the agent guarantees the property he took, and its form is that the debtor says to the agent: You are his agent, but I am not safe that he will deny the agency and take from me a second time, and that becomes a debt upon him unjustly, so you guarantee that taken amount. Then it is valid. So the implicit pronoun refers to the agent, and the explicit to the property. What is in al-Nihaya that it is the opposite of emphasis is a mistake, for it implies that the implicit is for the agent and the explicit for the debtor, which is not correct. And if the explicit refers to the property, then the apparent meaning of the book is that by property is meant what he collected (the referent of the pronoun in 'lost' and before it), and it is not correct because what is in the agent's hand is a trust due to his confirmation of the agency, so it is not permissible to guarantee it, since guaranteeing trusts is invalid. So it must be that the intended meaning is what the creditor takes from the debtor a second time. The apparent meaning of the book is that there is no recourse against the agent in the case of destruction except if he guarantees. In al-Khulasa and al-Bazzaziyya: Except if the debtor said: I fear that if the creditor comes, he will deny you, and he guaranteed him; or the claimant of agency said: Take from me on condition that I discharge you from the debt, just as if the father says to the son-in-law when taking his daughter's dowry: I take from you on condition that I discharge you from my daughter's dower, then if the daughter takes the dowry from the son-in-law, the son-in-law has recourse against the father. This is it. So there are two causes for recourse upon destruction. Then know that it is valid to prove the agency by evidence with the debtor's acknowledgment of it, and it has parallels which we wrote in the benefits that evidence is only established against a denier except in issues we mentioned in the chapter of acknowledgment. The agent. His statement: (Or he does not confirm him regarding the agency and pays to him based on his claim) is conjuncted to 'he guarantees', meaning if he does not confirm him, then he has recourse against him, because he only paid him in hope of ratification, and when his hope is cut off, he has recourse against him. He made it absolute, so it includes the case of silence, because the principle is lack of confirmation. As for if he denies him, the recourse is more evident. In all cases, he does not have the right to reclaim until the absent person appears, because what was paid becomes a right of the absent person either apparently or potentially, so it becomes like if he paid to a volunteer in hope of ratification: he does not have the right to reclaim due to the possibility of ratification. This is in al-Hidaya. It is mentioned in Jami' al-Fusulayn two opinions regarding reclaiming from the volunteer. According to the one who says it, if he paid to a man to pay to the creditor, then he can reclaim because he is the debtor's agent, and because one who undertakes an action for a purpose does not have the right to revoke it as long as he has not despaired of his purpose. Similarly, he attributed it to al-Dhakhira. And this is a good question, and the answer is not satisfactory according to Abu Hanifa, for if he were a messenger, he would not have the right to litigate. End. His statement: (which he took) meaning what the creditor took from the debtor, not what the agent took, as will be pointed out later. His statement: (And because one who undertakes an action for a purpose) is conjuncted to his statement 'because he only paid to him...' so it is repelled.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling when someone claims to be the agent of an absent creditor to collect a debt from a debtor who confirms the claim. The commentator first explains that the judge orders payment because the debtor has confessed against himself, as the money is the creditor's pure right. He notes that the agency to collect debt is permissible as it involves exchange and ownership. He discusses an objection from al-Dhakhira about whether this is agency to borrow, and answers that it is a message to borrow in meaning, not agency to borrow, because the agent attributes the collection to his principal. If the absent principal later confirms the agency, the matter ends; otherwise, the debtor must pay again and has recourse against the agent if the property remains. The commentator details the conditions of recourse: if the collected property is lost in the agent's hand, no recourse unless the agent guaranteed at the time of payment or the debtor did not confirm the agency. He also explains the concepts of emphasis and lightening in guarantee, and provides examples. The passage concludes with the ruling that if the debtor does not confirm the agency, the payer has recourse for the amount paid. In all cases, the debtor cannot reclaim until the absent person appears because payment becomes a right of the absent person. The Hanafi position is that the payment is valid pending ratification.

Key Terms
وكيل بقبض الدين (wakīl bi-qabḍ al-dayn): An agent appointed to receive a debt from the debtor on behalf of the creditor.
رسالة بالاستقراض (risāla bi al-istiqrāḍ): A message to request a loan; the messenger asks the debtor to lend money to the sender.
استيفاء (istīfā'): Collection or receipt of the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi response to the objection that agency to collect debt is like agency to borrow?
A) It is indeed agency to borrow, but that is valid in Hanafi law.
B) It is a message to borrow, not an agency to borrow, because of the requirement to attribute collection to the principal.
C) The objection is rejected because the debtor's confirmation makes it valid.
D) The agency is valid as a general agency (wikala mutlaqa).

Key Takeaway
Payment to an alleged agent is valid pending ratification; it is a message to borrow, not an agency to borrow.

intermediate
14 min
Recourse of the Debtor – Conditions for Recovery from the Agent
Conditions for recourse and guarantee scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a debtor can recover money paid to an alleged agent after the principal denies the agency, including cases of destruction and guarantee.

Core Idea
The debtor's recourse depends on the money's existence or a guarantee; without these, no recourse upon destruction.

When an absent principal denies the agency, the debtor must pay the debt again. However, the debtor may have a right of recourse (ruju') against the alleged agent to recover the first payment. This lesson details the conditions for recourse: if the money remains in the agent's hand, the debtor can recover it; if it is lost, there is generally no recourse unless the agent provided a guarantee or the debtor did not confirm the agency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The debtor paid a person claiming agency, and later the absent principal appears and denies the agency. The debtor must pay the principal again.
This triggers the question of recourse against the agent.
2
Determine the condition of the money
If the money the agent took still exists (in hand or constructive existence, e.g., replaced by a similar item), the debtor has recourse against the agent for that specific property.
Constructive existence includes if the agent consumed it, then he owes its equivalent, which is considered remaining.
3
Analyze the case of loss
If the money is lost in the agent's hand without his fault, there is no recourse because the agent, by the debtor's confirmation, is considered a trustee (amin) and the debtor is considered 'oppressed' (mazlum) in paying again. The oppressed does not oppress others.
Exception: if the agent guaranteed at the time of payment or the debtor did not confirm the agency.
4
Understand the guarantee types
Two forms of guarantee are discussed: emphasis (tashdid) where the debtor guarantees the agent (i.e., the debtor takes responsibility), and lightening (takhfif) where the agent guarantees the property he took. The latter gives the debtor recourse even if the money is lost.
These are contractual stipulations at the time of payment.
5
Note the case of non-confirmation
If the debtor does not confirm the agency (denies or remains silent), he has immediate recourse against the agent upon paying the principal, because he paid in hope of ratification, and once that hope is cut off, he can recover.
In all cases, the debtor cannot reclaim directly from the principal because the first payment becomes a right of the absent person, albeit potentially conditional.

His statement: (And whoever claims that he is the agent of the absent person to collect his debt, and the debtor confirms him, then he is ordered to pay it to him) because he (the debtor) has confessed against himself, since what he receives is pure property of the creditor. His statement: (If the absent person appears and confirms him, otherwise the debtor pays the debt a second time) because the collection is not proven when he denies it, and the saying in that is his saying with his oath, so the payment is invalidated if his collection was not permissible at its occurrence. His statement: (And he has recourse with it against the agent if it remains) meaning the payer has recourse for what the agent collected if it remains in his hand, because it becomes his property and the right of the claimant is cut off. He made it absolute in remaining, so it includes constructive remaining such as if the agent consumed it, for it remains by the remaining of its substitute. Hence it is said in al-Khulasa: If he consumed it, he guarantees its equivalent. If the agent claims its destruction or payment to the principal, he is made to swear to that. If the principal dies and the creditor inherits from him or gifts it to him while it is existing in the agent's hand, he takes it from him in all cases. If it is destroyed, he guarantees it, except if he confirms him regarding the agency. His statement: (And if it is lost, no) meaning if the collected property is lost in the agent's hand, there is no recourse against him, because by his confirmation he acknowledged that he was rightful in taking it, and he is oppressed in this taking, and the oppressed does not oppress others. An objection was raised: If one of two sons confirms the debtor's claim of fulfillment to the deceased and the other denies, and the one who denies has recourse on him for half, then the debtor has recourse on the confirmer for half if the deceased has an estate other than the debt, because in his view the denier is unjust. The answer is that the recourse on the confirmer is because he confessed a debt against his father. His statement: (that he is a minor) But if among them there is an adult, and the guardian claims agency on behalf of the creditor, he does not need to appoint a guardian, and that is the case of the fatwa. Consider the author's statement: (So the debtor confirms him). Al-Ramli said: This excludes the case where he does not confirm him, whether he denies or remains silent, as this commentator will clarify in his commentary on his statement 'or he does not confirm him regarding the agency'. His statement: (And by it falls what is in al-Dhakhira of question and answer). Al-Zayla'i said: In the issue there is a kind of problem: that the agency to collect a debt is agency to borrow in meaning, because debts are repaid with their equivalents, so what the creditor takes from the debtor becomes a guarantee upon him, and he has the like from the debtor, so they meet by set-off, and agency to borrow is not valid. The answer is that the agency to collect a debt is a message to borrow in terms of meaning, not agency to borrow, because the agent to collect a debt must attribute the collection to his principal by saying 'So-and-so appointed me to collect what he has against you of debt', and the messenger in borrowing must attribute to the sender by saying 'He sent me to you and said to you: lend me', so what we claimed is correct: this is a message in meaning, and the message to borrow is permissible. Thus it is mentioned in al-Nihaya. (PAGE_312) 312 Book of Agency, Chapter of Agency in Litigation and Receipt, on the agent if it remains and if it is lost, no, except if he guarantees at the time of payment or does not confirm him regarding the agency, because he took it from me. Except if he guarantees at the time of payment, because the second taking is guaranteed in both their views, and this is a guarantee added to the state of taking, so it is valid like the guarantee of what he has against so-and-so. They said: It is permissible in his guarantee to emphasize and lighten. The meaning of emphasis is that the debtor guarantees the agent, so the implicit pronoun refers to the debtor, and the explicit to the agent. The meaning of lightening is that the agent guarantees the property he took, and its form is that the debtor says to the agent: You are his agent, but I am not safe that he will deny the agency and take from me a second time, and that becomes a debt upon him unjustly, so you guarantee that taken amount. Then it is valid. So the implicit pronoun refers to the agent, and the explicit to the property. What is in al-Nihaya that it is the opposite of emphasis is a mistake, for it implies that the implicit is for the agent and the explicit for the debtor, which is not correct. And if the explicit refers to the property, then the apparent meaning of the book is that by property is meant what he collected (the referent of the pronoun in 'lost' and before it), and it is not correct because what is in the agent's hand is a trust due to his confirmation of the agency, so it is not permissible to guarantee it, since guaranteeing trusts is invalid. So it must be that the intended meaning is what the creditor takes from the debtor a second time. The apparent meaning of the book is that there is no recourse against the agent in the case of destruction except if he guarantees. In al-Khulasa and al-Bazzaziyya: Except if the debtor said: I fear that if the creditor comes, he will deny you, and he guaranteed him; or the claimant of agency said: Take from me on condition that I discharge you from the debt, just as if the father says to the son-in-law when taking his daughter's dowry: I take from you on condition that I discharge you from my daughter's dower, then if the daughter takes the dowry from the son-in-law, the son-in-law has recourse against the father. This is it. So there are two causes for recourse upon destruction. Then know that it is valid to prove the agency by evidence with the debtor's acknowledgment of it, and it has parallels which we wrote in the benefits that evidence is only established against a denier except in issues we mentioned in the chapter of acknowledgment. The agent. His statement: (Or he does not confirm him regarding the agency and pays to him based on his claim) is conjuncted to 'he guarantees', meaning if he does not confirm him, then he has recourse against him, because he only paid him in hope of ratification, and when his hope is cut off, he has recourse against him. He made it absolute, so it includes the case of silence, because the principle is lack of confirmation. As for if he denies him, the recourse is more evident. In all cases, he does not have the right to reclaim until the absent person appears, because what was paid becomes a right of the absent person either apparently or potentially, so it becomes like if he paid to a volunteer in hope of ratification: he does not have the right to reclaim due to the possibility of ratification. This is in al-Hidaya. It is mentioned in Jami' al-Fusulayn two opinions regarding reclaiming from the volunteer. According to the one who says it, if he paid to a man to pay to the creditor, then he can reclaim because he is the debtor's agent, and because one who undertakes an action for a purpose does not have the right to revoke it as long as he has not despaired of his purpose. Similarly, he attributed it to al-Dhakhira. And this is a good question, and the answer is not satisfactory according to Abu Hanifa, for if he were a messenger, he would not have the right to litigate. End. His statement: (which he took) meaning what the creditor took from the debtor, not what the agent took, as will be pointed out later. His statement: (And because one who undertakes an action for a purpose) is conjuncted to his statement 'because he only paid to him...' so it is repelled.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling when someone claims to be the agent of an absent creditor to collect a debt from a debtor who confirms the claim. The commentator first explains that the judge orders payment because the debtor has confessed against himself, as the money is the creditor's pure right. He notes that the agency to collect debt is permissible as it involves exchange and ownership. He discusses an objection from al-Dhakhira about whether this is agency to borrow, and answers that it is a message to borrow in meaning, not agency to borrow, because the agent attributes the collection to his principal. If the absent principal later confirms the agency, the matter ends; otherwise, the debtor must pay again and has recourse against the agent if the property remains. The commentator details the conditions of recourse: if the collected property is lost in the agent's hand, no recourse unless the agent guaranteed at the time of payment or the debtor did not confirm the agency. He also explains the concepts of emphasis and lightening in guarantee, and provides examples. The passage concludes with the ruling that if the debtor does not confirm the agency, the payer has recourse for the amount paid. In all cases, the debtor cannot reclaim until the absent person appears because payment becomes a right of the absent person. The Hanafi position is that the payment is valid pending ratification.

Key Terms
رجوع (rujū'): Right to recourse; the debtor's right to recover what he paid from the agent if the principal denies the agency.
تشديد (tashdīd): Emphasis in guarantee; the debtor guarantees the agent, meaning the debtor takes responsibility.
تخفيف (takhfīf): Lightening in guarantee; the agent guarantees the property he took, giving the debtor recourse even if lost.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the debtor have recourse against the agent even if the collected money is lost?
A) Always, because the agent is liable.
B) Only if the debtor confirms the agency.
C) Only if the agent guaranteed at payment or the debtor did not confirm the agency.
D) Only if the lost money is replaced.

Key Takeaway
The debtor's recourse against the agent is conditional on the money's existence or a guarantee; without these, loss of property extinguishes recourse.

intermediate
10 min
Debtor's Recovery from Claimed Agent When Agency Unconfirmed
Commentary on debtor's payment to a claimed agent and conditions for recourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a debtor can recover money paid to a claimed agent and apply the ruling to scenarios involving guarantee (ḍamān) and loss.

Core Idea
A debtor who pays a claimed agent can recover only if the money remains and no explicit guarantee was given.

This lesson examines a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding payment to a person who claims to be the creditor's agent. The key concern is whether the debtor can recover his money if the agency turns out to be false or unconfirmed. The ruling draws on the principle that payment was made hoping for ratification, so recovery is possible only under specific conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The core act is the debtor paying money to a person who claims to be the creditor's agent for collection. The ruling is that this payment is valid as an act of agency if subsequently ratified, but the debtor's right to recover depends on conditions.
If the agency is later denied, the payment may need to be undone.
2
Locate the condition for recovery: money extant vs. lost
If the money is still in the agent's possession (qāʿim), the debtor can recover it. If it has been lost (ḍāʿa), the debtor cannot recover unless the agent accepted liability (ḍamān) at the time of payment.
This is the default rule: extant = recovery; lost = no recovery without guarantee.
3
Understand the guarantee exception
If the agent guaranteed the money at the time of receipt (e.g., by saying 'I guarantee it'), then even if lost, the debtor can recover from the agent. The guarantee converts the trust (amāna) into a liability.
The guarantee must be explicit; a silent payment does not create liability.
4
Apply the qiyas to the unauthorized agent (fuḍūlī)
The ruling is analogized to payment made to an unauthorized agent hoping for ratification. In both cases, the payer cannot recover until the hope of ratification is cut off (i.e., the principal denies the agency).
This explains why recovery is not allowed before the principal returns or denies.

دعوى الرملي هنا. وفي حاشية المنح أنه غلط وقال في حاشية هذا الكتاب : صوابه وقيل لا لأن من باشر التصرف لغرض الخ. قوله : (وفي كافي الحاكم وإذا قبض رجل وديعة رجل الخ) قال في جامع الفصولين فلو حضر ربه وكذبه في الوكالة لا يرجع المودع على الوكيل لو صدقه ولم يشرط الضمان عليه وإلا رجع بعينه لو قائماً، وبقيمته لو هالكاً. أقول : لو صدقه ودفعه بلا شرط ينبغي أن يرجع على الوكيل لو قائماً إذ غرضه لم يحصل فله نقضه على قياس ما مر في الهداية من أن المديون بما دفعه إلى وكيل صدقه لو باقياً، كذا هذا والله تعالى أعلم .اهـ. قلت: ما بحثه مستفاد من
Book of Agency, Chapter on Agency in Litigation and Receipt. He has recourse against the agent if the [property] remains, and if it is lost, no, except if he made him liable [by guarantee] at the time of payment, or if he did not confirm him regarding the agency. Because he took it from me. Except if he made him liable at the time of payment, because what is taken secondly is subject to liability in their claim, and this is a guarantee attached to the state of receipt, so it is valid like a guarantee for what is owed to him by so-and-so. They said: In 'dammanahu' both tashdid and takhfif are permissible. The meaning of tashdid is that the debtor guarantees the agent, so the implicit pronoun refers to the debtor, and the explicit to the agent. The meaning of takhfif is that the agent guarantees the money he took, and its form is that the debtor says to the agent: 'You are his agent, but I am not safe that he will deny the agency and take from me a second time, and that becomes a debt upon him wrongfully, so he guarantees that taken [money]' – so it is valid. Then the implicit pronoun refers to the agent, and the explicit to the money. What is in al-Nihaya that it is the opposite of the tashdid is a mistake, because it would require that the implicit is for the agent and the explicit for the debtor, which is not correct. And if the explicit refers to the money, then the apparent meaning of the book is that what is intended by 'money' is what he [the agent] took – the referent of the pronoun in 'yad1a'a' [is lost] and what precedes it – and this is not correct, because what is in the hand of the agent is a trust due to his confirmation of the agency, so it is not permissible to guarantee it, since the guarantee of trusts is invalid. So it is determined that what is intended is what the creditor takes from him a second time. The apparent meaning of the book is that there is no recourse against the agent in the case of loss except if he guaranteed. And in al-Khulasa and al-Bazzaziyya: except if the debtor said, 'I fear that if the creditor comes, he will deny you regarding it,' and he guaranteed him; or if the claimant of agency said, 'Take [money] from me on condition that I acquit you of the debt,' just as when the father says to the son-in-law upon taking the dowry of his daughter, 'I take from you on condition that I acquit you of the dower of my daughter,' then if the daughter takes the dowry from the son-in-law, the son-in-law has recourse against the father. So there are two causes for recourse in case of loss. Then know that it is valid to prove agency by evidence along with the debtor's acknowledgment thereof, and there are analogies for this which we have written in the benefits: that evidence is not established except against a denier, except in cases we mentioned in the chapter of acknowledgment. The agent. His statement: 'or he did not confirm him regarding the agency and paid him upon his claim' is coordinated with 'he guaranteed him' – i.e., if he did not confirm him, then he has recourse against him because he only paid him hoping for ratification, so when his hope is cut off, he has recourse. He made it absolute, so it includes if he was silent, because the principle is non-confirmation. As for if he denied him, recourse is more apparent. In all these aspects, he does not have the right to recover [the money] until the absent one comes, because what was paid has become a right of the absent one either apparently or potentially, so it is like the case where he paid to an unauthorized agent hoping for ratification: he does not have the right to recover because of the possibility of ratification; thus in al-Hidaya. And in Jami' al-Fusulain, two opinions are mentioned regarding recovery from the unauthorized agent. According to the [second] opinion, if he paid to a man to deliver it to the owner of the debt, then he can recover because he is an agent of the debtor, and because one who undertakes an act for a purpose has no right to revoke it until he despairs of his purpose. And so on. He attributed it to al-Dhakhira. This is a good question, but the answer is not satisfactory according to Abu Hanifa, because if he were a messenger, he would not have the right to litigate. His statement: 'what he took' i.e., what the creditor took from the debtor, not what the agent took, as will be noted. His statement: 'and because one who undertakes an act for a purpose' is coordinated with his statement 'because he only paid him...' so it is rejected. And he paid him upon his claim. And if he said, 'I am his agent to receive a deposit,' and the depositary confirmed him, he is not ordered to pay to him. Similarly, if he claimed to have purchased it and he confirmed him. And if he claimed that the depositary died and left it as inheritance for him and he confirmed him... And he took from me. If the debtor produces evidence that he is not an agent, or of his acknowledgment to that effect, it is not accepted, and he has no right of recovery. And if he wants to make him swear an oath about that, he is not made to swear, because all that is based on a valid claim which does not exist, since he is seeking to undo what the absent one has established. And if the debtor produces evidence that the claimant denied the agency regarding the money, it is accepted. And if the debtor claims against the claimant at the time he wants recourse against him that he authorized the recipient, and he proves it, it is accepted and he is acquitted; and if he denies, he administers an oath to him; if he refuses, he is acquitted. In al-Bazzaziyya: he acknowledged the debt and denied the agency, and the claimant of agency requested his oath that he does not know if he is an agent – the Imam [Abu Hanifa] does not administer the oath to him, but his companion [Abu Yusuf] administers it. And in it: if the debtor wants to make him swear by God that he did not authorize him, and if he paid in silence, he does not have the right to make the creditor swear unless he returns to confirmation; and if he paid after denial, he does not have the right to make him swear even if he returns to confirmation, but he has recourse against the agent, and the agent may make the debtor swear in cases of denial and silence by God that he does not know that the creditor authorized him. If he swears, the matter is settled; if he refuses, he does not have recourse against the debtor. His statement: 'If he said, I am his agent to receive a deposit, and the depositary confirmed him, he is not ordered to pay to him' because it is an acknowledgment of another's property, unlike debt. So if he does not confirm him, he is not ordered a fortiori. In Kafi al-Hakim: if a man took a deposit of a man, and the owner of the deposit said, 'I did not authorize him,' and he swore to that, and the depositary guaranteed him, he has recourse against the recipient if it is still in existence; and if he said it is lost, or he said 'I paid it to the one who authorized me,' and the depositary confirmed him regarding the agency, he has no recourse against him for anything. If he denied him regarding the agency, or did not confirm him nor deny him, or confirmed him and guaranteed him the property, he has the right to guarantee him. And if he wants to recover it after he paid it to him, he does not have that right, because he is seeking to undo a completed matter from his side. And if the deposit perishes with him after he prevented [payment], it is said he is not liable, and it should be because he prevented it from the agent of the depositor in his claim. And if the agent proves that he is the agent to receive it, and the trustee claims to have paid it to the principal or to the agent, his statement is accepted regarding the discharge of himself; thus in Kafi al-Hakim. And in al-Qunyah: they differed regarding the finder if he acknowledges the lost item to a man: is he ordered to pay to him? His statement: 'Similarly, if he claimed to have purchased it and he confirmed him,' i.e., purchasing the deposit from its owner, and the depositary confirmed him, he is not ordered to pay to him because as long as... the guarantee? The claim of al-Ramli here. And in the margin of al-Minh, it is a mistake. And he said in the margin of this book: the correct version is 'and it is said no' because one who undertakes an act for a purpose... His statement: 'In Kafi al-Hakim, and if a man took a deposit of a man, etc.' He said in Jami' al-Fusulain: if its owner comes and denies his agency, the depositary does not have recourse against the agent if he confirmed him and did not stipulate a guarantee; otherwise, he has recourse against his property if it is still in existence, and its value if it is lost. I say: if he confirmed him and paid without stipulation, it should be that he has recourse against the agent if it is still in existence, because his purpose has not been achieved, so he may revoke it by analogy to what passed in al-Hidaya that the debtor, by what he paid to an agent he confirmed, [has recourse] if it remains. So this, and Allah knows best. I say: what he researched is derived from the statement of al-Kafi, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a portion of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the chapter on agency in litigation and receipt. The author, Zayn al-Din ibn Nujaym, explains the ruling when a debtor pays money to a person who claims to be the creditor's agent for collection. The main ruling: If the debtor pays the claimed agent, he can recover the money from the agent only if the money is still in the agent's possession (qāʾiman). If it is lost (ḍāʿa), the debtor cannot recover it unless the agent accepted liability (ḍamān) at the time of receipt. The author then clarifies the meaning of 'ḍammanahu' in two possible readings: with tashdīd (making the debtor guarantee the agent) or with takhfīf (making the agent guarantee the money). The correct interpretation is that the agent guarantees the money he receives a second time (if the principal denies the agency). The commentary also discusses the case where the debtor pays without confirming the agency, i.e., he pays on the claimant's mere assertion. In that case, the debtor can recover the money if still extant, because he paid hoping for ratification; if ratification fails, he may take back his property. However, he cannot recover it before the principal returns, because the payment may have become the principal's right either apparently or potentially. This is analogous to payment to an unauthorized agent (fuḍūlī). The text then moves to analogous cases involving deposits (wadīʿa): if someone claims to be the owner's agent to receive a deposit, and the depositary confirms, he is not ordered to pay. This is because it is an acknowledgment of another's property (iqrār bi-māl al-ghayr), which is not binding. The passage also covers scenarios of evidence, oaths, and the effect of the depositary's guarantee. The overall theme is the protection of the debtor/depositary from loss when dealing with a claimant whose agency is unconfirmed.

Key Terms
الوكالة (al-wakāla): A contract whereby one person appoints another to act on his behalf in a permissible matter.
الغريم (al-gharīm): The debtor; the person who owes money to the principal.
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Liability; guarantee to compensate for loss. Here, the agent's acceptance of liability for the money received.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When can a debtor who paid a claimed agent recover the money from the agent?
A) Always, if the debtor did not confirm the agency.
B) Only if the money is still in the agent's possession and the debtor did not confirm the agency or guarantee the liability.
C) Only if the money is lost and the agent accepted liability at payment.
D) Never, because payment is final.

Key Takeaway
The debtor's right to recover from a claimed agent hinges on whether the payment was made with a guarantee or with hope of ratification, and whether the money remains extant.

intermediate
8 min
Depositary Not Compelled by Depositor's Confirmation of Agency or Purchase
Listing scenarios where a depositor is not ordered to hand over the deposit to a claimant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a depositary is not ordered to hand over the deposit merely because the depositor confirms the claimant's agency, and explain the legal reasoning based on iqrar bi mal al-ghayr.

Core Idea
A depositor's confirmation of a claim does not compel the depositary to deliver; it is acknowledgment of another's property.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a common scenario in deposit law: someone claims to be the depositor's agent to receive the deposit, and the depositor confirms that claim. Intuitively, one might think the depositary must now hand it over. However, Hanafi fiqh rules that the depositary is not ordered to do so. Why? Because the confirmation is merely an acknowledgment (iqrar) of the depositor's own property, not proof of the claimant's right. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Locate the key sentence: 'If he says: I am his agent... and the depositor confirms him, he is not ordered to hand over to him.' This is the primary rule.
Similarly for a claim of purchase.
2
Understand the legal basis
The confirmation is iqrar bi mal al-ghayr (acknowledgment of another's property). It binds only the one who makes it (the depositor), not the depositary. It does not create a right for the claimant.
Contrast with iqrar for debt, where the debtor may be ordered to pay.
3
Contrast with debt analogy
The passage explicitly contrasts with debt (dīn). In debt, a confession by the debtor can obligate payment. Here, the depositor's confirmation is not an admission of a debt owed to the claimant, but an acknowledgment of his own property.
Therefore, the depositary is not compelled.
4
Note the expansions
The passage also covers scenarios: if the depositor denies, the depositary is even more clearly not ordered (a fortiori). It also discusses oaths and liability in subsequent lines.
These are secondary but important for comprehensive understanding.

بالدفع إليه) لأنه إقرار بمال الغير بخلاف الدين فإذا لم يصدقه لا يؤمر بالأولى. وفي كافي الحاكم وإذا قبض رجل وديعة رجل فقال رب الوديعة ما وكلته وحلف على ذلك وضمن المستودع رجع على القابض إن كان بعينه، وإن قال قد هلك أو قال دفعته إلى الذي وكلني وقد صدقه المستودع بالوكالة لم يرجع عليه بشيء، فإن كان كذبه بالوكالة أو لم يصدقه ولم يكذبه أو صدقه وضمنه المال كان له أن يضمنه ا هـ . ولو أراد استردادها بعد Book of Agency, Chapter of Agency for Litigation and Taking Possession. And he hands it over upon his claim. If he says: I am his agent to receive the deposit, and the depositor confirms him, he is not ordered to hand over to him. Similarly, if he claims purchase and he confirms him. If the debtor presents evidence that he is not an agent or that he (the claimant) confessed to that, it is not accepted and he has no right of recovery. If he wants to swear him on that, he is not sworn because all that is based on a valid claim and it does not exist as he is seeking to annul what the absent one established. If the debtor presents evidence that the claimant denied the agency of the property, it is accepted. If the debtor claims against the claimant when he wants to recourse on him that he appointed the recipient as agent and proves it, it is accepted and he becomes free. If he denies, he swears him; if he refuses, he is free. In al-Bazzaziyya: He confessed to the debt and denied the agency, and the claimant of agency requested him to swear on his lack of knowledge that he was an agent. Imam Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) does not swear him, and his companions swear him. (End) In it: If the debtor wants to swear him by Allah that he did not appoint him for that, and if he (the debtor) paid due to silence, he has no right to swear the creditor unless he returns to affirming. And if he paid due to denial, he has no right to swear him even if he returns to affirming, but he has recourse against the agent. The agent may swear the debtor in denial and silence by Allah that he does not know that the creditor appointed him. If he swears, the matter is settled; if he refuses, he does not have recourse against the debtor. (End) His statement: (If he says: I am his agent to receive the deposit, and the depositor confirms him, he is not ordered to hand over to him) because it is an acknowledgment of another's property, unlike debt. If he does not confirm him, he is not ordered a fortiori. In Kafi al-Hakim: If a man takes possession of another man's deposit, and the owner of the deposit says: I did not appoint him as agent, and he swears to that, and the depositary is liable, he has recourse against the taker if it is still in existence. If he says: it perished, or says: I handed it over to the one who appointed me, and the depositary confirmed the agency, he does not have recourse against him for anything. If he denied the agency, or did not confirm him nor denied, or confirmed him and made him liable for the property, he may make him liable. (End) If he wants to reclaim it after handing it over to him, he does not own that because he is seeking to annul a completed matter from his side. And in the marginalia of this book: the correct version is: and it is said no, because the one who undertakes the transaction for a purpose etc. His statement: (In Kafi al-Hakim: If a man takes possession of another man's deposit...) He said in Jami' al-Fusulayn: If its owner appears and denies the agency, the depositary does not have recourse against the agent if he confirmed him and did not stipulate liability on him; otherwise, he has recourse in kind if it exists, and its value if perished. I say: If he confirmed him and handed over without a condition, it is befitting that he should have recourse against the agent if it exists, because his purpose was not achieved, so he may revoke it by analogy to what is mentioned in al-Hidayah that the debtor who handed over to an agent he confirmed (can take back) if it remains. As such, and Allah knows best. (End) I said: What he researched is derived from the words of al-Kafi, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_statement from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, specifically the chapter on agency for litigation and taking possession. The author, Ibn Nujaym, explains two key scenarios. First, if a person claims to be the agent of the depositor to receive a deposit, and the depositor confirms this claim, the person currently holding the deposit (the depositary) is not ordered to hand it over to the claimant. The reason is that the claimant's confirmation is essentially an acknowledgment of another's property (the depositor's), and it does not create a right for the claimant to receive it. The ruling is analogous for a claim of purchase: if someone claims to have bought the deposit from its owner and the depositor confirms, the depositary is still not compelled to deliver. The passage then discusses various sub-rulings, including cases where the debtor (creditor of the absent depositor) denies the agency, the right to swear oaths, and the liability of the depositary. It also references other works like al-Bazzaziyya, Kafi al-Hakim, and Jami' al-Fusulayn. The overall principle is that mere acknowledgment by the depositor of a claim does not obligate the depositary to hand over the deposit; there must be a stronger basis, such as established agency or inheritance.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakīl): A person appointed by another (the principal) to act on their behalf in a specific matter, here to receive a deposit.
وديعة (wadīʿah): A deposit: property entrusted to someone for safekeeping without compensation.
إقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgment or confession, here the depositor's admission that the claimant is his agent or has bought the deposit.
غريم (gharīm): Creditor or debtor, here refers to the person against whom the claim is made, possibly the debtor of the absent principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone claims to be the agent of the depositor to receive a deposit, and the depositor confirms this claim, is the depositary ordered to hand over the deposit?
A) Yes, because the depositor has acknowledged the agency.
B) No, because the acknowledgment is an acknowledgment of another's property and does not compel delivery.
C) Yes, but only if the claimant swears an oath.
D) No, unless the claimant also produces a written contract.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a depositor's mere confirmation of a claimant's agency or purchase does not obligate the depositary to hand over the deposit; the confirmation is an acknowledgment of the depositor's own property, not proof of the claimant's right.
